The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Accidental vs. Necessary Conditionals in Syllogisms
Author refutes the use of accidental conditionals in syllogisms through an example and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya) conditionals and explain why only necessary conditionals are valid in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals are not real connections; valid inference requires necessity.

In this passage, the author refines a key point about conditional syllogisms. Not all true conditionals are usable in inference. You will learn why only necessary conditionals (luzumiyya) are valid, and why accidental ones (ittifaqiyya) lead to circularity or contradiction. This distinction is crucial for rigorous reasoning in Islamic sciences, where establishing a real connection between cause and effect is essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Notice the author distinguishes between accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya) conditionals. An accidental conditional is one where the consequent happens to coincide with the antecedent, but there is no real entailment. A necessary conditional has a genuine logical or causal connection.
In Arabic, 'ittifaq' means coincidence.
2
Understand the requirement for syllogistic use
The author states that for a conditional to be used in a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), it must be necessary. If it is accidental, knowing the antecedent does not give knowledge of the consequent, and vice versa.
This is because the inference depends on the connection being real.
3
Analyze the example given
The author gives the example: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying.' This is accidental because there is no causal link. The antecedent and consequent are unrelated.
The example shows that the truth of the conditional does not imply a real connection.
4
Trace the reductio argument
The author shows that if the accidental conditional were used, it would lead to a contradiction: from 'man is rational' and the conditional, we cannot infer 'donkey is braying'. If we try to use contraposition, we get another accidental conditional leading to 'donkey braying and not braying'.
This reductio (khalf) proves that accidental conditionals cannot be used in inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال إن المتّصلة السالبة إذا كانت مستلزمة للموجبة كانت من [۲۲۸] جملة ما يصح استعماله في الأقيسة الاستثنائية؛ إذ المنتج حينئذ هو المتصلة الموجبة لا السالبة والموجبة التي هي من اللوازم يجب أن تكون لزومية فإنها إذا كانت اتفاقية لا يكون العلم بعين التالي مستفادًا من العلم بوضع المقدم بل شيئًا إنما جعل هو أصلا للمقدم بسبب أمر عرف وجوده في نفسه مع وجود المقدم. ولو كان كذلك لما كان العلم بصدق تلك المتصلة مستفادًا من العلم بوجوده، ولا العلم بوجوده من العلم بتلك المتَّصلة أيضًا لاستحالة الدور. ثمَّ إنَّا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، لكن الإنسان ناطق فلا يلزم منه كون الحمار ناهقا وإلا يلزم الدور لما مر . وكذلك إذا قلنا الحمار ليس بناهق فلا يلزم منه إن الإنسان ليس بناطق وإلا لكان إنتاجه إياه بواسطة قولنا كلما لم يكن الحمار ناهقا لم يكن الإنسان ناطقا لكن هذه المتّصلة ليست لزومية، وهذا ظاهر، ولا اتفاقية فإن الاتفاقية هي التي يكون وجود التالي فيها مطابقاً لوجود المقدم. وإنه لا يكون مطابقا فيها لأنه ليس لشيء من جزأيها وجود في نفسه، وما لا وجود له في نفسه استحال أن يطابق وجوده وجود غيره. ولا يقال صدق هذه المتّصلة من لوازم صدق تلك المتصلة؛ وتلك صادقة فكذلك هذه بالضرورة. ولو كانت صادقة؛ والصادقة لا تخلو من أن تكون لزومية أو اتفاقية. وإنما قلنا إنه من اللوازم؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا وجب أن يصدق قولنا كلَّما لم يكن الحمار ناهقا لم يكن الإنسان ناطقا وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان الإنسان ناطقا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان الحمار ناهقا؛ هذا خلف.
And it is not said that if the negative conditional entails the affirmative, it belongs to [228] the set of what is validly used in exceptional syllogisms; for the product then is the affirmative conditional, not the negative. And the affirmative that is among the entailments must be necessary, for if it is accidental, knowledge of the very consequent would not be derived from knowledge of positing the antecedent; rather, it is something that is made a ground for the antecedent due to a matter known to exist in itself with the existence of the antecedent. And if it were so, knowledge of the truth of that conditional would not be derived from knowledge of its existence, nor would knowledge of its existence from knowledge of that conditional, due to the impossibility of circularity. Then, if we say: 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying,' but man is rational, it does not follow that the donkey is braying; otherwise, circularity would follow as before. Likewise, if we say: 'The donkey is not braying,' it does not follow that man is not rational; otherwise, its production would be by means of our saying: 'Whenever the donkey is not braying, man is not rational,' but this conditional is not necessary, and this is clear, nor is it accidental, for the accidental is that in which the existence of the consequent is coincident with the existence of the antecedent. And it is not coincident in it because neither of its parts has existence in itself, and what has no existence in itself cannot have its existence coincide with the existence of another. And it is not said that the truth of this conditional is from the entailments of the truth of that conditional; and that one is true, so this one is necessarily likewise. And if it were true, the true is not free from being necessary or accidental. And we only said it is from the entailments, because if our saying 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' is true, then our saying 'Whenever the donkey is not braying, man is not rational' must be true; otherwise, its opposite would be true, namely our saying 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, man is rational,' and 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' would produce 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, the donkey is braying'; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage refines the conditions for using conditional propositions in syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). The author addresses a potential objection: could a negative conditional be used if it implies an affirmative one? He responds that the product then is the affirmative, which must be necessary (luzumiyya). If it is accidental (ittifaqiyya), the syllogism fails because the connection is not real—the consequent does not genuinely follow from the antecedent, but only coincidentally. For example, the conditional 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' is accidental—there is no real causal link. Thus, positing the antecedent does not give knowledge of the consequent, and vice versa, leading to circularity if one tried to use it. The author then demonstrates with a reductio: if such a conditional were true, then by contraposition it would imply another conditional, which would lead to a contradiction (the donkey both braying and not braying). The passage emphasizes that only necessary conditionals—where the connection is real—are valid for inference. In Islamic legal reasoning, this is analogous to the requirement that the 'illa (ratio legis) must be properly connected to the hukm (ruling); an accidental correlation cannot ground a valid analogy.

Key Terms

اللزومية
القيسة الاستثنائية
الدور
الخلف

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the use of the conditional 'Whenever man is rational, the donkey is braying' in a syllogism?
A) It is negative, not affirmative.
B) The antecedent is false.
C) It is accidental (ittifaqiyya) with no real connection.
D) It is not a conditional proposition.

Key Takeaway
Only necessary conditionals (luzumiyya) are valid for inference in conditional syllogisms; accidental ones (ittifaqiyya) lead to circularity or contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism using a necessary conditional from fiqh: 'If a substance is intoxicating, it is forbidden (haram). Date wine (nabidh) is intoxicating. Therefore, date wine is forbidden.' Explain why this conditional is necessary.
?
Identify why the following conditional is accidental and cannot be used in a syllogism: 'If someone is in Makkah, then it is raining in Makkah.'

The Necessity of Implicative Conditionals
Author argues for lizumiyya conditionals in hypothetical syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lizumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (accidental) conditionals and explain why only lizumiyya conditionals can yield certain knowledge in a hypothetical syllogism.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals do not transmit knowledge; they lead to circularity.

In this passage, the author tackles a common objection about using negative conditionals. He argues that the positive conditional that emerges must be implicative (lizumiyya); otherwise, knowledge of the consequent would not be derived from the antecedent, causing a vicious circle. This distinction is essential for ensuring that hypothetical syllogisms produce real knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Look for the terms 'lizumiyya' (implicative) and 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental) in the Arabic passage. These distinguish conditionals where the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent versus where it merely coincides.
The author uses the example 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' to illustrate a non-lizumiyya conditional.
2
Understand the objection
Someone might claim that a negative conditional that entails a positive could be used in a hypothetical syllogism. The author rejects this, stating that only the positive conditional that is entailed and is lizumiyya can produce the conclusion.
3
Analyze the circularity argument
If the conditional were accidental, knowledge of the consequent would not come from the antecedent but from an external factor, leading to a vicious circle (dawr). The passage states: 'ولو كان كذلك لما كان العلم بصدق تلك المتصلة مستفادًا من العلم بوجوده، ولا العلم بوجوده من العلم بتلك المتَّصلة أيضًا لاستحالة الدور'.
This means that neither the truth of the conditional nor existence can be known from each other, making the inference useless.
4
Apply the test
Given a conditional, check if the truth of the antecedent necessarily gives knowledge of the consequent without circularity. If not, it is ittifaqiyya and cannot be used productively in a syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك. ولا يقال إن المتّصلة السالبة إذا كانت مستلزمة للموجبة كانت من جملة ما يصح استعماله في الأقيسة الاستثنائية؛ إذ المنتج حينئذ هو المتصلة الموجبة لا السالبة والموجبة التي هي من اللوازم يجب أن تكون لزومية فإنها إذا كانت اتفاقية لا يكون العلم بعين التالي مستفادًا من العلم بوضع المقدم بل شيئًا إنما جعل هو أصلا للمقدم بسبب أمر عرف وجوده في نفسه مع وجود المقدم. ولو كان كذلك لما كان العلم بصدق تلك المتصلة مستفادًا من العلم بوجوده، ولا العلم بوجوده من العلم بتلك المتَّصلة أيضًا لاستحالة الدور. ثمَّ إنَّا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، لكن الإنسان ناطق فلا يلزم منه كون الحمار ناهقا وإلا يلزم الدور لما مر . وكذلك إذا قلنا الحمار ليس بناهق فلا يلزم منه إن الإنسان ليس بناطق وإلا لكان إنتاجه إياه بواسطة قولنا كلما لم يكن الحمار ناهقا لم يكن الإنسان ناطقا لكن هذه المتّصلة ليست لزومية، وهذا ظاهر، ولا اتفاقية فإن الاتفاقية هي التي يكون وجود التالي فيها مطابقاً لوجود المقدم. وإنه لا يكون مطابقا فيها لأنه ليس لشيء من جزأيها وجود في نفسه، وما لا وجود له في نفسه استحال أن يطابق وجوده وجود غيره. ولا يقال صدق هذه المتّصلة من لوازم صدق تلك المتصلة؛ وتلك صادقة فكذلك هذه بالضرورة. ولو كانت صادقة؛ والصادقة لا تخلو من أن تكون لزومية أو اتفاقية. وإنما قلنا إنه من اللوازم؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا وجب أن يصدق قولنا كلَّما لم يكن الحمار ناهقا لم يكن الإنسان ناطقا وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان الإنسان ناطقا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان الحمار ناهقا؛ هذا خلف.
ثم إنهم أجابوا عنه بوجهين: أحدهما أن يكون كونه خلفا في حيز المنع فإن معناه قد يكون إذا كان الحمار ناهقاً في الفرض كان الحمار ناهقاً في الوجود؛ وذلك لأن قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا قول لا تعلق له بالوجود أصلا بل إلى الفرض. وقولنا كان الحمار ناهقًا من جملة ما له تعلق بالوجود لما أنه مأخوذ من موافقة الوجود كما في الكبرى من القياس المذكور. وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا القول وهو النتيجة قولا حقًا فلا يمكن أن يقال إنه خلف. ألا ترى أنك إذا فرضت هذا الكذب وهو قولك لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان في موافقة الوجود كل حمار ناهق لأن قولنا كل حمار ناهق لما كان صادقا في نفسه فأي حال فرضتها يكون هذا صادقا معه اتفاقا، ولا يكون لازما عن ذلك الفرض. فإذن ليس قولك كل حمار ناهق لازما عن قولك لم يكن الحمار ناهقا. نعم إذا كان كل واحد منهما بحسب الوجود كان ذلك محالا . وثانيهما أنه إذا لزم من موافقة ما يكون صادقا في الوجود لما يكون صادقا في الوجود موافقته إياه في الكذب حتى إذا كذب أحدهما كذب الآخر لا يمكننا أن نتعرض لقياس الخلف؛ وذلك لأنا نأخذ في الخلف نقيض قول موافق في الصدق لمقدمتي القياس، ونضيف إلى إحدى المقدمتين الصادقتين حتى يلزم المحال فيقال لا يسلم أن صدق إحدى المقدمتين يكون باقيًا على تقدير صدق النقيض يمكن أن لا يصدق على ذلك التقدير. وكيف وكلامنا على تقدير أن تكون الموافقة في الصدق موجبًا للموافقة في الكذب بل إذا كان الأمر كذلك كان أي حق رفع لزم رفع أي حق يتفق ؛ فظهر بما ذكرناه أن المتصلة الموجبة فيما نحن فيه يجب أن تكون لزومية. والعلم بالمطلوب مجهولا حتى إذا انضم إلى العلم بهذه المتّصلة العلم بوضع المقدم أو العلم بنقيض التالي لزم من الأول العلم بعين التالي ومن الثاني العلم بنقيض المقدم علمًا مستفادًا من القياس
Similarly. And it is not said that the negative conditional, if it entails the positive, would be among what can be used in hypothetical syllogisms; for what produces the conclusion then is the positive conditional, not the negative, and the positive that is among the entailments must be implicative. For if it were accidental, knowledge of the very consequent would not be obtained from knowledge of positing the antecedent, but rather something that was made a basis for the antecedent due to something known to exist in itself along with the existence of the antecedent. And if that were so, knowledge of the truth of that conditional would not be obtained from knowledge of its existence, nor knowledge of its existence from knowledge of that conditional, due to the impossibility of circularity. Then if we say 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying, and man is speaking,' it does not follow that the donkey is braying; otherwise circularity would follow for what has passed. And similarly, if we say 'The donkey is not braying,' it does not follow that man is not speaking; otherwise its production would be by means of our saying 'Whenever the donkey is not braying, man is not speaking,' but this conditional is not implicative, and this is obvious, nor is it accidental, for the accidental is that in which the existence of the consequent corresponds to the existence of the antecedent. And it is not corresponding in it because neither of its two parts has existence in itself, and what has no existence in itself cannot have its existence correspond to the existence of another. And it is not said that the truth of this conditional is among the entailments of the truth of that conditional; and that is true, so this is likewise necessarily. And if it were true, and the true is not devoid of being either implicative or accidental. And we only said it is among the entailments; that is because if our statement 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' is true, then our statement 'Whenever the donkey is not braying, man is not speaking' must be true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, man is speaking,' and 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' yields 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, the donkey is braying'; this is a reductio.
Then they answered it in two ways: First, that its being a reductio is subject to denial, for its meaning is 'It may be that if the donkey is braying in the supposition, the donkey is braying in existence'; that is because our statement 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying' is a statement that has no relation to existence at all but rather to supposition. And our statement 'The donkey is braying' is among those that have relation to existence because it is taken from conformity to existence as in the major premise of the mentioned syllogism. And if that is so, then this statement, which is the conclusion, is a true statement, so it cannot be said that it is a reductio. Do you not see that if you suppose this falsehood, namely your statement 'The donkey is not braying,' it is in conformity to existence that every donkey is braying, because our statement 'Every donkey is braying' since it is true in itself, in whatever state you suppose it, this would be true with it accidentally, and it is not entailed by that supposition. Therefore, your statement 'Every donkey is braying' is not entailed by your statement 'The donkey is not braying.' Yes, if each of them were with respect to existence, that would be impossible. Second, that if it follows from the conformity of what is true in existence to what is true in existence that it conforms to it in falsehood such that if one is false the other is false, we cannot engage in reductio syllogism; that is because in reductio we take the contradictory of a statement that conforms in truth to the two premises of the syllogism, and we add it to one of the two true premises until an impossibility follows, and it is said that it is not granted that the truth of one of the two premises remains upon the supposition of the truth of the contradictory, such that it may not be true under that supposition. How so, and our discussion is on the supposition that conformity in truth necessitates conformity in falsehood? Nay, if the matter were so, then any truth that is removed necessitates the removal of any truth that coincides. So it is clear from what we have mentioned that the positive conditional in what we are discussing must be implicative. And knowledge of the sought conclusion is unknown until when joined to knowledge of this conditional, knowledge of positing the antecedent or knowledge of the contradictory of the consequent, there follows from the first knowledge of the very consequent and from the second knowledge of the contradictory of the antecedent, knowledge obtained from the syllogism.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the nature of conditional propositions (mutasila) in the context of hypothetical syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), specifically addressing an objection about the use of negative conditionals and the necessity that the positive conditional be implicative (lizumiyya) rather than accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author begins by rejecting a potential claim: that a negative conditional that entails a positive could be used in a hypothetical syllogism. He argues that what actually produces the conclusion is the positive conditional, and that this positive conditional must be lizumiyya. If it were ittifaqiyya, knowledge of the consequent would not be derived from knowledge of the antecedent; instead, it would be based on an extraneous factor. This would lead to a vicious circle (dawr). The author provides an example: 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying.' This conditional is not lizumiyya because there is no real entailment between the two events. If we affirm the antecedent (man is speaking), we cannot validly conclude the consequent (donkey braying) because the conditional is false in terms of actual implication. Similarly, if we deny the consequent (donkey is not braying), we cannot validly conclude the denial of the antecedent (man is not speaking) without using the contrapositive, which would be another non-lizumiyya conditional. The author then explains that an ittifaqiyya conditional is one where the existence of the consequent merely coincides with the existence of the antecedent, but since in such conditionals the parts often have no independent existence (as in counterfactuals), correspondence of existence is impossible. He refutes an attempted defense that the truth of one conditional entails the truth of another, showing that such a view would lead to a reductio ad absurdum: from 'Whenever man is speaking, donkey braying' and the false supposition 'It may be that if donkey is not braying, man is speaking', one could derive the absurd conclusion 'It may be that if donkey is not braying, donkey is braying'. The author then presents two responses from logicians to this reductio. The first response denies that the derived conclusion is truly a reductio, because the conclusion is actually a true statement when interpreted properly: the supposition 'donkey is not braying' is a mental assumption with no connection to actual existence, while the consequent 'donkey is braying' is true in existence (since all donkeys bray in reality). The truth of 'every donkey is braying' is accidental to the supposition, not entailed by it. The second response argues that if truth in existence always entailed truth in falsehood, then reductio reasoning would be undermined, because when we assume the contradictory of a true premise, that premise might not remain true under the assumption. The author concludes that the positive conditional in such contexts must be lizumiyya (implicative) to yield certain knowledge from the syllogism.

Key Terms
متّصلة
لزومية
اتفاقية
قياس استثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can a positive conditional that is not lizumiyya not produce certain knowledge in a hypothetical syllogism?
A) Because it would lead to a contradiction in terms.
B) Because knowledge of the consequent would not be derived from knowledge of the antecedent; instead it would be based on an extraneous factor, causing circularity.
C) Because the conditional would be false in itself.
D) Because the syllogism would require an additional premise.

Key Takeaway
Only implicative (lizumiyya) conditionals allow valid inferences in hypothetical syllogisms; accidental conditionals lead to circularity and do not yield certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'Whenever a person is fasting, they are not eating.' Is this lizumiyya or ittifaqiyya? Explain your reasoning based on the author's criteria.
?
In a hypothetical syllogism with the major premise 'If the prayer is valid, it includes recitation of Surah al-Fatiha' and the minor premise 'This prayer is valid,' what conclusion follows? Is this a lizumiyya conditional? Why?

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Reductio ad Absurdum and the Two Responses
Author defends lizumiyya conditionals by refuting a potential reductio argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a reductio argument from the passage and evaluate the two responses that defend against it, understanding when a reductio is valid.

Core Idea
A reductio only succeeds if the conditional involved is lizumiyya; accidental conditionals can lead to false absurdities.

After establishing that only lizumiyya conditionals are productive, the author addresses a potential reductio that might be used against his position. He then presents two responses from logicians that refute the reductio, clarifying the conditions under which reductio reasoning is valid.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the reductio
Assume the contradictory of the claim that the conditional 'Whenever man is speaking, donkey is braying' is not lizumiyya. Show that if it were true, from that and another supposition you derive an impossibility: 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, the donkey is braying.'
This is the reductio the author constructs to test the validity of using non-lizumiyya conditionals.
2
Understand the first response
The conclusion is not truly an impossibility because the supposition ('donkey is not braying') is a mental assumption with no relation to existence, while the consequent ('donkey is braying') is true in existence; thus, there is no logical contradiction.
The response argues that the absurdity is only apparent due to a category mismatch.
3
Understand the second response
If truth in existence always entailed truth in falsehood, then reductio would be undermined because the premises might not remain true under the assumption of the contradictory.
This response questions the underlying assumption of the reductio method.
4
Conclude
The reductio only works if the conditional is lizumiyya and the parts are considered in terms of existence; otherwise, the responses show the reductio fails.
Thus, the author's position that only lizumiyya conditionals are productive is reinforced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك. ولا يقال إن المتّصلة السالبة إذا كانت مستلزمة للموجبة كانت من جملة ما يصح استعماله في الأقيسة الاستثنائية؛ إذ المنتج حينئذ هو المتصلة الموجبة لا السالبة والموجبة التي هي من اللوازم يجب أن تكون لزومية فإنها إذا كانت اتفاقية لا يكون العلم بعين التالي مستفادًا من العلم بوضع المقدم بل شيئًا إنما جعل هو أصلا للمقدم بسبب أمر عرف وجوده في نفسه مع وجود المقدم. ولو كان كذلك لما كان العلم بصدق تلك المتصلة مستفادًا من العلم بوجوده، ولا العلم بوجوده من العلم بتلك المتَّصلة أيضًا لاستحالة الدور. ثمَّ إنَّا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا، لكن الإنسان ناطق فلا يلزم منه كون الحمار ناهقا وإلا يلزم الدور لما مر . وكذلك إذا قلنا الحمار ليس بناهق فلا يلزم منه إن الإنسان ليس بناطق وإلا لكان إنتاجه إياه بواسطة قولنا كلما لم يكن الحمار ناهقا لم يكن الإنسان ناطقا لكن هذه المتّصلة ليست لزومية، وهذا ظاهر، ولا اتفاقية فإن الاتفاقية هي التي يكون وجود التالي فيها مطابقاً لوجود المقدم. وإنه لا يكون مطابقا فيها لأنه ليس لشيء من جزأيها وجود في نفسه، وما لا وجود له في نفسه استحال أن يطابق وجوده وجود غيره. ولا يقال صدق هذه المتّصلة من لوازم صدق تلك المتصلة؛ وتلك صادقة فكذلك هذه بالضرورة. ولو كانت صادقة؛ والصادقة لا تخلو من أن تكون لزومية أو اتفاقية. وإنما قلنا إنه من اللوازم؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا كلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقا وجب أن يصدق قولنا كلَّما لم يكن الحمار ناهقا لم يكن الإنسان ناطقا وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان الإنسان ناطقا، وكلَّما كان الإنسان ناطقا كان الحمار ناهقًا ينتج قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان الحمار ناهقا؛ هذا خلف.
ثم إنهم أجابوا عنه بوجهين: أحدهما أن يكون كونه خلفا في حيز المنع فإن معناه قد يكون إذا كان الحمار ناهقاً في الفرض كان الحمار ناهقاً في الوجود؛ وذلك لأن قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا قول لا تعلق له بالوجود أصلا بل إلى الفرض. وقولنا كان الحمار ناهقًا من جملة ما له تعلق بالوجود لما أنه مأخوذ من موافقة الوجود كما في الكبرى من القياس المذكور. وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا القول وهو النتيجة قولا حقًا فلا يمكن أن يقال إنه خلف. ألا ترى أنك إذا فرضت هذا الكذب وهو قولك لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان في موافقة الوجود كل حمار ناهق لأن قولنا كل حمار ناهق لما كان صادقا في نفسه فأي حال فرضتها يكون هذا صادقا معه اتفاقا، ولا يكون لازما عن ذلك الفرض. فإذن ليس قولك كل حمار ناهق لازما عن قولك لم يكن الحمار ناهقا. نعم إذا كان كل واحد منهما بحسب الوجود كان ذلك محالا . وثانيهما أنه إذا لزم من موافقة ما يكون صادقا في الوجود لما يكون صادقا في الوجود موافقته إياه في الكذب حتى إذا كذب أحدهما كذب الآخر لا يمكننا أن نتعرض لقياس الخلف؛ وذلك لأنا نأخذ في الخلف نقيض قول موافق في الصدق لمقدمتي القياس، ونضيف إلى إحدى المقدمتين الصادقتين حتى يلزم المحال فيقال لا يسلم أن صدق إحدى المقدمتين يكون باقيًا على تقدير صدق النقيض يمكن أن لا يصدق على ذلك التقدير. وكيف وكلامنا على تقدير أن تكون الموافقة في الصدق موجبًا للموافقة في الكذب بل إذا كان الأمر كذلك كان أي حق رفع لزم رفع أي حق يتفق ؛ فظهر بما ذكرناه أن المتصلة الموجبة فيما نحن فيه يجب أن تكون لزومية. والعلم بالمطلوب مجهولا حتى إذا انضم إلى العلم بهذه المتّصلة العلم بوضع المقدم أو العلم بنقيض التالي لزم من الأول العلم بعين التالي ومن الثاني العلم بنقيض المقدم علمًا مستفادًا من القياس
Similarly. And it is not said that the negative conditional, if it entails the positive, would be among what can be used in hypothetical syllogisms; for what produces the conclusion then is the positive conditional, not the negative, and the positive that is among the entailments must be implicative. For if it were accidental, knowledge of the very consequent would not be obtained from knowledge of positing the antecedent, but rather something that was made a basis for the antecedent due to something known to exist in itself along with the existence of the antecedent. And if that were so, knowledge of the truth of that conditional would not be obtained from knowledge of its existence, nor knowledge of its existence from knowledge of that conditional, due to the impossibility of circularity. Then if we say 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying, and man is speaking,' it does not follow that the donkey is braying; otherwise circularity would follow for what has passed. And similarly, if we say 'The donkey is not braying,' it does not follow that man is not speaking; otherwise its production would be by means of our saying 'Whenever the donkey is not braying, man is not speaking,' but this conditional is not implicative, and this is obvious, nor is it accidental, for the accidental is that in which the existence of the consequent corresponds to the existence of the antecedent. And it is not corresponding in it because neither of its two parts has existence in itself, and what has no existence in itself cannot have its existence correspond to the existence of another. And it is not said that the truth of this conditional is among the entailments of the truth of that conditional; and that is true, so this is likewise necessarily. And if it were true, and the true is not devoid of being either implicative or accidental. And we only said it is among the entailments; that is because if our statement 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' is true, then our statement 'Whenever the donkey is not braying, man is not speaking' must be true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, man is speaking,' and 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying' yields 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, the donkey is braying'; this is a reductio.
Then they answered it in two ways: First, that its being a reductio is subject to denial, for its meaning is 'It may be that if the donkey is braying in the supposition, the donkey is braying in existence'; that is because our statement 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying' is a statement that has no relation to existence at all but rather to supposition. And our statement 'The donkey is braying' is among those that have relation to existence because it is taken from conformity to existence as in the major premise of the mentioned syllogism. And if that is so, then this statement, which is the conclusion, is a true statement, so it cannot be said that it is a reductio. Do you not see that if you suppose this falsehood, namely your statement 'The donkey is not braying,' it is in conformity to existence that every donkey is braying, because our statement 'Every donkey is braying' since it is true in itself, in whatever state you suppose it, this would be true with it accidentally, and it is not entailed by that supposition. Therefore, your statement 'Every donkey is braying' is not entailed by your statement 'The donkey is not braying.' Yes, if each of them were with respect to existence, that would be impossible. Second, that if it follows from the conformity of what is true in existence to what is true in existence that it conforms to it in falsehood such that if one is false the other is false, we cannot engage in reductio syllogism; that is because in reductio we take the contradictory of a statement that conforms in truth to the two premises of the syllogism, and we add it to one of the two true premises until an impossibility follows, and it is said that it is not granted that the truth of one of the two premises remains upon the supposition of the truth of the contradictory, such that it may not be true under that supposition. How so, and our discussion is on the supposition that conformity in truth necessitates conformity in falsehood? Nay, if the matter were so, then any truth that is removed necessitates the removal of any truth that coincides. So it is clear from what we have mentioned that the positive conditional in what we are discussing must be implicative. And knowledge of the sought conclusion is unknown until when joined to knowledge of this conditional, knowledge of positing the antecedent or knowledge of the contradictory of the consequent, there follows from the first knowledge of the very consequent and from the second knowledge of the contradictory of the antecedent, knowledge obtained from the syllogism.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the nature of conditional propositions (mutasila) in the context of hypothetical syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), specifically addressing an objection about the use of negative conditionals and the necessity that the positive conditional be implicative (lizumiyya) rather than accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author begins by rejecting a potential claim: that a negative conditional that entails a positive could be used in a hypothetical syllogism. He argues that what actually produces the conclusion is the positive conditional, and that this positive conditional must be lizumiyya. If it were ittifaqiyya, knowledge of the consequent would not be derived from knowledge of the antecedent; instead, it would be based on an extraneous factor. This would lead to a vicious circle (dawr). The author provides an example: 'Whenever man is speaking, the donkey is braying.' This conditional is not lizumiyya because there is no real entailment between the two events. If we affirm the antecedent (man is speaking), we cannot validly conclude the consequent (donkey braying) because the conditional is false in terms of actual implication. Similarly, if we deny the consequent (donkey is not braying), we cannot validly conclude the denial of the antecedent (man is not speaking) without using the contrapositive, which would be another non-lizumiyya conditional. The author then explains that an ittifaqiyya conditional is one where the existence of the consequent merely coincides with the existence of the antecedent, but since in such conditionals the parts often have no independent existence (as in counterfactuals), correspondence of existence is impossible. He refutes an attempted defense that the truth of one conditional entails the truth of another, showing that such a view would lead to a reductio ad absurdum: from 'Whenever man is speaking, donkey braying' and the false supposition 'It may be that if donkey is not braying, man is speaking', one could derive the absurd conclusion 'It may be that if donkey is not braying, donkey is braying'. The author then presents two responses from logicians to this reductio. The first response denies that the derived conclusion is truly a reductio, because the conclusion is actually a true statement when interpreted properly: the supposition 'donkey is not braying' is a mental assumption with no connection to actual existence, while the consequent 'donkey is braying' is true in existence (since all donkeys bray in reality). The truth of 'every donkey is braying' is accidental to the supposition, not entailed by it. The second response argues that if truth in existence always entailed truth in falsehood, then reductio reasoning would be undermined, because when we assume the contradictory of a true premise, that premise might not remain true under the assumption. The author concludes that the positive conditional in such contexts must be lizumiyya (implicative) to yield certain knowledge from the syllogism.

Key Terms
خلف
لزومية
اتفاقية
قياس استثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first response to the reductio, why is the conclusion 'It may be that if the donkey is not braying, the donkey is braying' not actually an absurdity?
A) Because the conditional 'Whenever man is speaking, donkey is braying' is false.
B) Because the supposition 'donkey is not braying' is a mental assumption with no relation to existence, while the consequent 'donkey is braying' is true in existence.
C) Because the author made a logical error.
D) Because the conclusion is itself a conditional and cannot be absurd.

Key Takeaway
Reductio arguments require that the conditionals involved be lizumiyya; otherwise, the absurdity may be only apparent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reconstruct a reductio argument from the passage using your own words. Then evaluate whether the two responses would apply to a different example, such as: 'If the world is eternal, then it has no beginning. The world does not have no beginning; therefore, it is not eternal.' Is this conditional lizumiyya? Could a similar response be made?
?
Take the conditional 'Whenever a person is asleep, they are not conscious.' Use this in a hypothetical syllogism and then try to construct a reductio against it. Is the conditional lizumiyya? Why or why not?

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Two Responses to the Khalaf Objection in Conditional Syllogisms
Author gives two responses to an objection about reductio in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a conditional syllogism requires an implicative (luzumiyya) conditional and identify the two responses to the reductio objection.

Core Idea
Reductio in conditional syllogisms fails if the conditional is accidental, not implicative.

In conditional syllogisms, a common objection is that assuming the opposite of the conclusion leads to a contradiction (khalaf). This passage provides two defenses: first, that the false supposition is purely hypothetical and does not affect the truth of the universal premise; second, that the very structure of reductio would collapse if truth correspondence forced falsehood correspondence. The key takeaway is that the conditional must be luzumiyya (implicative) for the syllogism to be valid. Let us walk through the logical steps to see how these responses work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Khalaf Objection
The objection claims that in a conditional syllogism, assuming the contradictory of the conclusion leads to an impossibility (khalaf). For example, if the major premise is 'Every donkey is braying' and the minor is 'This donkey is not braying', then the conclusion 'This donkey is braying' seems to contradict the minor. But the objector says this shows the syllogism is invalid.
The objection hinges on confusing suppositional truth with existential truth.
2
Analyze the First Response: Supposition vs. Existence
The author distinguishes between statements about supposition (e.g., 'if the donkey was not braying') and statements about existence (e.g., 'every donkey is braying'). The false supposition has no existential import, so the universal 'every donkey is braying' remains true independently. Thus the conclusion 'the donkey is braying' is actually true, not a contradiction.
The key insight: the truth of the universal does not depend on the hypothetical assumption.
3
Analyze the Second Response: Correspondence in Truth and Falsehood
The author argues that if we assume that a true statement in existence forces a corresponding truth in another statement (and similarly for falsehood), then the reductio method becomes problematic. Because when we assume the contradictory of a true premise, that premise might no longer be true on that assumption, so we cannot guarantee an impossibility.
This response shows that the reductio argument only works if the conditional is strictly implicative, not if it is accidental.
4
Extract the Necessary Condition: Luzumiyya
The passage concludes that for a conditional syllogism to be valid, the conditional must be luzumiyya (implicative), meaning the antecedent necessarily entails the consequent. Accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals do not support valid reductio arguments.
This condition is essential for sound reasoning in disciplines like Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم أجابوا عنه بوجهين: أحدهما أن يكون كونه خلفا في حيز المنع فإن معناه قد يكون إذا كان الحمار ناهقاً في الفرض كان الحمار ناهقاً في الوجود؛ وذلك لأن قولنا قد يكون إذا لم يكن الحمار ناهقا قول لا تعلق له بالوجود أصلا بل إلى الفرض. وقولنا كان الحمار ناهقًا من جملة ما له تعلق بالوجود لما أنه مأخوذ من موافقة الوجود كما في الكبرى من القياس المذكور. وإذا كان كذلك كان هذا القول وهو النتيجة قولا حقًا فلا يمكن أن يقال إنه خلف. ألا ترى أنك إذا فرضت هذا الكذب وهو قولك لم يكن الحمار ناهقا كان في موافقة الوجود كل حمار ناهق لأن قولنا كل حمار ناهق لما كان صادقا في نفسه فأي حال فرضتها يكون هذا صادقا معه اتفاقا، ولا يكون لازما عن ذلك الفرض. فإذن ليس قولك كل حمار ناهق لازما عن قولك لم يكن الحمار ناهقا. نعم إذا كان كل واحد منهما بحسب الوجود كان ذلك محالا . وثانيهما أنه إذا لزم من موافقة ما يكون صادقا في الوجود لما يكون صادقا في الوجود موافقته إياه في الكذب حتى إذا كذب أحدهما كذب الآخر لا يمكننا أن نتعرض لقياس الخلف؛ وذلك لأنا نأخذ في الخلف نقيض قول موافق في الصدق لمقدمتي القياس، ونضيف إلى إحدى المقدمتين الصادقتين حتى يلزم المحال فيقال لا يسلم أن صدق إحدى المقدمتين يكون باقيًا على تقدير صدق النقيض يمكن أن لا يصدق على ذلك التقدير. وكيف وكلامنا على تقدير أن تكون الموافقة في الصدق موجبًا للموافقة في الكذب بل إذا كان الأمر كذلك كان أي حق رفع لزم رفع أي حق يتفق ؛ فظهر بما ذكرناه أن المتصلة الموجبة فيما نحن فيه يجب أن تكون لزومية.
Then they answered it with two aspects: one is that its being a reductio is in the domain of prevention, for its meaning is that it may be that if the donkey was braying in the supposition, then the donkey was braying in existence. That is because our statement 'it may be if the donkey was not braying' is a statement that has no relation to existence at all, but only to supposition. And our statement 'the donkey was braying' is among that which has relation to existence, because it is taken from correspondence to existence, as in the major premise of the mentioned syllogism. And if that is the case, then this statement, which is the conclusion, is a true statement, so it cannot be said that it is a reductio. Do you not see that if you suppose this falsehood, namely your statement 'the donkey was not braying', it was in correspondence to existence that every donkey is braying, because our statement 'every donkey is braying' since it is true in itself, any state you suppose it, this will be true with it coincidentally, and does not follow necessarily from that supposition. So then your statement 'every donkey is braying' is not a necessary consequent of your statement 'the donkey was not braying'. Yes, if each of them is on the basis of existence, that would be impossible. And the second is that if it follows from the correspondence of what is true in existence to what is true in existence that it corresponds to it in falsehood, such that if one is false the other is false, we cannot engage in a syllogism of reductio; because we take in the reductio the contradictory of a statement that corresponds in truth to the two premises of the syllogism, and we add it to one of the two true premises until an impossibility follows, and then it is said: it is not granted that the truth of one of the two premises remains on the assumption of the truth of the contradictory, and it is possible that it may not be true on that assumption. How so? And our discourse is on the assumption that correspondence in truth necessitates correspondence in falsehood. Rather, if the matter is so, then any truth that is raised necessitates the raising of any truth that coincides. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that the affirmative conditional in our discussion must be implicative.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the validity of conditional syllogisms against an objection about reductio (khalaf). The author presents two responses. The first response distinguishes between a suppositional statement and a statement about existence. The supposed falsehood (e.g., 'The donkey was not braying') is purely hypothetical and has no existential import, while the true universal premise ('Every donkey is braying') is true in actuality. Therefore, the conclusion 'The donkey was braying' is actually true, not a reductio, because the false supposition does not affect the truth of the universal. The second response argues that if truth implies truth and falsehood implies falsehood for the statements involved, then the reductio method fails because on the assumption of the contradictory of a true premise, that premise might no longer be true. The author concludes that for a conditional syllogism to be valid, the conditional proposition must be luzumiyya (implicative), meaning the antecedent necessarily entails the consequent.

Key Terms
خلف
لزومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first response, why does the conclusion 'the donkey is braying' not create a reductio when the antecedent 'the donkey was not braying' is assumed?
A) Because the universal 'every donkey is braying' is false.
B) Because the false supposition has no existential import, so the true universal remains true.
C) Because the syllogism is in the first figure.
D) Because the conditional is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms require implicative (luzumiyya) conditionals; accidental conditionals do not support valid reductio arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Conditions for a Valid Conditional Syllogism
Author argues that conditional syllogisms require an implicative and universal conditional, with temporal unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two essential conditions (implicative and universal) required for a valid conditional syllogism and verify temporal unity.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is valid only when the conditional is necessary and universal.

Conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) are powerful tools for deriving knowledge, but they have strict prerequisites. In this passage, the author explains that the conditional premise must be implicative (luzumiyya) – not merely accidental – and universal (kulliyya), because a particular conditional can break the necessary connection between the antecedent and consequent across time. Understanding these conditions will help you judge the soundness of conditional arguments in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the shartiyya muttasila (conditional proposition) in the argument. Note its antecedent and consequent.
The author uses 'Whenever A-B, then C-D' as a schema.
2
Check for implicativity (luzum)
Determine whether the antecedent necessarily entails the consequent. If the connection is accidental (ittifaqiyya), the syllogism cannot yield knowledge.
An accidental conditional like 'If it is Friday, then it is raining' does not support inference.
3
Check for universality (kulliyya)
Verify that the conditional holds for all instances in the relevant time frame. A particular conditional (e.g., 'Sometimes if A then B') allows the possibility that the antecedent and consequent are true at different times.
If the conditional is particular, the exceptional premise might be true at a time when the conditional is not true, breaking the inference.
4
Verify temporal unity
Ensure that the time at which the conditional is true is the same as the time at which the exceptional premise (positing the antecedent or removing the consequent) holds. If times differ, the syllogism yields no conclusion.
The author stresses that 'it is necessary that the time be unified.'
5
Apply modus ponens or modus tollens
If the conditional passes the above checks, then positing the antecedent (modus ponens) validly yields the consequent, and removing the consequent (modus tollens) validly yields the negation of the antecedent.
The passage illustrates: knowledge of the antecedent gives knowledge of the consequent, and knowledge of the contradictory of the consequent gives knowledge of the contradictory of the antecedent.

والعلم بالمطلوب مجهولا حتى إذا انضم إلى العلم بهذه المتّصلة العلم بوضع المقدم أو العلم بنقيض التالي لزم من الأول العلم بعين التالي ومن الثاني العلم بنقيض المقدم علمًا مستفادًا من القياس كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» فإنه إذا حصل لنا العلم بوجود «أب» حصل لنا العلم بوجود (ج) د ؛ وإذا حصل لنا العلم بنقيض ج د حصل لنا العلم بنقيض (أب) علما مستفادًا من العلم بالمقدمتين. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إذا كانت المتّصلة اتفاقية. وكما أنه يجب أن هذه المتّصلة الموجبة لزومية فكذلك يجب أن تكون كلية ؛ فإنها إذا كانت جزئية فلا يلزم من العلم بوجود المقدم العلم بوجود التالي وإن كانت الجزئية صادقة لا مجال للشبهة فيها. ولا من العلم بنقيض التالي العلم بنقيض المقدم كذلك؛ فإن من الجائز أن يكون زمان صدق المتّصلة غير زمان صدق المقدم أو نقيض التالي. ولو كان كذلك فلا يبعد أن لا يتحد زمان صدق المتّصلة مع زمان صدق المقدمة الاستثنائية وهي وضع المقدم أو رفع التالي. والقياس الذي هذا شأنه لا ينتج شيئًا فإن من اللوازم أن يكون الزمان متحدا. ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه وضع المقدم كما لا يمكن أن يكون صدقه في غير زمان صدق المتّصلة إذا كانت المتّصلة كلية فكذلك إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية؛ فإنه إذا كان في غير ذلك الزمان فلا يكون ذلك الوضع وضع المقدم. والمقدم عبارة عما يمتنع وجوده دون وجود التالي، ولا يمتنع وجوده دون وجود التالي إلا في زمان كانت تلك المتصلة صادقة في ذلك الزمان. وكذلك رفع التالي فإن التالي لا يكون تاليا إلا في ذلك الزمان. ولا يستراب في أن الشيء المتصف بصفة كونه مقدما أو تاليا من حيث هو هو غير ذلك الشيء مع هذه الصفة يعني غير ما يكون لفظ المقدم أو التالي عبارة عنه؛ فوجود الإنسان مثلا من حيث هو وجود الإنسان لا يكون ملزوما لوجود الحيوان. وكيف وأنه من حيث هو هو لا يكون متصفّا بصفة ما أصلا. ولما لم يكن ملزوما فلا يكون وضعه وضع الملزوم؛ والكلام في وضع الملزوم. وعلى هذا في رفع
And knowledge of the desired unknown is such that when there is joined to the knowledge of this conditional the knowledge of positing the antecedent or the knowledge of the contradictory of the consequent, there follows from the first knowledge of the very consequent, and from the second knowledge of the contradictory of the antecedent, a knowledge derived from the syllogism, as in our statement: 'Whenever A-B, then C-D' — for if we obtain knowledge of the existence of A-B, we obtain knowledge of the existence of C-D; and if we obtain knowledge of the contradictory of C-D, we obtain knowledge of the contradictory of A-B, knowledge derived from the knowledge of the two premises. And it cannot be so if the conditional is accidental. And just as it is necessary that this affirmative conditional be implicative, so too must it be universal; for if it is particular, then from knowledge of the existence of the antecedent knowledge of the existence of the consequent does not follow, even if the particular is true without any room for doubt. Nor from knowledge of the contradictory of the consequent does knowledge of the contradictory of the antecedent follow likewise; for it is possible that the time of truth of the conditional be other than the time of truth of the antecedent or the contradictory of the consequent. And if it were so, then it is not unlikely that the time of truth of the conditional does not coincide with the time of truth of the exceptional premise, which is positing the antecedent or removing the consequent. And a syllogism of this property produces nothing, for it is necessary that the time be unified. Then an objector may say regarding the positing of the antecedent: just as its truth cannot be in other than the time of truth of the conditional if the conditional is universal, so too if the conditional is particular; for if it is in other than that time, then that positing is not the positing of the antecedent. And the antecedent is that whose existence is impossible without the existence of the consequent, and its existence is not impossible without the existence of the consequent except in a time in which that conditional was true in that time. And similarly the removal of the consequent, for the consequent is not a consequent except in that time. And there is no doubt that the thing qualified by the attribute of being antecedent or consequent in itself is other than that thing with this attribute, meaning other than what the term 'antecedent' or 'consequent' expresses. For the existence of man, for example, insofar as it is the existence of man, is not necessitating the existence of animal. How so? And insofar as it is itself, it is not qualified by any attribute at all. And since it is not necessitating, its positing is not the positing of the necessitator; and the discussion is about the positing of the necessitator. And likewise for the removal.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for a valid conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The author begins by describing how knowledge is derived: if we know a conditional and then come to know either the antecedent (musaddaq bihi) or the contradictory of the consequent, we gain knowledge of the consequent or contradictory of the antecedent respectively. This is illustrated with the example 'Whenever A-B, then C-D'. However, this only works if the conditional is luzumiyya (implicative), not ittifaqiyya (accidental) — i.e., the antecedent must necessarily entail the consequent. Furthermore, the conditional must also be kulliyya (universal), because a particular conditional leaves open the possibility that the truth of the conditional occurs at a different time than the truth of the antecedent or the removal of the consequent. If the times do not coincide, the syllogism yields no valid conclusion. The author then answers a potential objection: even with a particular conditional, the positing of the antecedent must be in the time when the conditional is true, otherwise it is not truly the antecedent. The antecedent is defined as that whose existence is impossible without the consequent, and this impossibility is tied to the time of the conditional's truth. A thing considered in itself (e.g., man's existence) does not entail another (e.g., animal) unless it is qualified as an antecedent. So the argument about the positing of the antecedent must refer to the antecedent as such, not the thing in isolation.

متصلة
لزومية
كلية

Checkpoint
Why must the conditional be universal (kulliyya) for a valid conditional syllogism?
A) Because a particular conditional cannot be true.
B) Because the time of truth of a particular conditional may differ from the time of the antecedent/consequent, breaking the inference.
C) Because universality ensures the conditional is implicative.
D) Because the syllogism only works with universal premises.

Key Takeaway
A valid conditional syllogism requires an implicative and universal conditional, and the truth times of the conditional and the exceptional premise must coincide.

The Syllogism

Conditions for a Valid Conditional Syllogism
Author states requirements for a conditional syllogism to produce knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional syllogism meets the conditions of necessity, universality, and temporal unity required for valid inference.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism yields knowledge only if its premise is necessary, universal, and temporally aligned.

We have learned that a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) allows us to derive a conclusion by affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent. However, not every conditional premise yields a valid inference. The author here lays down three crucial conditions: the conditional must be necessary (luzumiyya), universal (kulliyya), and the time of its truth must coincide with the time of the exceptional premise. Without these, the syllogism fails to produce knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the shartiyya muttasila (conditional statement) in the syllogism. It typically has the form: 'If A, then B' (in the passage: 'Whenever A-B, then C-D').
The conditional is the major premise.
2
Check for necessity (luzumiyya)
Determine if the connection between antecedent and consequent is necessary and not merely accidental (ittifaqiyya). A necessary conditional holds in all relevant circumstances; an accidental one may happen to be true but lacks entailment.
The author states that if the conditional is accidental, the syllogism cannot produce knowledge.
3
Check for universality (kulliyya)
Ensure the conditional is universal, covering all instances, not just some. A particular conditional (juz'iyya) may be true in some cases but not guarantee the inference in every case.
Even if a particular conditional is true without doubt, the inference from antecedent to consequent does not necessarily follow.
4
Verify temporal unity
Confirm that the time of the conditional's truth is the same as the time when the antecedent is affirmed or the consequent denied. If these times differ, the syllogism yields no conclusion.
The author warns that without temporal unity, the syllogism 'produces nothing'.
5
Determine syllogism validity
If all three conditions (necessity, universality, temporal unity) are met, the syllogism is valid and yields certain knowledge. Otherwise, it is invalid.
The passage emphasizes that these conditions are necessary for the syllogism to produce knowledge (istifada).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» فإنه إذا حصل لنا العلم بوجود «أب» حصل لنا العلم بوجود (ج) د ؛ وإذا حصل لنا العلم بنقيض ج د حصل لنا العلم بنقيض (أب) علما مستفادًا من العلم بالمقدمتين. ولا يمكن أن يكون كذلك إذا كانت المتّصلة اتفاقية. وكما أنه يجب أن هذه المتّصلة الموجبة لزومية فكذلك يجب أن تكون كلية ؛ فإنها إذا كانت جزئية فلا يلزم من العلم بوجود المقدم العلم بوجود التالي وإن كانت الجزئية صادقة لا مجال للشبهة فيها. ولا من العلم بنقيض التالي العلم بنقيض المقدم كذلك؛ فإن من الجائز أن يكون زمان صدق المتّصلة غير زمان صدق المقدم أو نقيض التالي. ولو كان كذلك فلا يبعد أن لا يتحد زمان صدق المتّصلة مع زمان صدق المقدمة الاستثنائية وهي وضع المقدم أو رفع التالي. والقياس الذي هذا شأنه لا ينتج شيئًا فإن من اللوازم أن يكون الزمان متحدا.
As in our saying: 'Whenever A-B, then C-D.' For if knowledge of the existence of A-B occurs to us, knowledge of the existence of C-D occurs to us; and if knowledge of the contradictory of C-D occurs to us, knowledge of the contradictory of A-B occurs to us, knowledge derived from knowledge of the two premises. And it cannot be like that if the conditional is accidental. And just as it is necessary that this affirmative conditional be necessary, so too must it be universal; for if it is particular, then knowledge of the existence of the antecedent does not necessitate knowledge of the existence of the consequent, even if the particular is true with no room for doubt. Nor similarly [does knowledge] from knowledge of the contradictory of the consequent [necessitate] knowledge of the contradictory of the antecedent; for it is possible that the time of the truth of the conditional is other than the time of the truth of the antecedent or the contradictory of the consequent. And if it were so, it is not unlikely that the time of the truth of the conditional does not coincide with the time of the truth of the exceptional premise, which is positing the antecedent or negating the consequent. And the syllogism of this description produces nothing, for it is among the requirements that the time be united.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for a valid conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The author states that the conditional premise must be both necessary (luzumiyya) and universal (kulliyya). If it is accidental (ittifaqiyya) or particular (juz'iyya), the inference does not yield knowledge because the time of the conditional's truth may differ from the time of the posited antecedent or denied consequent. The syllogism then produces nothing (la yuntij shay'an). The author emphasizes temporal unity as a necessary condition.

Key Terms
المتّصلة (al-muttasila) - the conditional premise
لزومية (luzumiyya) - necessary conditional
اتفاقية (ittifaqiyya) - accidental conditional

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it cannot be used in a syllogism with two premises.

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is only valid if its premise is necessary, universal, and temporally unified with the exceptional premise; otherwise it produces no certain knowledge.

Form over Matter: Refuting the Temporal Unity Objection
Author refutes an objection about temporal unity in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical form (sura) and material content (madda) of a conditional premise, and explain why the objection about temporal unity fails.

Core Idea
Logical form, not material conditions, governs conditional syllogism validity.

In this passage, the author confronts an objection that would tie the validity of a conditional syllogism to the time at which its antecedent is true. He masterfully refutes this by showing that the antecedent and consequent are formal roles, not material facts. The existence of man as such does not necessitate animal; that relation only holds under the description of being antecedent. Therefore, syllogistic validity must be assessed by the form (sura) of the premise, not its matter (madda).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
The objector claims that positing the antecedent only counts as positing the necessary condition if it occurs in the same time as the truth of the conditional, whether universal or particular.
2
Recognize the Author's Core Distinction
The author distinguishes the thing itself (e.g., existence of man) from the thing under the description of being antecedent (e.g., man qua necessary condition for animal). The former is not a necessary condition; only the latter is.
Example: 'existence of man' as such does not necessitate 'existence of animal'.
3
Focus on Logical Form
The author insists that the proper consideration in a syllogism is the form of the premise, not its material content. He uses the example of the universal affirmative categorical: if we considered material coextensiveness, we would get absurd results.
The form (sura) is the import of the concept qua assumed premise.
4
Apply to Conditional Syllogisms
Therefore, in a conditional syllogism, we must attend to the concept of the premise according to form, ignoring material relations like temporal unity or conversion of terms.
This follows Ibn Sina's approach in al-Shifa.

ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه وضع المقدم كما لا يمكن أن يكون صدقه في غير زمان صدق المتّصلة إذا كانت المتّصلة كلية فكذلك إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية؛ فإنه إذا كان في غير ذلك الزمان فلا يكون ذلك الوضع وضع المقدم. والمقدم عبارة عما يمتنع وجوده دون وجود التالي، ولا يمتنع وجوده دون وجود التالي إلا في زمان كانت تلك المتصلة صادقة في ذلك الزمان. وكذلك رفع التالي فإن التالي لا يكون تاليا إلا في ذلك الزمان. ولا يستراب في أن الشيء المتصف بصفة كونه مقدما أو تاليا من حيث هو هو غير ذلك الشيء مع هذه الصفة يعني غير ما يكون لفظ المقدم أو التالي عبارة عنه؛ فوجود الإنسان مثلا من حيث هو وجود الإنسان لا يكون ملزوما لوجود كانت أو شرطية: وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه. والملزوم في الموجبة الكلية كما يستلزم وجود اللازم استلزاما كليا فكذلك في الموجبة الجزئية لاستحالة وجود الملزوم دون اللازم. وكذلك نقيض اللازم فإنه مما يستلزم نقيض الملزوم استلزاما كليا سواء كانت الموجبة كلية أو جزئية. وبالجملة فالمتصلة فيما نحن فيه كلية كانت أو جزئية فهي مقدمة من مقدّمتي القياس. واللازم هو النظر في صورة القياس كما أشار إليه الشيخ في الشفاء وهو النظر المقتصر علي موجب مفهوم المقدمة من حيث هي المقدمة المفروضة. فأما من حيث إن لها مادة ومادة وخصوصية وخصوصية فليس ذلك نظرًا فيها بالذات؛ فإذا قلنا إن كل «أب» فـ ج د وجعلنا هذا القول مقدّمة يبنى عليها قياس يجب أن يلتفت إلى مفهوم هذه المقدمة بحسب الصورة. فأما أن تاليها هل هو تنعكس على مقدمها كما ذهب إليه قوم من القدماء في الملازمة التامة: فذلك نظر في غير صورة المقدمة بل هو نظر في الموجبة الكلية ما هو تام الحمل ومنه ما ليس تام الحمل فإن كان تام الحمل فإنها تنعكس مثل نفسها، وإن لم يكن تام الحمل تنعكس جزئية. ومن المعلوم أنه لا تكون اللوازم أصلا فإذن يجب في الشرطيات أن
Then an objector may say regarding it: The positing of the antecedent, just as its truth cannot be at a time other than the time of the truth of the conditional if the conditional is universal, so too if the conditional is particular; for if it is at a time other than that time, then that positing is not the positing of the antecedent. And the antecedent is that whose existence is impossible without the existence of the consequent, and its existence is not impossible without the existence of the consequent except in a time when that conditional is true in that time. Likewise the negation of the consequent, for the consequent is not a consequent except in that time. And there is no doubt that the thing described by the attribute of being antecedent or consequent insofar as it is itself is other than that thing with this attribute, meaning other than what the word 'antecedent' or 'consequent' is an expression for; for example, the existence of man insofar as it is the existence of man is not a necessary condition for the existence of animal. How so? And it, insofar as it is itself, is not described by any attribute at all. Since it is not a necessary condition, its positing is not the positing of the necessary condition; and the discussion is about the positing of the necessary condition. And similarly regarding the negation of the consequent: for the consequent insofar as it is the consequent is other than the thing which is described by the attribute of being consequent or necessary; therefore the negation of that thing is not the negation of the necessary consequent. And it is not said: 'If it were as mentioned, then the particular affirmative would convert by contraposition; but that is subject to prevention; for the particular affirmative converts by contraposition, whether categorical or conditional: and this is among what has been discussed before.' And the necessary condition in the universal affirmative, just as it necessitates the existence of the necessary consequent with universal necessitation, so too in the particular affirmative, because of the impossibility of the existence of the necessary condition without the necessary consequent. Likewise the contradictory of the necessary consequent, for it necessitates the contradictory of the necessary condition with universal necessitation, whether the affirmative is universal or particular. In sum, the conditional in our discussion, whether universal or particular, is a premise from the two premises of the syllogism. And the necessary thing is to consider the form of the syllogism, as the Shaykh indicated in al-Shifa, and it is the consideration confined to the import of the concept of the premise insofar as it is the assumed premise. As for insofar as it has matter and particularity, that is not an essential consideration of it. So if we say 'Every A-B is C-D' and we make this statement a premise upon which a syllogism is built, we must attend to the concept of this premise according to form. As for whether its consequent converts with its antecedent, as some of the ancients held in complete entailment: that is a consideration outside the form of the premise, rather it is a consideration of the matter of the premise. And it is only like the consideration of the predicate of the universal affirmative insofar as it is coextensive with the subject or not. So if this kind of consideration were considered in defining the rulings of premises and syllogisms, it would be necessary to say there that among universal affirmatives there is what has complete predication and what does not have complete predication; if it has complete predication it converts as itself, and if it does not have complete predication it converts as particular. And it is known that there are no [such] necessary implications at all. So then it is necessary in conditionals that...
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding temporal unity in conditional syllogisms. The objector argues that even in a particular conditional, the positing of the antecedent must be at the time of the conditional's truth, otherwise it is not truly the antecedent. The author responds by distinguishing between the thing itself and the thing qua antecedent or consequent: existence of man as such does not necessarily entail animal; it only does so under the attribute of being antecedent. The focus should be on the logical form (sura) of the premise, not its matter (madda). The author supports this by referencing Ibn Sina's al-Shifa, arguing that considerations of conversion (aks) based on material coextensiveness are not essential to formal syllogistic validity. He gives the example of universal affirmative categoricals: if we considered material completeness, we would have to say some convert as themselves, some as particulars, which leads to absurdity. Therefore, in conditional syllogisms, we must consider form alone.

Necessity of Implication in Conditional Syllogisms
Author refutes possible implication and distinguishes form from matter in conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate conditional propositions for the necessity of their implication and distinguish between the formal and material aspects of a conditional premise as required for valid syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
Implication must be necessary; possibility yields only epistemic uncertainty.

In this passage, the author elaborates on a crucial requirement for conditional syllogisms: the implication (luzum) between antecedent and consequent must be necessary, not merely possible. He also distinguishes between the logical form (sura) and the content (madda) of a premise, following Ibn Sina. This lesson will guide you through these key distinctions and their implications for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent in a conditional proposition
Find the malzum (antecedent) and lazim (consequent) in the given conditional. Note that the consequent is distinct from the antecedent, so denying the antecedent does not deny the consequent.
The author says 'رفع ذلك الشيء ليس رفع اللازم'.
2
Determine the universality of implication
Check whether the conditional is universal or partial. In a universal affirmative conditional, the antecedent necessitates the consequent universally; in a partial affirmative, the antecedent cannot exist without the consequent. Similarly, the negation of the consequent necessitates the negation of the antecedent universally.
The author states 'والملزوم في الموجبة الكلية كما يستلزم وجود اللازم استلزاما كليا فكذلك في الموجبة الجزئية'.
3
Separate form from matter
Recognize that for logical analysis, we consider only the form of the premise—the fact that it is a conditional with an antecedent and consequent—not the specific content. Do not consider whether the terms are coextensive or whether the consequent is convertible with the antecedent; that belongs to matter.
This echoes Ibn Sina's guidance in al-Shifa.
4
Verify necessity of implication
Ensure that the implication is necessary (wajib al-luzum). If the implication is merely possible, the syllogism will not yield a certain conclusion. The conclusion must be either existence or non-existence absolutely.
The author says 'وإلا لا يكون القياس منتجا'.
5
Refute the possibility of possible implication
Understand that statements like 'If this is an animal, it might be a human' do not reflect a genuine necessary implication but only mental possibility. In reality, every actual animal is either necessarily human or necessarily not human.
Ibn Sina is credited with this refutation.

إذ الموجبة الجزئية تنعكس عكس النقيض حملية كانت أو شرطية : وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه . والملزوم في الموجبة الكلية كما يستلزم وجود اللازم استلزاما كليا فكذلك في الموجبة الجزئية لاستحالة وجود محفوظة من حيث هي متّصلة لها مقدم وتال هما كذا وكذا فلا يجب أن يلتفت إليه ؛ فإن ذلك مما لا يفهم عن صورة المقدمة بل من اللواحق الممكنة. ثمَّ التالي لا يكون تاليا للمقدم إلا وأن يكون واجب اللزوم عند
