هذا حيوانًا فيمكن أن يكون إنسانا»؛ وإن حكم الاستثناء في هذه الصورة على عكس الحكم فيما سلف. وإنما عرفتم هذه المادة على الإمكان فيها إمكان بحسب الذهن لا بحسب نفس الأمر؛ إذ ليس شيء من خارج هو حيوان؛ ويمكن أن يكون إنسانا بل واجب أن يكون إنسانًا أو واجب أن لا يكون إنسانًا البتة. ولا يمكن أن يكون بوجه يمكن أن يصير إنسانًا، وجوهره باق كما كان. وقد مر من قبل أن الحيوان الذي هو إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون غير إنسان والحيوان الذي هو غير إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون إنسانًا البتة. ولو كان كذلك فذلك غير منتج على ما ظنوه. ولا يلزم منه شيء أصلا بل (عسى» إنما يلزم منه في الإمكان الذهني الذي يتحقق بنسبة الأعم إلى الأخص؛ وهذا شيء وراء كون اللزوم ممكنا. ثم من المعلوم أن يكون إمكان اللزوم غير لزوم الإمكان. وكيف وقد كان من الممكن أن يوجد هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم من كون ذلك مشتملا على الفساد أن يكون هذا مشتملا على الفساد، ومن كون هذا مشتملا على النتيجة
For the consequent, inasmuch as it is a consequent, is other than the thing described by the attribute of being a consequent or a necessary concomitant; so the negation of that thing is not the negation of the necessary concomitant. And it is not said that if it were as mentioned, then the partial affirmative would convert by contraposition; that is subject to denial, because the partial affirmative converts by contraposition whether it is categorical or conditional: and this is among what has already been discussed. And the antecedent in the universal affirmative, just as it necessarily entails the existence of the consequent universally, likewise in the partial affirmative due to the impossibility of the existence of the antecedent without the consequent. And likewise the contradictory of the consequent entails the contradictory of the antecedent universally, whether the affirmative is universal or partial. In summary, the conditional [muttasila] in our context, whether universal or partial, is a premise of the two premises of the syllogism. And the necessary [consideration] is the consideration of the form of the syllogism, as al-Shaykh indicated in al-Shifa: it is the consideration confined to the meaning of the premise inasmuch as it is the assumed premise. As for inasmuch as it has matter and particularity, that is not a consideration of it per se. So if we say 'Every A is B and C' and make this statement a premise upon which a syllogism is built, we must attend to the meaning of this premise according to form. As for whether its consequent converts to its antecedent as a group of the ancients held regarding complete implication—that is a consideration not of the form of the premise but a consideration of the matter of the premise. It is nothing but like considering the predicate of the universal affirmative in terms of whether it is equal to the subject or not equal. So if this type of consideration were taken into account in defining the laws of premises and syllogisms, it would follow that one would have to say there that among universal affirmatives there is that which is complete in predication and that which is not complete in predication: if it is complete in predication, it converts as itself, and if it is not complete, it converts as a partial. And it is known that no such consequences are required at all. Therefore, in conditionals, one must attend to the condition itself or to the antecedent and consequent inasmuch as the consequent is a consequent and the antecedent is an antecedent, and to what necessarily follows from that as a general necessity whatever the matter may be. As for that in which the form of the premise is preserved inasmuch as it is a conditional having an antecedent and a consequent that are such and such, one need not attend to it; for that is not understood from the form of the premise but from possible accidents. Furthermore, the consequent is not a consequent of the antecedent unless it is of necessary implication when the antecedent is realized, and it is the [antecedent] that is the antecedent; otherwise the syllogism would not be productive, because the conclusion is either the existence of the thing or its non-existence, absolutely, not possibly. And al-Shaykh mentioned in al-Shifa a group of the earlier thinkers who thought that implication could be in the mode of possibility, as in their saying 'If this is an animal, it is possible that it is a human'; and the rule of exception in this form is the opposite of the rule in what preceded. They only knew this matter as possibility, a possibility according to the mind, not according to the actual state of affairs; for there is no external thing that is an animal and is possibly a human, but it is necessarily a human or necessarily not a human at all. And it is not possible that there be a way by which it could become a human while its essence remains as it was. And it has passed before that the animal that is human cannot be non-human, and the animal that is non-human cannot be human at all. And if it were as they thought, then it would not be productive. And nothing necessarily follows from it at all, but 'maybe' follows from it only according to mental possibility which is realized by the relation of the more general to the more specific; and this is something beyond implication being possible. Then it is known that the possibility of implication is not the implication of possibility. And how could it be, when it was possible that this exist without that, and vice versa. And if it were so, then it would not follow that from the fact that that involves corruption this involves corruption, and from this involving the conclusion...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the nature of implication (luzum) in conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) and the distinction between form (sura) and matter (madda) in logical premises. The author begins by clarifying the relationship between the antecedent (malzum) and consequent (lazim) in a conditional proposition. He stresses that the consequent is distinct from the antecedent; thus, denying the antecedent does not deny the consequent. He then addresses the conversion (aks) of partial affirmative propositions by contraposition (aks al-naqid), affirming that both categorical and conditional partial affirmatives convert by contraposition—a point already established earlier. The author emphasizes that in a universal affirmative conditional, the antecedent necessitates the consequent universally, and similarly in a partial affirmative, since the antecedent cannot exist without the consequent. Likewise, the negation of the consequent necessitates the negation of the antecedent universally, regardless of quantity. The core of the argument is that every conditional proposition, universal or partial, serves as a premise in a syllogism. The necessary consideration, as taught by Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) in al-Shifa, is the form of the syllogism—i.e., the meaning of the premise as a premise, not its specific content or matter. For example, if one says 'Every A is B and C', we must examine the proposition's logical form, not whether its consequent converts to its antecedent as some ancients held regarding complete implication (mulazama tamma). That latter consideration pertains to matter, not form. To illustrate, the author analogizes it to considering whether the predicate of a universal affirmative is coextensive with the subject—this would lead to classifying universal affirmatives into those that convert as themselves (if predicate and subject are equal) and those that convert as partial (if not). However, such distinctions are not required in the formal study of premises and syllogisms. Therefore, in conditionals, we must attend only to the condition itself or to the antecedent and consequent as such, and to the universal necessity of implication regardless of the matter. The author then stresses a crucial condition: the consequent is a valid consequent of the antecedent only if the implication is necessary (wajib al-luzum) when the antecedent holds; otherwise the syllogism will not yield a certain conclusion. The conclusion must be either existence or non-existence absolutely, not possibly. Finally, the author recounts Ibn Sina's refutation of earlier thinkers who posited possible implication, as in 'If this is an animal, it might be a human.' Ibn Sina argues that such a statement reflects a mental possibility, not objective reality: any actual animal is either necessarily human or necessarily not human. The possibility is merely epistemic, based on the relation of the more general (animal) to the more specific (human). This does not constitute genuine implication; the possibility of implication (imkan al-luzum) is distinct from the implication of possibility (luzum al-imkan). Thus, such conditionals are unproductive for syllogistic reasoning because the necessary connection is absent.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the conditional 'If this is an animal, it might be a human' not productive in a syllogism?
A) Because 'animal' and 'human' are not related as genus and species.
B) Because the implication is not necessary; every animal is either necessarily human or necessarily not human.
C) Because the conditional is universal but the conclusion is partial.
D) Because the antecedent is not identical to the consequent.

Key Takeaway
For a conditional syllogism to yield a certain conclusion, the implication between antecedent and consequent must be necessary, not merely possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism based on a ruling in fiqh: 'If the sale is free of ambiguity (gharar), it is valid.' Now, identify whether the implication is necessary or possible. Justify your answer using the criteria from this lesson.
?
Analyze the statement 'If the prayer is invalid due to impurity, then it must be repeated.' Determine if the implication is necessary and whether it can serve as a premise in a productive conditional syllogism.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Necessity of Implication in Conditional Syllogisms
Author argues necessity of implication for syllogistic productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional syllogism is productive by checking if the implication is necessary, and distinguish necessary implication from mental possibility.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism yields certainty only if the implication is necessary, not possible.

In this passage, the author explores a critical condition for the productivity of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i). He critiques ancient logicians who thought that a possible implication could yield a conclusion, and others who demanded actual truth of the premises. Through his analysis, you will learn to identify what makes an implication necessary and why mere mental possibility fails to produce knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
Read the author's statement: 'the consequent is not a consequent of the antecedent unless it is necessarily implied when the antecedent is realized, otherwise the syllogism is not productive'. This is the central claim: the implication (luzum) must be necessary (wajib al-luzum).
Focus on the phrase 'واجب اللزوم' in the Arabic.
2
Analyze the first critique: possible implication
The author refutes the view that implication can be possible, as in 'If this is an animal, then it is possible that it be a human.' He argues that this is only mental possibility (imkan dhihni), not real. In reality, every animal either is necessarily human or necessarily not human. Therefore, the implication is not necessary and the syllogism is unproductive.
Contrast the mental relation (general to specific) with actual necessity.
3
Examine the second critique: actual truth requirement
Some ancients claimed that productivity requires the implication to be true with the consequent actually false (or antecedent actually true). The author rejects this, giving the example 'If man were a horse, he would be an animal.' This implication is true even though the antecedent is false; the syllogism is productive because the logical connection holds and the contrapositive is valid.
Productivity depends on the logical form, not on the material truth of the premises.
4
Apply the condition
Take a conditional syllogism from Islamic theology: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, it has a cause.' Check if the implication is necessary. Since temporality necessarily entails a cause (in kalām), the syllogism is productive. If the implication were merely possible, the syllogism would fail.
Use this to solidify the distinction between necessary and possible implication.

يلتفت إلى نفس الشرط أو إلى المقدم والتالي من حيث إن التالي تال والمقدم مقدم، وإلى نفس ما يلزم ذلك لزوما عامًا كيف كانت المادة. فأما ما يكون صورة المقدمة فيه محفوظة من حيث هي متّصلة لها مقدم وتال هما كذا وكذا فلا يجب أن يلتفت إليه؛ فإن ذلك مما لا يفهم عن صورة المقدمة بل من اللواحق الممكنة. ثمَّ التالي لا يكون تاليا للمقدم إلا وأن يكون واجب اللزوم عند تحققه وهو الملزوم وإلا لا يكون القياس منتجا؛ إذ المطلوب إما وجود الشيء وإما عدمه قطعا لا محتملا. والشيخ ذكر في الشفاء قوما من المتقدمين ظنوا أن اللزوم قد يكون على سبيل الإمكان كما في قولهم إن كان هذا حيوانًا فيمكن أن يكون إنسانا؛ وإن حكم الاستثناء في هذه الصورة على عكس الحكم فيما سلف. وإنما عرفتم هذه المادة على الإمكان فيها إمكان بحسب الذهن لا بحسب نفس الأمر؛ إذ ليس شيء من خارج هو حيوان؛ ويمكن أن يكون إنسانا بل واجب أن يكون إنسانًا أو واجب أن لا يكون إنسانًا البتة. ولا يمكن أن يكون بوجه يمكن أن يصير إنسانًا، وجوهره باق كما كان. وقد مر من قبل أن الحيوان الذي هو إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون غير إنسان والحيوان الذي هو غير إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون إنسانًا البتة. ولو كان كذلك فذلك غير منتج على ما ظنوه. ولا يلزم منه شيء أصلا بل (عسى) إنما يلزم منه في الإمكان الذهني الذي يتحقق بنسبة الأعم إلى الأخص؛ وهذا شيء وراء كون اللزوم ممكنا. ثم من المعلوم أن يكون إمكان اللزوم غير لزوم الإمكان. وكيف وقد كان من الممكن أن يوجد هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. ولو كان كذلك فلا يلزم من كون ذلك مشتملا على الفساد أن يكون هذا مشتملا على الفساد، ومن كون هذا مشتملا على النتيجة أن يكون ذلك مشتملا على النتيجة، وهذا ظاهر. ثم إن قوما من المتقدمين زعموا أن المنتج من الملازمة هو صدق الملازمة مع انتفاء اللازم في نفس الأمر، أو ثبوت الملزوم كذلك لا مع فرض انتفاء اللازم أو فرض ثبوت الملزوم: وهذا فاسد فإن المنتج هو القياس الاستثنائي؛ وذلك فيما نحن فيه وهو الملازمة مع ثبوت الملزوم حقيقة أو فرضًا، أو الملازمة مع انتفاء اللازم كذلك. وكون القياس منتجا لا يكون مفتقرًا إلى أن تكون المقدمات بأجمعها مطابقة لما هو في الخارج حقيقة بل قد يكون منتجا وإن لم تكن المقدمات مطابقة لما هو في الخارج كلا أو بعضًا كما في قولنا إن كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانا فإنه صادق. ولو كان صادقًا لكان من اللوازم أن يكون نقيض التالي مستلزما لنقيض المقدم؛ فيكون القياس منتجا.
He considers the condition itself, or the antecedent and consequent inasmuch as the consequent is consequent and the antecedent is antecedent, and the very thing that necessitates that universally, whatever the matter may be. As for that which preserves the form of the premise insofar as it is connected, having an antecedent and a consequent which are such-and-such, it is not necessary to consider it; for that is not understood from the form of the premise but from possible concomitants. Then, the consequent is not a consequent of the antecedent unless it is necessarily implied when the antecedent is realized, otherwise the syllogism is not productive; for the required [conclusion] is either existence of the thing or its non-existence, decisively, not probabilistically. And the Shaykh mentioned in the Shifa a group of the ancients who thought that implication might be possible, as in their saying, 'If this is an animal, then it is possible that it be a human'; and the rule of exception in this form is contrary to the rule in what preceded. You only know this matter regarding possibility in it as a possibility according to the mind, not according to the actual state of affairs; for there is nothing external that is 'animal' and possible to be 'human'; rather it is necessarily either human or necessarily not human at all. And it cannot be in any way that it could become human while its essence remains as it was. It has already passed that the animal which is human cannot be non-human, and the animal which is non-human cannot be human at all. If it were so, then that would be unproductive according to what they thought. Nothing follows from it at all; rather 'perhaps' only follows from it in mental possibility which is realized by the relation of the more general to the more specific; and this is beyond the implication being possible. Then it is known that possibility of implication is not the same as implication of possibility. How? It was possible that this exist without that, and vice versa. If it were so, then it does not follow from that thing's containing corruption that this thing contains corruption, and from this thing's containing the result that that thing contains the result. This is obvious. Then a group of the ancients claimed that the productive [syllogism] from the implication is the truth of the implication with the negation of the consequent in actual fact, or the affirmation of the antecedent likewise, not with the supposition of the negation of the consequent or the supposition of the affirmation of the antecedent. This is corrupt, for the productive is the conditional syllogism; and that is in what we are discussing, namely the implication with the affirmation of the antecedent actually or suppositionally, or the implication with the negation of the consequent likewise. And the syllogism being productive does not require that all the premises correspond to what is external in reality; rather it may be productive even if the premises do not correspond to what is external, wholly or partially, as in our saying, 'If man were a horse, he would be an animal,' for it is true. And if it were true, it would be among the necessary consequences that the contradictory of the consequent entails the contradictory of the antecedent; thus the syllogism would be productive.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the condition for a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) to be productive. The author first distinguishes between considering the form of the premise—where the antecedent and consequent are taken merely as such—and considering the actual implication that holds universally regardless of matter. For productivity, the implication must be necessary (wajib al-luzum), not contingent, because the conclusion must be decisive. He then critiques two groups of ancient logicians. The first group, mentioned by Ibn Sina in the Shifa, thought implication could be possible, as in 'If this is an animal, then it is possible that it be a human.' The author argues this is merely mental possibility (imkan dhihni) based on the relation of the more general (animal) to the more specific (human), not real possibility. In reality, every animal is either necessarily human or necessarily not human; possibility does not exist in actuality. If the implication were merely possible, the syllogism would not produce a certain conclusion. The second group claimed that productivity requires the implication to be true while the consequent is actually false (or the antecedent actually true), not just suppositionally. The author rejects this, stating that productivity can occur even if the premises are not actually true, as in the hypothetical 'If man were a horse, he would be an animal.' This implication is true because of the logical connection, and the contrapositive also holds, so the syllogism is valid. Thus, the key is the necessity of the implication itself, not the material truth of the premises.

Key Terms
اللزوم
الإمكان الذهني
القياس الاستثنائي

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the proposition 'If this is an animal, then it is possible that it be a human' not productive in a conditional syllogism?
A) Because the implication is not necessary; it is only mental possibility.
B) Because the antecedent is false.
C) Because the consequent is false.
D) Because the syllogism requires a categorical premise.

Key Takeaway
For a conditional syllogism to produce a certain conclusion, the implication between antecedent and consequent must be necessary (wajib al-luzum), not merely possible, and this necessity does not depend on the actual truth of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional syllogism on a topic from usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'If a ruling is based on effective cause ('illah), then it applies wherever the 'illah is found. This ruling is based on 'illah. Therefore, it applies here.') Check if the implication is necessary. If the 'illah is properly established, the implication is necessary; if not, the syllogism fails.
?
Identify the mistake in the following argument: 'If it is a liquid, then it is possible that it is wine. It is a liquid. Therefore, it is wine.' Explain why this conditional syllogism is unproductive, using the author's concept of mental possibility.

Types of Affirmative Conditionals and Their Productivity
Author classifies affirmative conditionals and examines their productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an affirmative conditional is particular, absolute, or quantified, and determine whether it yields a valid conclusion in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Only conditionals with specified implication guarantee productivity.

Not every conditional proposition can be used in a conditional syllogism to produce a certain conclusion. The author classifies affirmative conditionals into three types and shows that only those with a specified time or universal quantifier yield valid results. This lesson will help you recognize each type and apply the conditions for productivity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of affirmative conditionals
The author mentions mukhasasa (particular), muhmala (absolute), and mahdura (quantified). Each type differs in how the implication is qualified. Look for time-bound expressions, absence of quantifiers, or explicit universality/particularity.
2
Analyze the particular conditional example
The example is 'If he comes to me this hour, then I honor him; but he did not come, so I did not honor him.' Here the time 'this hour' specifies the scope of the implication. This syllogism uses modus tollens and is productive because the implication holds for that specific time.
3
Understand why the absolute conditional fails
The absolute conditional 'If every A is B, then every C is D' lacks a quantifier over the implication. It does not state whether the implication holds in all considerations or only some. When we deny the consequent, we cannot be sure that the implication still applies in the given instance, so the syllogism is not productive.
4
Contrast with quantified conditionals
Quantified conditionals (universal or particular) specify the scope of the implication. If the conditional is universal, the implication holds in all cases, and modus tollens is valid. If particular, it holds only for some cases, limiting productivity. The passage does not elaborate fully, but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن المتصلة الموجبة في هذا القياس قد تكون مخصوصة، وقد تكون مهملة، وقد تكون محصورة والمحصورة قد تكون كلية، وقد تكون جزئية والمقدم في كل واحدة من المهملة والمحصورة قد يكون كليا، وقد يكون جزئيا. وكذلك التالي في كل واحدة منهما. أما إذا كانت مخصوصة كما في قولنا إن جاءني هذه الساعة فأكرمه لكنه لم يجى فلم أكرمه؛ فإنه منتج لا ريب فيه. فأما إذا كانت مهملة فإما أن يكون كل واحد من المقدم والتالي فيها كليا كما في قولنا إن كان كل «أب» فكل جد»؛ وإنه لا يكون منتجا فإن معنى هذه القضية أن يكون هذا الكلي مستلزما لذلك من غير بيان أن يكون ذلك الاستلزام كليًّا يعني غير بيان أنه في جميع الأوقات وجميع الاعتبارات أم فبتقدير أن لا يتحقق ذلك إلا على بعض الاعتبارات. فلا يبعد أن تكون حال الاستثناء غير حال اللزوم؛ وحينذ لا يلزم أن يكون منتجا، لا يقال متى ثبت اللزوم على بعض الاعتبارات ثبت اللزوم دائما يعني على
If you know this, then we say: The affirmative conditional in this syllogism may be particular (mukhasasa), or absolute (muhmala), or quantified (mahdura). And the quantified may be universal or particular. The antecedent in each of the absolute and the quantified may be universal or particular, and likewise the consequent in each of them. As for when it is particular, as in our saying, 'If he comes to me this hour, then I honor him, but he did not come, so I did not honor him,' it is undoubtedly productive. As for when it is absolute, then if each of the antecedent and consequent in it is universal, as in our saying, 'If every A is a B, then every C is a D,' it is not productive, for the meaning of this proposition is that this universal necessitates that, without explaining whether that necessitation is universal—that is, without explaining whether it is in all times and all considerations, or it is realized only in some considerations. So it is not unlikely that the state of the exception is different from the state of the implication; then it does not necessarily follow that it is productive. It cannot be said: whenever the implication is established in some considerations, it is established always, meaning...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage classifies affirmative conditional propositions (al-muttasila al-mujaba) used in conditional syllogisms into three types: particular (mukhasasa), absolute (muhmala), and quantified (mahdura). The quantified further divides into universal and particular. The author notes that for each type, the antecedent and consequent can be universal or particular. He then examines the productivity of each. A particular conditional, like 'If he comes to me this hour, then I honor him, but he did not come, so I did not honor him,' is clearly productive; it is a specific time-bound conditional where the implication holds in that particular case. However, an absolute conditional—one without any quantifier—is problematic. If both the antecedent and consequent are universal terms, like 'If every A is a B, then every C is a D,' the conditional does not specify whether the implication holds universally (in all times and considerations) or only in some. Because of this ambiguity, when we deny the consequent (the second premise in modus tollens), we cannot be sure that the implication still holds in the specific case we are considering. Hence, the syllogism is not productive. The author argues against the objection that if the implication holds in some considerations, it holds always; he implies that is not necessarily the case. This classification relates to how logicians analyze conditional propositions in terms of generality, which affects the validity of inferences.

Key Terms
المتصلة الموجبة
مخصوصة
مهملة
محصورة

C) Because the antecedent and consequent are both particular.
D) Because the conditional is false.

Key Takeaway
The productivity of a conditional syllogism depends on whether the implication is specified as universal or time-bound; absolute conditionals lack this specification and are thus unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Productiveness in Conditional Syllogisms: Truth vs. Implication
Author refutes claim that productiveness requires truth of premises in reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between productiveness based on logical implication and productiveness based on ontological truth, using the example of a false-antecedent true conditional.

Core Idea
Productiveness depends on implication structure, not on actual truth of premises.

In this passage, the author corrects a common misconception among earlier logicians. They thought that a conditional syllogism is productive only if the premises are true in external reality. The author shows that productiveness is about the logical implication and the exception, regardless of actual truth. Understanding this protects us from confusing logical validity with factual truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false view
Find the claim of the earlier scholars: productiveness requires the truth of the implication together with the non-existence of the consequent in reality (or the existence of the antecedent in reality), not merely hypothetically.
The false view conflates logical productiveness with factual truth.
2
Understand the author's correction
The author states that productiveness is about the conditional syllogism (قياس استثنائي) and requires only the implication (ملازمة) together with the existence of the antecedent (in reality or hypothetically) or the non-existence of the consequent (similarly). Thus productiveness is a matter of logical structure, not ontological correspondence.
The author uses 'حقيقة أو فرضًا' to emphasize both reality and hypothesis.
3
Analyze the counterexample
Examine the example: 'If man were a horse, he would be an animal.' This conditional is true because every horse is an animal, regardless of the fact that man is not a horse. The implication holds universally, making the conditional true and the syllogism productive if the exception (e.g., affirming the antecedent) is made.
False antecedent does not affect the truth of the conditional; only the implication matters.
4
Apply to Islamic legal reasoning
Consider a legal conditional: 'If an act is intoxicating, it is forbidden.' This conditional is true whether or not a specific intoxicant exists. A syllogism that affirms the antecedent (this drink is intoxicating) yields the conclusion (it is forbidden). The productiveness does not depend on the actual existence of the drink.
This principle underpins the validity of analogical reasoning (qiyas) in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون ذلك مشتملا على النتيجة، وهذا ظاهر. ثم إن قوما من المتقدمين زعموا أن المنتج من الملازمة هو صدق الملازمة مع انتفاء اللازم في نفس الأمر، أو ثبوت الملزوم كذلك لا مع فرض انتفاء اللازم أو فرض ثبوت الملزوم: وهذا فاسد فإن المنتج هو القياس الاستثنائي؛ وذلك فيما نحن فيه وهو الملازمة مع ثبوت الملزوم حقيقة أو فرضًا، أو الملازمة مع انتفاء اللازم كذلك. وكون القياس منتجا لا يكون مفتقرًا إلى أن تكون المقدمات بأجمعها مطابقة لما هو في الخارج حقيقة بل قد يكون منتجا وإن لم تكن المقدمات مطابقة لما هو في الخارج كلا أو بعضًا كما في قولنا إن كان الإنسان فرسا كان حيوانًا فإنه صادق. ولو كان صادقًا لكان من اللوازم أن يكون نقيض التالي مستلزما لنقيض المقدم؛ فيكون القياس منتجا.
That the [syllogism] includes the conclusion - this is apparent. Then a group of earlier scholars claimed that the productiveness of the implication is the truth of the implication together with the non-existence of the consequent in reality, or the existence of the antecedent likewise, not with the assumption of non-existence of the consequent or assumption of existence of the antecedent. This is false, for the productive [syllogism] is the conditional syllogism; and that is in our subject: the implication together with the existence of the antecedent in reality or hypothetically, or the implication together with the non-existence of the consequent similarly. And that the syllogism is productive does not require that all premises correspond to what is in external reality, but rather it may be productive even if the premises do not correspond to external reality in whole or in part, as in our saying: 'If man were a horse, he would be an animal' - for it is true. And if it were true, it would be among the necessary consequences that the contradictory of the consequent is implicative of the contradictory of the antecedent; thus the syllogism becomes productive.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of productiveness (intaj) in conditional syllogisms. The author first clarifies a common misconception: productiveness does not depend on the actual truth of premises in reality, but rather on the logical implication structure. He uses the example 'If man were a horse, he would be an animal' which is a true conditional even though the antecedent is false. The key point: productiveness is about the validity of the implication and the truth of the exception (istithna') in the hypothetical sense. This guards against the mistake of thinking that logical inference requires ontological truth.

Key Terms
منتج
ملازمة
قياس استثنائي
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is productive based on the truth of the implication and the exception, not on the actual truth of the premises in external reality.

Classification of Conditionals: Makhsusa, Muhmala, Mahsura
Author classifies conditional propositions and gives example of productive particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conditional propositions (makhsusa, muhmala, mahsura) and explain why a particular conditional (makhsusa) is productive in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
The particular conditional (makhsusa) is the only type that yields certainty because its time is fixed.

In conditional syllogisms, not all conditionals lead to certain conclusions. The author classifies conditionals into three categories based on how they are quantified as a whole. Understanding this classification is essential for determining which conditional syllogisms are valid and productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the Arabic, find the three categories: makhsusa (particular, time-bound), muhmala (unspecified, no quantifier), and mahsura (quantified, either universal or particular).
2
Differentiate mahsura sub-types
Note that mahsura splits into kulliya (universal) and juz'iyya (particular). The quantifier applies to the entire conditional, not its parts.
3
Analyze the parts
Each conditional's muqaddima (antecedent) and tali (consequent) can independently be universal or particular, regardless of the conditional's type.
4
Examine the example
Read the example: 'If he comes to me this hour, I honor him; but he did not come, so I did not honor him.' Recognize that the time specification ('this hour') makes the conditional makhsusa, allowing a clear modus tollens inference.
The productivity is undoubted because the fixed time eliminates ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن المتصلة الموجبة في هذا القياس قد تكون مخصوصة، وقد تكون مهملة، وقد تكون محصورة والمحصورة قد تكون كلية، وقد تكون جزئية والمقدم في كل واحدة من المهملة والمحصورة قد يكون كليا، وقد يكون جزئيا. وكذلك التالي في كل واحدة منهما. أما إذا كانت مخصوصة كما في قولنا إن جاءني هذه الساعة فأكرمه لكنه لم يجى فلم أكرمه ؛ فإنه منتج لا ريب فيه.
If you have known this, we say: The affirmative conditional in this syllogism may be particular (makhsusa), or unspecified (muhmala), or quantified (mahsura), and the quantified may be universal (kulliya) or particular (juz'iyya). The antecedent in each of the unspecified and quantified may be universal or particular, and similarly the consequent in each of them. As for when it is particular as in our saying: 'If he comes to me this hour, I honor him, but he did not come, so I did not honor him' - it is undoubtedly productive.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage begins a detailed classification of conditional propositions (muttasila) in conditional syllogisms. The author distinguishes three types: particular (makhsusa: referring to a specific time/individual), unspecified (muhmala: not quantified), and quantified (mahsura: with a quantifier). The quantified further divides into universal and particular. Each part (muqaddima and tali) can independently be universal or particular. The author then gives an example of a productive particular conditional: 'If he comes to me this hour, I honor him; but he did not come, so I did not honor him.' This is straightforwardly productive because the time is fixed, so the exception is clear.

Key Terms

مهملة

Key Takeaway
The particular conditional (makhsusa) is distinguished by its fixed time or condition, making it the only type of conditional that yields a certain conclusion in a conditional syllogism.

Why Unspecified Conditionals Fail
Author explains why unspecified conditionals are generally not productive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an unspecified conditional (muhmala) and explain why it is generally not productive in conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
An unspecified conditional lacks guaranteed universal implication, so its syllogism is unproductive.

The author delves into the unspecified conditional (muhmala) and shows why it fails to produce a valid conclusion. This lesson unpacks the two subcases and the reasoning behind the non-productiveness, using examples from the natural world to illustrate time-specific necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subcases
The author considers two situations: where both antecedent and consequent are universal terms, and where they are not both universal. Recognize that the conditional itself lacks a quantifier.
2
Analyze the first subcase: universal terms
For 'If every A is B, then every C is D', the implication (luzum) is not specified as universal in time. The exception (istithna') may occur at a time when the implication does not hold, so the conclusion is uncertain. The example of breathing shows that necessity can be time-specific.
The author refutes the objection that implication once established is always established.
3
Examine the second subcase: non-universal terms
When antecedent and consequent are not universal, each can be true of a single individual. The conditional could be true for different individuals at different times, breaking the uniform connection. The example 'If A is B, then C is D' illustrates this ambiguity.
4
Conclude on non-productiveness
In both subcases, the connection between antecedent and consequent is not guaranteed across all times and individuals. Therefore, the unspecified conditional does not yield a certain conclusion in a conditional syllogism.
The author states 'it is not productive' (la yakunu muntijan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إذا كانت مهملة فإما أن يكون كل واحد من المقدم والتالي فيها كليا كما في قولنا إن كان كل «أب» فكل «ج د»؛ وإنه لا يكون منتجا فإن معنى هذه القضية أن يكون هذا الكلي مستلزما لذلك من غير بيان أن يكون ذلك الاستلزام كليًّا يعني غير بيان أنه في جميع الأوقات وجميع الاعتبارات أم فبتقدير أن لا يتحقق ذلك إلا على بعض الاعتبارات. فلا يبعد أن تكون حال الاستثناء غير حال اللزوم؛ وحينذ لا يلزم أن يكون منتجا. لا يقال متى ثبت اللزوم على بعض الاعتبارات ثبت اللزوم دائما يعني على جميع الاعتبارات، ومتى لم يثبت لم يثبت أصلا كالتنفس للإنسان؛ فإنه لا يكون لازما في البعض من الأوقات فلا يكون لازما أبدا؛ إذ اللزوم في البعض من الأوقات لا يكون مفتقرًا إلى اللزوم في جميع الأوقات بل الشيء قد يكون لازما للشيء في البعض من الأوقات وإن لم يكن لازما له بل لا يمكن أن يكون لازما له في جميع الأوقات نحو الخسوف للقمر حال حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس. فإن وجود القمر في هذا الوقت مما يمتنع وجوده بدون الانخساف. وكذلك التنفّس للإنسان في البعض من الأوقات فإنه لا يمكن أن يكون وهو غير متنفّس دائما بل بالضرورة أن يكون متنفسا وإن كان في وقت ما. وأما أن لا يكون كل واحد منهما كليًّا وإنه لا يكون منتجا أيضا لما مرَّ فيما مر. ولأن كل واحد منهما أي من المقدم والتالي إذا لم يكونا كليين كفى في صدق كل واحد منهما أن يكون في شخص واحد مثلا في قولنا إن كان «أب» فـ «ج د»؛ فقولنا «أب» مهملة فيكفي في صدقه اتصاف شخص من أشخاص له «أ» تاليا. وهكذا القول في التالي. ولما كفى في صدق المهملة التي نحن بصددها أن يتصف شخص من أشخاص ما يحمل عليه التالي بمحموله عند اتصاف شخص واحد من أشخاص ما يحمل عليه المقدم بمحموله لا زمان واحد. فإذا قلنا وكل «أب» جاز أن يكون الألف الذي في هذا الاستثناء غير الألف الذي لأجله صدق هذا الشرط، وأن لا يكون الحكم في جميع الأوقات واحدًا. وإذا كان كذلك فإنه لا يكون منتجا.
As for when it is unspecified (muhmala): either both antecedent and consequent are universal, as in our saying: 'If every A is B, then every C is D' - it is not productive. For the meaning of this proposition is that this universal is implicative of that without clarifying whether that implication is universal, i.e., without clarifying whether it is in all times and all respects, or perhaps it is realized only in some respects. So it is not unlikely that the state of the exception differs from the state of the implication; then it does not follow that it is productive. It cannot be said: when the implication is established in some respects, it is established always, i.e., in all respects, and when not established, it is not established at all, like breathing for man - it is not a necessary concomitant in some times, so it is not a necessary concomitant ever. For the implication in some times does not require implication in all times, but a thing may be a necessary concomitant for another in some times even if it is not a necessary concomitant for it, nay, it cannot be a necessary concomitant for it in all times, such as the lunar eclipse for the moon when the earth interposes between it and the sun. For the existence of the moon at that time cannot exist without eclipse. Similarly, breathing for man in some times - he cannot be without breathing always, but necessarily he breathes even if only at some time. And as for when both (i.e., antecedent and consequent) are not universal, it is also not productive, as has passed. And because each of them, i.e., antecedent and consequent, when they are not universal, it suffices for the truth of each of them that it applies to a single individual, e.g., in our saying: 'If A is B, then C is D' - our saying 'A is B' is unspecified, so it suffices for its truth that one person of the individuals of A is characterized by B [the predicate]. And likewise for the consequent. And since it suffices for the truth of the unspecified proposition we are dealing with that one person of the individuals of what the consequent is predicated of is characterized by its predicate, when one person of the individuals of what the antecedent is predicated of is characterized by its predicate, but not at a single time - so if we say 'every A is B, it is possible that the A in this exception is not the A that made the condition true, and that the judgment is not uniform across all times. And if it is so, then it is not productive.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed examination of the unspecified conditional (muhmala) and why it is generally not productive unless certain conditions are met. The author considers two subcases: (1) both antecedent and consequent are universal terms, and (2) they are not both universal. In the first subcase, the implication (mulazama) is not specified as to whether it holds in all times and respects, so the exception (istithna') may not match the implication. The example of breathing is used to show that a thing may be necessary at some times but not at all times, so a universal claim cannot be inferred. In the second subcase, the antecedent and consequent are not universal, so they could be true of different individuals, leading to non-productiveness. The author concludes that the unspecified conditional is not productive because the connection is not guaranteed.

Key Terms
مهملة (muhmala): Unspecified conditional proposition, without quantifier on the conditional itself.
لزوم (luzum): Necessity or implication; the relationship of necessary consequence between antecedent and consequent.
استثناء (istithna'): Exception; the minor premise in a conditional syllogism that affirms or denies one part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because it is a negative conditional.
D) Because it is a particular conditional.

Key Takeaway
An unspecified conditional (muhmala) is not productive because the implication is not guaranteed to hold across all times and individuals, leading to potential mismatch with the exception.

Truth Conditions of 'كلما' Conditionals
Author defends truth of 'كلما' conditional against temporal objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a 'كلما' conditional is true despite apparent temporal counterexamples, by distinguishing between coverage of states (ahwal) and continuous temporal perpetuity.

Core Idea
'كلما' conditionals cover all states, not all moments.

An objector challenges the conditional 'Whenever this is a human, it is actually breathing', pointing out that humans hold their breath. The author defends the conditional by arguing that 'كلما' covers all states (ahwal), not all moments, and that the truth of the consequent can be grounded in a different time. This clarification is essential for evaluating universal conditionals in Islamic logic and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that the conditional 'كلما كان هذا إنسانًا فهو متنفس بالفعل' is false because there are times when a human is not actually breathing (e.g., holding breath). He argues that 'كلما' requires encompassing all times, and since there is a time where antecedent is true but consequent false, the conditional fails.
2
Understand the author's defense
The author grants that 'كلما' covers all states, but asserts that it is still true because there is no state of a human where it is not actually breathing—even when holding breath, the human is 'actually breathing' in the broader sense, either before or after. The truth of the consequent is not tied to the exact moment of the antecedent.
3
Extract the general principle
The author states: 'The universality of a proposition is not conditioned on being perpetual (دائمة).' This means that a universal conditional does not require the consequent to be true at every moment the antecedent is true; it only requires that in every state where the antecedent holds, the consequent is true, possibly at a different time.
This principle guards against confusing temporal continuity with state coverage.
4
Apply the principle to a new example
Consider the fiqh principle: 'Whenever there is a sale contract (it is valid unless a defect exists).' If a defective contract is found, one might object that the conditional is false. However, applying the author's principle, the conditional covers all states of sale contracts, and the validity is understood in a qualified sense; the conditional remains true because it accounts for defects as exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول قولكم كلما كان هذا إنسانًا فهو متنفس بالفعل قضية كاذبة لما أن الصدق فيه مفتقر إلى استحالة الاتفاق بينهما، وقد كانت كلمة كلَّما لإحاطة جميع الأحوال لكنا نقول: هب أنها لإحاطة جميع الأحوال فلم قلتم بأن الإحاطة غير متحققة في هذه الصورة بل متحققة لأنه لا حال ولا وقت للإنسان إلا ويصدق معه كونه متنفسًا بالفعل لما أنه متنفّس بالفعل وإن كان في وقت من الأوقات. وإن قيل هذه أيضًا كاذبة لأنه لا يمكن أن يكون متنفسا حال كونه مستنشقا بالفعل فنقول بل هو متنفس بالفعل في تلك الحالة لكن لا يكون بالفعل في تلك الحالة بل بالفعل الذي في غير تلك الحالة إما قبلها أو بعدها. وقد مرّ من قبل أن كلية القضية لا تكون مشروطة بشرط أن تكون دائمة؛ وما ذكرتم يقتضي ذلك وهو كونه مشروطًا بذلك الشرط
Then an objector may say: Your statement 'Whenever this is a human, it is actually breathing' is a false proposition because its truth requires the impossibility of disagreement between them, and the word 'كلما' is for encompassing all states; but we say: Suppose it is for encompassing all states, why have you said that encompassing is not realized in this case? Rather it is realized because there is no state or time for a human except that it is true with it that it is actually breathing, since it is actually breathing even if it is in some time. And if it is said: This too is false because it cannot be that it is breathing while it is actually breath-holding, we say: Rather it is actually breathing in that state, but it is not actually breathing in that state, but by the actuality that is in another state, either before it or after it. And it has passed before that the universality of a proposition is not conditioned on the condition that it be perpetual; and what you have mentioned requires that, namely it being conditioned on that condition.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about the truth conditions of a conditional proposition with 'كلما' (whenever). The author presents an objection: the conditional 'Whenever this is a human, it is actually breathing' is false because there are humans who are holding their breath. The objector argues that the word 'كلما' requires covering all times, and there are times when a human is not actually breathing. The author responds by first granting that 'كلما' covers all states, but argues that it is still true because there is no state of a human in which it is not actually breathing – meaning that even when holding breath, the human is still a breathing being in a broader sense, either before or after. The author then clarifies a general principle: the universality of a proposition does not require that the predicate be true continuously (perpetually) at every moment; it only requires that whenever the subject exists, the predicate is true, but the predicate may be true at a different time. This guards against a misunderstanding that conditionals of the 'كلما' type require synchronicity of antecedent and consequent.

Key Terms
كلما
دائمة

Checkpoint

A) The human is not actually breathing at that moment, so the conditional is false.
B) The human is actually breathing by virtue of being a breather in another state, so the conditional is true.
C) The word 'كلما' does not cover such states, so the conditional is false.
D) The human is not a human at that moment, so the conditional is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The universality of a 'كلما' conditional does not require the consequent to be true at every moment the antecedent holds; it only requires that in every state where the antecedent holds, the consequent is true, possibly at a different time.

Perpetual Condition in Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains how the condition of perpetuity affects inference in conditional syllogisms when attached to antecedent or consequent alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which inference (modus ponens or modus tollens) is valid given the placement of a perpetual condition in a conditional syllogism and justify why the other is invalid.

Core Idea
The placement of a perpetual condition determines which logical inference is valid.

In conditional syllogisms, the scope of a constant condition like 'always' affects the validity of the two standard inference patterns: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). This passage from the logic text explains how attaching the perpetual condition to only the antecedent or only the consequent changes the possibilities. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly handling temporal or modal conditions in arguments from Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the baseline
Recognize that when the condition of perpetuity is not attached to either the antecedent or the consequent, both modus ponens and modus tollens are valid.
2
Perpetuity in antecedent
Consider the case where the perpetual condition is attached to the antecedent only. The author gives the example: 'Whenever it is perpetual that every A is B, then every C is D'. Note that 'perpetual' qualifies the entire antecedent proposition.
3
Test modus ponens and modus tollens
According to the text, from this conditional, excluding the same antecedent (affirming it) yields a conclusion, but excluding the contradictory of the consequent (denying the consequent) does not. So only modus ponens works.
The author states: 'it does not yield by excluding the contradictory of the consequent'.
4
Perpetuity in consequent
Now consider the condition attached to the consequent only. Both inferences work: modus ponens works for the same reason as before, and modus tollens works because denying a perpetual consequent (i.e., asserting its perpetual negation) contradicts the absolute affirmation of the antecedent.
5
Understand the asymmetry
Placement of the perpetual condition in the antecedent blocks modus tollens: the conditional claims that whenever the antecedent holds perpetually, the consequent holds absolutely. But if the consequent does not hold, it does not necessarily mean the antecedent does not hold perpetually; it might hold non-perpetually. So denial fails. When the condition is in the consequent, denying its perpetual truth forces the antecedent to be false absolutely, so modus tollens is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إذا لم يكن معتبراً لا في المقدم ولا في التالي. فأما إذا كان معتبراً في المقدم دون التالي كما في قولنا كلَّما كان دائمًا كل «أب» فكل «ج د» فإنه ينتج باستثناء عين المقدم لكنه لا يكون منتجاً باستثناء نقيض التالي لما مر . وإن كان معتبراً في التالي دون المقدم فإنه ينتج باستثناء كل واحد منهما إما من جانب المقدم فلما مرَّ ، وإما من جانب التالي فلأن السلب الدائم منافي الإيجاب المطلق؛ فيكون منافيًا لطبيعة المقدم.
This is if it is not considered in the antecedent nor in the consequent. As for if it is considered in the antecedent without the consequent, as in our saying: 'Whenever it is perpetual, every A is B so every C is D', then it yields by excluding the same antecedent but it does not yield by excluding the contradictory of the consequent for the reason that passed. And if it is considered in the consequent without the antecedent, then it yields by excluding each one of them: either from the side of the antecedent for what passed, or from the side of the consequent because the perpetual negation is in opposition to absolute affirmation; thus it is in opposition to the nature of the antecedent.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage sets out the rules for conditionals where a condition of perpetuity (دوام) is explicitly added either to the antecedent or to the consequent. The author first states a baseline: when the condition of perpetuity is not considered at all (neither in antecedent nor consequent), standard rules apply. Then he examines two cases: (1) If the condition of perpetuity is attached only to the antecedent (e.g., 'Whenever it is perpetual that every A is B, then every C is D'), then the syllogism yields a conclusion only by affirming the antecedent (modus ponens), not by denying the consequent (modus tollens). (2) If the condition is attached only to the consequent, then both modus ponens and modus tollens work. The reason given for the latter is that a perpetual negation (the opposite of the consequent if it were perpetual) contradicts an absolute affirmation (the antecedent), so denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent. This is a technical nuance in quantifying temporal conditions in conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
دائم
اعتبار
سلب دائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conditional where the condition of perpetuity is attached only to the antecedent, which inference is valid?
A) Both affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent.
B) Only affirming the antecedent.
C) Only denying the consequent.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The validity of modus ponens and modus tollens in a conditional with a perpetual condition depends on whether the condition attaches to the antecedent or the consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Validity of Partially Quantified Conditionals
Dispute on validity of partially quantified conditional inferences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a partially quantified conditional yields a valid inference and identify the author's ruling on its productivity.

Core Idea
Partially quantified conditionals do yield conclusions.

This passage addresses a key dispute in conditional logic: whether a conditional with a partially quantified antecedent (e.g., 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D') can still produce a valid inference when the antecedent is affirmed. Some scholars argued it cannot, but the author defends its validity with careful reasoning about quantification and exclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed position
Some scholars claim partially quantified conditionals never produce a conclusion because the antecedent's quantification is partial, similar to indefinite conditionals. Others claim they do, based on a response about temporal unity. The author rejects both extreme positions.
2
Extract the author's ruling
The author states the truth is that partially quantified conditionals do yield conclusions. The same rules for universality and perpetuity apply as for universal conditionals.
3
Analyze the objection about the ambiguity of 'some'
An opponent argues that excluding a partially quantified antecedent does not guarantee the consequent because the 'some' in the exclusion might refer to a different individual than the 'some' in the antecedent. The author rebuffs this, insisting the exclusion is of the same antecedent, otherwise it would not be a true exclusion.
4
Understand the general principle
The author summarizes: for any conditional (indefinite or partial, with partial or universal antecedent), the inference is valid: existence of antecedent implies existence of consequent, and non-existence of consequent implies non-existence of antecedent. The exclusion must be properly restricted to the same antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت المحصورة جزئية فمنهم من زعم أنه لا يكون منتجا في هذه الصورة أصلا لما مرَّ من قبل في المهملة، ومنهم من زعم أنه يكون منتجا لما مرَّ من قبل في الجواب عن قولهم إن الاتحاد في الزمان من اللوازم»، وذلك متعذر في الجزئية. والحق أنه يكون منتجا وحكمها في كلية المقدم والتالي واعتبار الدوام فيهما هو الحكم في المحصورة الكلية كما تقدم. ثم من الناس من زعم أن استثناء عين المقدم لا ينتج عين التالي إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة جزئيا كما في قولنا كلما كان بعض «أب» فـ «ج د»، فأما إذا قلنا لكن بعض «أب» فلا يلزم منه ج د فإنه يمكن أن يكون البعض من الألف الذي في الاستثناء غير البعض من الألف الذي كان موضوع الباء في المقدم. غير أنه فاسد وقد مرّ من الكلام فيه من قبل ولأن الكلام في استثناء المقدم ولو كان كما ذكرتم فلا يكون قولنا لكن بعض أب» استثناء عين المقدم بل استثناء شيء يمكن أن يكون عين المقدم ولا يلزم من استثناء بل هذا الشيء استثناء عين المقدم فإذن لا يلزم منه عين التالي. وبالجملة فالشرطية إذا كانت مهملة أو جزئية والمقدم في كل واحدة منهما جزئياً أو كليا : فإنه منتج يلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم، ومن عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم يمكن من اللوازم أن يقيد عند الاستثناء بقيد يفهم منه استثناء عين المقدم وهو الملزوم، أو يفهم منه استثناء نقيض التالي وهو اللازم. ثمَّ المقدم إذا كان كثير الأجزاء فاستثناء جزء من تلك الأجزاء لا يكون منتجا؛ فإنه لا يلزم منه استثناء عين المقدم على خلاف ما إذا كان التالي كثير الأجزاء فإنه يلزم من استثناء نقيض جزء من تلك الأجزاء استثناء نقيض التالي ضرورة انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء جزء من أجزائه. ولقائل أن يقول كيف هو وقد كان استثناء البعض من أجزاء المقدم منتجا في كثير من الصور كما إذا كان البعض مستلزما للكل مثلًا فيقال كلَّما كان الحيوان الناطق موجودًا كان الحسّاس موجودا ، لكن الناطق موجود فيكون الحساس موجودا. لكنا نقول: إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان لازم لازم الشيء لازما للشيء.
And as for when the quantified [conditional] is partial, there are those who assert that it does not yield [a conclusion] in this form at all for what passed before regarding the indefinite, and there are those who assert that it yields [a conclusion] for what passed before in the response to their saying 'unification in time is among the necessary concomitants', and that is impossible in the partial. And the truth is that it yields [a conclusion] and its ruling regarding the universality of the antecedent and consequent and the consideration of perpetuity in them is the ruling for the universally quantified conditional as previously stated. Then among people there are those who claim that excluding the same antecedent does not yield the same consequent if the antecedent of the conditional is partial, as in our saying: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D'; for if we say 'but some A is B', it does not necessitate C is D because it is possible that the 'some' from the A that is in the exclusion is other than the 'some' from the A that was the subject of the B in the antecedent. However, it is invalid, and discussion has passed on it before, and because the discourse is about excluding the antecedent, and if it were as you mentioned, then our saying 'but some A is B' would not be an exclusion of the same antecedent but an exclusion of a thing that could be the same antecedent, and from excluding such a thing the same consequent is not necessitated. In summary, the conditional, if it is indefinite or partial, and the antecedent in each of them is partial or universal, then it yields [a conclusion]; from the existence of the necessitating (malzum) the existence of the necessary (lazim) follows, and from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessitating follows. It is possible that among the necessary concomitants one may restrict at the time of exclusion by a restriction that implies excluding the same antecedent, which is the necessitating, or implies excluding the contradictory of the consequent, which is the necessary. Then, if the antecedent has many parts, excluding a part of those parts does not yield [a conclusion]; for from it the exclusion of the same antecedent does not follow, unlike if the consequent has many parts, for from excluding the contradictory of a part of those parts the exclusion of the contradictory of the consequent follows necessarily because the whole is negated by the negation of a part of its parts. And an objector may say: How is that, while excluding some of the parts of the antecedent yields [a conclusion] in many forms, as when the part is necessarily entailing the whole, for example it is said: 'Whenever the rational animal exists, the sentient exists, but the rational exists, therefore the sentient exists.' But we say: That is only so if the necessary of the necessary of a thing is necessary for the thing.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage covers two related issues: (1) the validity of inferring the consequent from the antecedent when the conditional is partially quantified (جزئية), and (2) the effect of the antecedent or consequent having multiple parts (كثير الأجزاء). The author begins by noting that for partially quantified conditionals, some scholars claim they never yield a conclusion (like indefinite conditionals), while others claim they do, based on an earlier response about the necessity of temporal unity. The author affirms the correct view: they do yield conclusions, and the same rules of perpetuity apply as for universally quantified conditionals. Then he addresses a specific objection: if the antecedent is partial (e.g., 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D'), excluding the antecedent ('but some A is B') does not guarantee the consequent because the 'some' in the exclusion might refer to a different individual than the 'some' in the antecedent. The author rejects this objection, arguing that the expression 'some A is B' when used as the exclusion of the same antecedent should be understood as referring to the same individual; otherwise, it would not be an exclusion of the same antecedent. Then he gives a general principle: for any conditional (indefinite or partial, with partial or universal antecedent), the inference holds: from the existence of the necessitating (malzum) the necessary (lazim) follows, and from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessitating follows, provided the exclusion is properly restricted to the same antecedent. Finally, the author addresses cases where the antecedent has many parts: excluding one part does not amount to excluding the entire antecedent, so no conclusion follows; but if the consequent has many parts, excluding one part's contradictory does exclude the entire consequent's contradictory (since the whole is negated if any part is negated). An objection offers a counterexample where a part entails the whole (e.g., rational animal necessarily entails sentient because rational animal is a part of the definition of sentient?), but the author clarifies that this works only if the part's necessary is also necessary for the whole—i.e., if the part's necessary is a transitive property.

Key Terms
جزئية
ملزوم
لازم
مهملة

A) They never yield a conclusion.
B) They yield a conclusion only if the antecedent is universal.
C) They yield a conclusion, and the rules of universality and perpetuity apply as for universal conditionals.
D) They yield a conclusion, but only if the consequent is also partial.

Key Takeaway
Partially quantified conditionals do yield valid inferences, provided the exclusion correctly refers to the same antecedent.

Inferences from Multi-Part Antecedents and Consequents
Rules for inferences from multi-part antecedents and consequents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether excluding a part of a multi-part antecedent or consequent yields a valid inference in a conditional syllogism, and identify the exception when the part entails the whole.

Core Idea
Excluding part of a multi-part antecedent is invalid; excluding part of consequent's contradictory is valid.

This passage extends the discussion to conditionals where the antecedent or consequent is compound (كثير الأجزاء). The author presents an asymmetry: if the antecedent has many parts, excluding one part does not amount to negating the whole antecedent, so no conclusion follows. But if the consequent has many parts, negating one part's contradictory does negate the whole consequent. An objection raises a seeming counterexample where excluding a part of the antecedent does yield the consequent, but the author clarifies the condition of transitivity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule for multi-part antecedent
If the antecedent has many parts (e.g., a conjunction of conditions), excluding one part does not exclude the entire antecedent. Therefore, the consequent cannot be inferred.
2
Understand the rule for multi-part consequent
If the consequent has many parts (e.g., a conjunction of results), then negating the contradictory of one part negates the whole consequent. So from 'not (part of consequent)' you can infer 'not (whole consequent)'.
3
Analyze the objection and the author's response
An objector points to cases where excluding a part of the antecedent does yield the consequent, e.g., 'Whenever the rational animal exists, the sentient exists' and excluding 'the rational exists' yields 'the sentient exists'. The author responds that this works only if the necessary of the part is also necessary for the whole (transitivity).
4
Apply the distinction to new examples
Practice identifying whether an inference from a compound conditional is valid based on whether the part excluded is from the antecedent or consequent, and whether the part entails the whole.

وأما إذا كانت المحصورة جزئية فمنهم من زعم أنه لا يكون منتجا في هذه الصورة أصلا لما مرَّ من قبل في المهملة، ومنهم من زعم أنه يكون منتجا لما مرَّ من قبل في الجواب عن قولهم إن الاتحاد في الزمان من اللوازم»، وذلك متعذر في الجزئية. والحق أنه يكون منتجا وحكمها في كلية المقدم والتالي واعتبار الدوام فيهما هو الحكم في المحصورة الكلية كما تقدم. ثم من الناس من زعم أن استثناء عين المقدم لا ينتج عين التالي إذا كان مقدم المتّصلة جزئيا كما في قولنا كلما كان بعض «أب» فـ «ج د»، فأما إذا قلنا لكن بعض «أب» فلا يلزم منه ج د فإنه يمكن أن يكون البعض من الألف الذي في الاستثناء غير البعض من الألف الذي كان موضوع الباء في المقدم. غير أنه فاسد وقد مرّ من الكلام فيه من قبل ولأن الكلام في استثناء المقدم ولو كان كما ذكرتم فلا يكون قولنا لكن بعض أب» استثناء عين المقدم بل استثناء شيء يمكن أن يكون عين المقدم ولا يلزم من استثناء بل هذا الشيء استثناء عين المقدم فإذن لا يلزم منه عين التالي. وبالجملة فالشرطية إذا كانت مهملة أو جزئية والمقدم في كل واحدة منهما جزئياً أو كليا : فإنه منتج يلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم، ومن عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم يمكن من اللوازم أن يقيد عند الاستثناء بقيد يفهم منه استثناء عين المقدم وهو الملزوم، أو يفهم منه استثناء نقيض التالي وهو اللازم. ثمَّ المقدم إذا كان كثير الأجزاء فاستثناء جزء من تلك الأجزاء لا يكون منتجا؛ فإنه لا يلزم منه استثناء عين المقدم على خلاف ما إذا كان التالي كثير الأجزاء فإنه يلزم من استثناء نقيض جزء من تلك الأجزاء استثناء نقيض التالي ضرورة انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء جزء من أجزائه. ولقائل أن يقول كيف هو وقد كان استثناء البعض من أجزاء المقدم منتجا في كثير من الصور كما إذا كان البعض مستلزما للكل مثلًا فيقال كلَّما كان الحيوان الناطق موجودًا كان الحسّاس موجودا ، لكن الناطق موجود فيكون الحساس موجودا. لكنا نقول: إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان لازم لازم الشيء لازما للشيء.
And as for when the quantified [conditional] is partial, there are those who assert that it does not yield [a conclusion] in this form at all for what passed before regarding the indefinite, and there are those who assert that it yields [a conclusion] for what passed before in the response to their saying 'unification in time is among the necessary concomitants', and that is impossible in the partial. And the truth is that it yields [a conclusion] and its ruling regarding the universality of the antecedent and consequent and the consideration of perpetuity in them is the ruling for the universally quantified conditional as previously stated. Then among people there are those who claim that excluding the same antecedent does not yield the same consequent if the antecedent of the conditional is partial, as in our saying: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D'; for if we say 'but some A is B', it does not necessitate C is D because it is possible that the 'some' from the A that is in the exclusion is other than the 'some' from the A that was the subject of the B in the antecedent. However, it is invalid, and discussion has passed on it before, and because the discourse is about excluding the antecedent, and if it were as you mentioned, then our saying 'but some A is B' would not be an exclusion of the same antecedent but an exclusion of a thing that could be the same antecedent, and from excluding such a thing the same consequent is not necessitated. In summary, the conditional, if it is indefinite or partial, and the antecedent in each of them is partial or universal, then it yields [a conclusion]; from the existence of the necessitating (malzum) the existence of the necessary (lazim) follows, and from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessitating follows. It is possible that among the necessary concomitants one may restrict at the time of exclusion by a restriction that implies excluding the same antecedent, which is the necessitating, or implies excluding the contradictory of the consequent, which is the necessary. Then, if the antecedent has many parts, excluding a part of those parts does not yield [a conclusion]; for from it the exclusion of the same antecedent does not follow, unlike if the consequent has many parts, for from excluding the contradictory of a part of those parts the exclusion of the contradictory of the consequent follows necessarily because the whole is negated by the negation of a part of its parts. And an objector may say: How is that, while excluding some of the parts of the antecedent yields [a conclusion] in many forms, as when the part is necessarily entailing the whole, for example it is said: 'Whenever the rational animal exists, the sentient exists, but the rational exists, therefore the sentient exists.' But we say: That is only so if the necessary of the necessary of a thing is necessary for the thing.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage covers two related issues: (1) the validity of inferring the consequent from the antecedent when the conditional is partially quantified (جزئية), and (2) the effect of the antecedent or consequent having multiple parts (كثير الأجزاء). The author begins by noting that for partially quantified conditionals, some scholars claim they never yield a conclusion (like indefinite conditionals), while others claim they do, based on an earlier response about the necessity of temporal unity. The author affirms the correct view: they do yield conclusions, and the same rules of perpetuity apply as for universally quantified conditionals. Then he addresses a specific objection: if the antecedent is partial (e.g., 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D'), excluding the antecedent ('but some A is B') does not guarantee the consequent because the 'some' in the exclusion might refer to a different individual than the 'some' in the antecedent. The author rejects this objection, arguing that the expression 'some A is B' when used as the exclusion of the same antecedent should be understood as referring to the same individual; otherwise, it would not be an exclusion of the same antecedent. Then he gives a general principle: for any conditional (indefinite or partial, with partial or universal antecedent), the inference holds: from the existence of the necessitating (malzum) the necessary (lazim) follows, and from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessitating follows, provided the exclusion is properly restricted to the same antecedent. Finally, the author addresses cases where the antecedent has many parts: excluding one part does not amount to excluding the entire antecedent, so no conclusion follows; but if the consequent has many parts, excluding one part's contradictory does exclude the entire consequent's contradictory (since the whole is negated if any part is negated). An objection offers a counterexample where a part entails the whole (e.g., rational animal necessarily entails sentient because rational animal is a part of the definition of sentient?), but the author clarifies that this works only if the part's necessary is also necessary for the whole—i.e., if the part's necessary is a transitive property.

According to the author, what is the rule for a conditional with a multi-part antecedent when a part of the antecedent is excluded?
A) It always yields the same consequent.
B) It never yields the same consequent.
C) It yields the same consequent only if the part is confined to the same time.
D) It yields the same consequent only if the part's necessary is also necessary for the whole.

Key Takeaway
Excluding a part of a multi-part antecedent does not yield the consequent, unless the part's necessary is also necessary for the whole.

The Syllogism

Productivity of Conditional Syllogisms with Particular Antecedents
Author refutes a mistaken view and states the correct rule for conditional syllogisms with particular antecedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a conditional syllogism with a particular antecedent is productive and refute the mistaken view that particularity blocks productivity.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism remains productive even when the antecedent is particular, as long as the exact antecedent is excepted.

In this passage, the author addresses a common error: some logicians think that if the antecedent of a conditional is particular (e.g., 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D'), excepting that antecedent does not yield the consequent. The author dismantles this view and establishes the general principle that productivity depends on the exactness of the exception, not on the universality of the antecedent. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Find the assertion that some people make about conditional syllogisms when the antecedent is particular. What do they claim does not follow?
The claim is that excepting the exact antecedent does not produce the exact consequent in a particular conditional.
2
Examine the example and the supposed counterexample
The author gives an example: 'Whenever some A is B, then C is D.' If we except 'but some A is B', the mistaken view says the consequent does not follow because the 'some' in the exception might refer to a different subset.
The criticism hinges on the possible ambiguity of 'some'.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
The author calls the view corrupt (fasid). He argues that if the exception is of something that could be the exact antecedent, it is not truly an exception of the exact antecedent. The discussion is about excepting the exact antecedent, not a possible version.
The key is that an exception of a possible antecedent does not count as an exception of the exact antecedent.
4
State the author's correct rule
A conditional, whether universal or particular, is productive: from the existence of the malzum (necessary condition) follows the existence of the lazim (necessary), and from non-existence of lazim follows non-existence of malzum. The exception must be qualified to indicate it is the exact malzum or the negation of the lazim.
Productivity does not depend on the quantifier of the antecedent but on the exactness of the exception.
5
Apply the rule to a concrete Islamic example
Consider: 'Whenever some khamr is intoxicating, then it is haram.' If we know 'But some khamr is intoxicating' (exact antecedent), then it follows that some khamr is haram. The particularity of 'some' does not obstruct the inference.
This shows the practical relevance in fiqh where many legal conditions are particular.
