Classification of Conditional Syllogisms by Composition
Author begins a classification of conditional syllogisms by composition of the conditional premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional syllogism according to the composition of its conditional premise (e.g., from two categoricals, two conditionals, etc.).

Core Idea
The diversity of conditional syllogisms springs from the internal structure of the conditional premise.

This passage introduces a systematic classification of conditional syllogisms based on the types of propositions that compose the conditional premise. Understanding this classification is crucial for recognizing the logical form of any conditional argument, especially when analyzing juristic reasoning that uses conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The conditional syllogism always has a conditional premise (muqaddima shartiyya). Locate it and determine whether it is conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila).
2
Determine the composition of the conditional premise
According to the author, the conditional premise can be composed of two categoricals (hamliyyat) or two conditionals (shartiyyat). Identify which type it is.
3
If from categoricals, analyze the antecedent and consequent
Each part (antecedent and consequent) can be particular (shakhsiyya), universal (muhmala or mahsura), quantified (kulliyya or juz'iyya), affirmative or negated, etc. This mirrors the classification of categorical propositions.
4
Recognize the combinatorial explosion
The author emphasizes that due to these many possibilities, conditional syllogisms are 'numerous to the utmost degree.' This shows the systematic approach of Islamic logicians.

Source Text

فصل : [ في أقسام الاستثنائية من جهة التركيب ] والقياس الاستثنائي لما كان مشتملا على مقدِّمة شرطية كان متعددا غاية التعداد لما أن الشرطية قد تكون من حمليتين، وقد يكون من شرطيتين؛ إما متصلتين أو منفصلتين إلى آخر أقسامها التسعة التي مرّ ذكرها. فلو كانت من حمليتين فالمقدم منها قد يكون شخصية من الحمليّات، وقد يكون مهملة وقد يكون محصورة؛ إما كلية أو جزئية. وعلى هذا قد يكون محصلة. وقد يكون معدولة؛ إما معدولة الموضوع أو المحمول، أو المعدولة الموضوع والمحمول موجبة كانت كل واحدة منها أو سالبة. وكذلك التالي فإنه قد يكون شخصية. وقد يكون مهملة إلى آخر ما مر والمجموع منهما ما هو
Section: On the divisions of the conditional [syllogism] from the perspective of composition. And the conditional syllogism, since it includes a conditional premise, is numerous to the utmost degree of numeration, because the conditional [premise] may be from two categoricals, or may be from two conditionals, either conjunctive or disjunctive, up to its nine divisions which have been mentioned. So if it is from two categoricals, then the antecedent from them may be a particular statement among the categoricals, or may be a universal, or may be quantified, either universal or particular. And likewise it may be affirmative, or may be negated, either with a negated subject or predicate, or with a negated subject and predicate, whether each of them is affirmative or negative. And likewise the consequent may be particular, or may be universal (or unquantified), up to what has passed, and the combination of the two is what

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section on the classification of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) based on the internal composition of the conditional premise. The author states that because the conditional premise can be formed from different types of propositions, the syllogism itself is highly diverse. The conditional premise may consist of two categorical propositions, two conditional propositions (either conjunctive or disjunctive), and so on, covering the nine divisions previously discussed. The author then begins to detail the case where the premise is composed of two categoricals: the antecedent can be a particular proposition (shakhsiyya), a universal (muhmala or mahsura), quantified (kulliyya or juz'iyya), and further subdivided by quality and modality (affirmative/negated, with negation applying to subject or predicate or both). The same divisions apply to the consequent. The text cuts off as it begins to mention the combination of the two parts.

Classification of Conditional Syllogisms by Premise Composition
Author classifies conditional syllogisms by premise composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the combinatorial possibilities for the major premise of a conditional syllogism when it is composed of two categoricals or two conditionals.

Core Idea
The conditional premise's composition generates a vast combinatorial tree.

In this lesson, we explore how the major premise (المقدمة الشرطية) of a conditional syllogism (القياس الاستثنائي) can be decomposed. The author shows that the shartiyya can be made of two categoricals (حمليتين) or two conditionals (شرطيتين). Each component can vary in quantification, quality, and modification, leading to many possible forms. Understanding this classification helps in recognizing the diversity of valid conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types of composition
The conditional premise can be composed of (i) two categorical propositions or (ii) two conditional propositions. Note that the latter itself can be connected or disjunctive, giving nine sub-types mentioned earlier.
This is the foundational division.
2
Analyze the variations in the categorical case
For the antecedent and consequent, each can be singular (شخصية), indefinite (مهملة), or quantified (محصورة) such as universal or particular. Also each can be affirmative, negative, or modified (معدولة) in subject, predicate, or both. The total number is the product of the possibilities for antecedent and consequent.
The author says 'ضرب المبلغ في المبلغ' (multiplying the quantity by the quantity).
3
Understand the conditional case expansion
When the premise is composed of two conditionals, the number of possible forms is necessarily double (على الضعف) that of the two-categorical case, because each conditional already has its own combinatorial possibilities.
This is a multiplicative effect.
4
Recognize the significance of this classification
This classification shows the vast number of possible valid conditional syllogisms. In practice, many are not used, but the principle highlights the logical richness.
It also helps avoid false generalizations about conditional arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في أقسام الاستثنائية من جهة التركيب ] والقياس الاستثنائي لما كان مشتملا على مقدِّمة شرطية كان متعددا غاية التعداد لما أن الشرطية قد تكون من حمليتين، وقد يكون من شرطيتين؛ إما متصلتين أو منفصلتين إلى آخر أقسامها التسعة التي مرّ ذكرها. فلو كانت من حمليتين فالمقدم منها قد يكون شخصية من الحمليّات، وقد يكون مهملة وقد يكون محصورة؛ إما كلية أو جزئية. وعلى هذا قد يكون محصلة. وقد يكون معدولة؛ إما معدولة الموضوع أو المحمول، أو المعدولة الموضوع والمحمول موجبة كانت كل واحدة منها أو سالبة. وكذلك التالي فإنه قد يكون شخصية. وقد يكون مهملة إلى آخر ما مر والمجموع منهما ما هو الحاصل من ضرب المبلغ في المبلغ؛ وهذه الشرطية المشتملة على هذه الأعداد قد تكون مخصوصة، وقد تكون مهملة . وقد تكون محصورة كلية كانت أو جزئية. وعلى هذا إذا كانت مركبة من الشرطيتين فإنه على الضعف من هذا بالضرورة.
Section: [On the divisions of the conditional syllogism in terms of composition] The conditional syllogism, since it includes a conditional premise, is multiply varied to the utmost extent, because the conditional premise may be from two categoricals, or may be from two conditionals; either connected or disjunctive, up to its nine divisions that have been mentioned. So if it is from two categoricals, then its antecedent may be singular among categoricals, or may be indefinite, or may be quantified; either universal or particular. And likewise it may be affirmative, or may be modified; either modified in subject or predicate, or modified in subject and predicate, whether each one is affirmative or negative. And similarly the consequent, for it may be singular, or may be indefinite, up to what has passed, and the total of both is the product of multiplying the quantity by the quantity. And this conditional premise that includes these numbers may be particular, or may be indefinite, or may be quantified, universal or particular. And likewise if it is composed of two conditionals, then it is necessarily twice this.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional syllogisms (القياس الاستثنائي) based on the composition of their major premise (المقدمة الشرطية). The author states that since the conditional premise can be composed of two categorical propositions (حمليتين) or two conditional propositions (شرطيتين), which themselves can be connected or disjunctive, and so on, there are numerous possibilities. Specifically: 1) If composed of two categoricals, the antecedent can be singular (شخصية), indefinite (مهملة), or quantified (محصورة) universal or particular, and likewise for the consequent. Moreover, each can be affirmative, negative, or modified (معدولة). The total number is the product of the counts. 2) If composed of two conditionals, the number of forms is necessarily double that of the first case (على الضعف). This classification shows the vast combinatorial possibilities, which is important for understanding the variety of valid conditional syllogisms. For example, in Fiqh, one might have a conditional syllogism: 'If one drinks wine (mugaddam), then one becomes intoxicated (tali). One drank wine, therefore one becomes intoxicated.' This is one of many forms.

Truth Condition of the Connected Conditional
Author explains the necessary condition for a connected conditional proposition to be true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the truth condition of a connected conditional (المتصلة) and apply it to determine the validity of conditional arguments.

Core Idea
A conditional is true exactly when the antecedent cannot be true without the consequent.

In this passage, the author unpacks the logical foundation of conditional propositions. Using a concrete example from astronomy, he shows that a conditional statement like 'if the sun is rising, then daytime exists' is not about whether the two parts are actually true, but about the impossibility of a specific combination. This insight is crucial for evaluating conditional reasoning in Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional example
The author uses 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists' as a sample connected conditional. Notice that it has two parts: the antecedent (sun rising) and the consequent (daytime exists).
2
Understand the two valid inference forms
From the same conditional, the author gives two valid arguments: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). This shows that the conditional licenses both inferences.
3
Extract the truth condition
The author states that the conditional is 'not realized unless either the negation of the antecedent or the consequent itself holds'. This is because the only false case is antecedent true and consequent false, so truth requires avoiding that combination.
Negation of antecedent = 'not sun rising', consequent itself = 'daytime exists'. So the conditional is true if either the sun is not rising or daytime exists.
4
Analyze the composite negation
The author further explains that the truth of the conditional requires the negation of the composite 'existence of antecedent with non-existence of consequent'. This is another way of saying the same condition: it is impossible for the antecedent to be true while the consequent is false.
This composite is the only situation that makes the conditional false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما إذا كانت من حمليتين فيقال مثلا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة فيكون النهار موجودا. أو يقال كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ، لكن النهار ليس بموجود فلا تكون الشمس طالعة؛ فالمتصلة لا تكون متحققة إلا وأن يكون نقيض المقدم أو عين التالي لما أن المقدم لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما كان يتحقق أحدهما. وكذلك التالي لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا أو لا يكون وإنما كان يتحقق أحدهما سواء كان اللزوم من الجانبين أو من جانب واحد. وكما أن أحدهما من صدق المتّصلة فكذلك صدق المتصلة من لوازم أحدهما فإنه لا يتحقق أحدهما إلا وأن لا يتحقق هذا المجموع وهو وجود المقدم مع عدم التالي. وانتفاء هذا المجموع من جملة ما يحقق المتصلة بين المقدم والتالي؛ إذ المقدم مما يمتنع وجوده دون وجود التالي حينئذ وإلا لكان المجموع متحققاً على تقدير أن لا يكون متحققا. وكذلك نقيض التالي فإنه مما يمتنع وجوده دون نقيض المقدم وإلا لكان المجموع متحققاً على تقدير أن لا يكون متحققا . وبالجملة فالمجموع لا يكون منتفيا أي مجموع كان إلا وأن تكون الملازمة متحققة بين أجزاء ذلك المجموع لما أن انتفاء ذلك المجموع هو انتفاء المجموع الذي مرَّ ذكره. وانتفاؤه في نفس الأمر لا يكون إلا بتحقق أحدهما؛ وهو إما نقيض المقدم أو عين التالي في جميع الصور؛ وهذه كلها من اللوازم في القياسات الاستثنائية. ثم المقدمة الشرطية في هذه القياسات قد تكون بيئة اللزوم كما في المثال
As for if it is from two categoricals, it is said for example: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists, but the sun is rising, therefore daytime exists.' Or it is said: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists, but daytime does not exist, therefore the sun is not rising.' So the connected conditional is not realized unless either the negation of the antecedent or the consequent itself [holds], because the antecedent cannot but be either realized or not; and one of the two must be realized. And similarly the consequent cannot but be either realized or not, and only one of the two is realized, whether the implication is from both sides or from one side. And just as one of them [the negation of the antecedent or the consequent] is part of the truth of the connected conditional, so too the truth of the connected conditional is one of the necessary consequences of one of them, for one of them is not realized unless this composite is not realized, namely the existence of the antecedent with the non-existence of the consequent. The negation of this composite is among what realizes the connected conditional between the antecedent and the consequent; because the antecedent is such that its existence is impossible without the existence of the consequent at that time, otherwise the composite would be realized on the assumption that it is not realized. And similarly the negation of the consequent is such that its existence is impossible without the negation of the antecedent, otherwise the composite would be realized on the assumption that it is not realized. In summary, the composite is not negated, whichever composite it is, unless the implication is realized between the parts of that composite, because the negation of that composite is the negation of the composite that was mentioned. And its negation in reality is only by the realization of one of them; and it is either the negation of the antecedent or the consequent itself in all forms. And all these are among the necessary consequences in conditional syllogisms. Then the conditional premise in these syllogisms may be evident in implication (bayyin al-luzum) as in the example.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage explains the truth condition of the connected conditional (المتصلة) using a concrete example from astronomy: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists.' The author gives two valid forms: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). He then explains the underlying logical principle: the connected conditional is true only when either the negation of the antecedent (نقيض المقدم) or the consequent itself (عين التالي) holds. This is because the antecedent and consequent each can be true or false, and the only combination that makes the conditional false is antecedent true and consequent false. Therefore, the truth of the conditional requires that we do not have that combination, which is equivalent to the disjunction 'not antecedent or consequent'. The author emphasizes that this condition is necessary and sufficient: the conditional is true exactly when the composite (existence of antecedent with non-existence of consequent) is negated. He further notes that this negation is realized by the truth of either the negation of the antecedent or the consequent. This is a foundational principle for conditional reasoning. The passage ends by noting that some conditionals have 'evident implication' (بينة اللزوم) like the example given, where the connection is obvious from the meanings.

Key Terms
متصل
نقيض المقدم
عين التالي
بينة اللزوم

D) The antecedent must be false.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a connected conditional reduces to the disjunction 'negation of antecedent or consequent itself', which is equivalent to the impossibility of antecedent true with consequent false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Truth Condition of the Conjunctive Conditional
Author explains the truth condition of conditional premises and the method to establish their implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the truth condition of a conjunctive conditional (muttasila) and apply the method of negating the composite (intifa' al-majmu') to verify an implication in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
A conditional is true only if the antecedent and the opposite of the consequent cannot coexist.

In conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-istithna'i), the validity of the whole argument rests on the truth of the conditional premise. The author now lays down the precise condition that makes a conditional true: it is not enough that both parts are factual; there must be a necessary link such that the antecedent cannot be true while the consequent is false. This condition is captured by the 'negation of the composite' — the impossibility of the antecedent together with the negation of the consequent. In this lesson, we will unpack that condition and see how to demonstrate an implication when it is not self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the truth condition
The author states that a conjunctive conditional (al-muttasila) is not realized unless either the contradictory of the antecedent or the identical of the consequent obtains. This means it is impossible for the antecedent to be true while the consequent is false. The key is the impossibility of the composite (majmu') of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent.
This is the logical definition of material implication, but Islamic logicians emphasize the necessity of the connection (luzum).
2
Identify the composite being negated
The composite (al-majmu') is the conjunction of the antecedent (muqaddam) and the denial of the consequent (tali). Its negation (intifa') means that this conjunction cannot occur in reality. The author says: 'the negation of this composite is among what realizes the conjunctive conditional.'
The composite is also discussed in terms of the contradictory of the consequent: if the consequent is false, its contradictory must entail the contradictory of the antecedent.
3
Apply the method of negation to verify an implication
When the conditional is not self-evident (bayyina al-luzum), its truth must be demonstrated by negating the composite. The author explains that the composite can be of three types: (1) things agreeing in existence, (2) things agreeing in non-existence, or (3) things differing in existence and non-existence. For each case, one shows that the negation of the composite necessarily leads to the implication.
The author provides a detailed reductio argument for the first case (existence to existence).
4
Distinguish the three types of implication
Implication can hold between: existence of a thing and existence of another (as in 'If the sun is shining, then day exists'), between non-existence and non-existence, or between existence and non-existence. The composition of the composite differs accordingly, and the method of negation adapts to each type.
The passage ends while explaining the third case (existence to non-existence), so the reader must infer the pattern from the first two.

Source Text

الحاصل من ضرب المبلغ في المبلغ ؛ وهذه الشرطية المشتملة على هذه الأعداد قد تكون مخصوصة، وقد تكون مهملة . وقد تكون محصورة كلية كانت أو جزئية. وعلى هذا إذا كانت مركبة من الشرطيتين فإنه على الضعف من هذا بالضرورة. أما إذا كانت من حمليتين فيقال مثلا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، لكن الشمس طالعة فيكون النهار موجودا. أو يقال كلَّما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود ، لكن النهار ليس بموجود فلا تكون الشمس طالعة؛ فالمتصلة لا تكون متحققة إلا وأن يكون نقيض المقدم أو عين التالي لما أن المقدم لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا، أو لا يكون؛ وإنما [۲۳۳] كان يتحقق / أحدهما. وكذلك التالي لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا أو لا يكون وإنما كان يتحقق أحدهما سواء كان اللزوم من الجانبين أو من جانب واحد. وكما أن أحدهما من صدق المتّصلة فكذلك صدق المتصلة من لوازم أحدهما فإنه لا يتحقق أحدهما إلا وأن لا يتحقق هذا المجموع وهو وجود المقدم مع عدم التالي. وانتفاء هذا المجموع من جملة ما يحقق المتصلة بين المقدم والتالي؛ إذ المقدم مما يمتنع وجوده دون وجود التالي حينئذ وإلا لكان المجموع متحققاً على تقدير أن لا يكون متحققا. وكذلك نقيض التالي فإنه مما يمتنع وجوده دون نقيض المقدم وإلا لكان المجموع متحققاً على تقدير أن لا يكون متحققا . وبالجملة فالمجموع لا يكون منتفيا أي مجموع كان إلا وأن تكون الملازمة متحققة بين أجزاء ذلك المجموع لما أن انتفاء ذلك المجموع هو انتفاء المجموع الذي مرَّ ذكره. وانتفاؤه في نفس الأمر لا يكون إلا بتحقق أحدهما؛ وهو إما نقيض المقدم أو عين التالي في جميع الصور؛ وهذه كلها من اللوازم في القياسات الاستثنائية.
ثم المقدمة الشرطية في هذه القياسات قد تكون بيئة اللزوم كما في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره، وقد لا تكون فيحتاج القياس إلى أن يبين. وبيانه بانتفاء المجموع في الحقيقية، وذلك المجموع إما مركب من الأشياء المتفقة في الوجود أو من الأشياء المتفقة في العدم أو من الأشياء المختلفة في الوجود والعدم. وذلك لأن اللزوم إما بين وجود الشيء ووجود غيره كما مر أو بين عدم الشيء وعدم غيره أو بين وجود الشيء وعدم غيره فإن كان الأول كان ذلك المجموع مركبًا من الأشياء المختلفة في الوجود والعدم فيقال في انتفائه إن العدم لا يخلو من أن يكون شاملا لهذه الأشياء، أو لا يكون، فإن كان شاملا فظاهر. وإن لم يكن فكذلك ضرورة ارتفاع ذلك العدم إلا لكان الشمول من لوازم وجود ذلك الشيء في الجملة؛ وذلك لا يمكن أو يقال إن الوجود لا يخلو من أن يكون شاملا ، أو لا يكون فإن كان شاملًا [ فظاهر] وإن لم يكن فكذلك ضرورة ارتفاع ذلك الوجود وإلا لكان الشمول من لوازم عدم ذلك الشيء؛ وذلك لا يمكن. وإن كان الثاني فكذلك فإن المجموع مركب من الأشياء المختلفة في الوجود والعدم أيضًا فيمكن أن يقال في انتفائه ما قلناه في الأول. ويمكن أيضًا أن يقال انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء الجزء الأول منه لا غير أخص من انتفائه بجزء ما في التحقق، وقد أمكن انتفاؤه بالجزء الثاني منه كما يمكن انتفاؤه بالجزء الأول. ولما كان أخص منه فلا يمكن أن يكون متحدا معه في التحقق حتى لا يمكن أن يتحقق هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وإذا لم يكن متحدا معه في التحقق وقد تحقق / انتفاؤه بجزء ما عند تحقق انتفائه بالجزء [٢٣٤و] الأول؛ فقد تحقق عند عدم التحقق. وكذلك في الجملة وإلا لكان متحدا معه في التحقق ؛ وليس كذلك. أو يقال انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء الجزء الثاني منه لا غير أخص من انتفائه بجزء ما في التحقق ؛ وقد أمكن انتفاؤه بالجزء الأول منه كما يمكن به انتفاؤه بالجزء الثاني إلى آخر ما مر . وإن كان الثالث كان
The result from multiplying the amount by the amount; and this conditional that contains these numbers may be specific, and may be general. And it may be quantified, universal or particular. And accordingly, if it is composed of two conditionals, then it is necessarily weak from this. As for if it is composed of two categoricals, it is said for example: Whenever the sun is shining, then the day exists, but the sun is shining, so the day exists. Or it is said: Whenever the sun is shining, then the day exists, but the day does not exist, so the sun is not shining. So the conjunctive conditional is not realized unless either the contradictory of the antecedent or the identical of the consequent, because the antecedent cannot but be either realized or not; and [233] only one of them is realized. Likewise, the consequent cannot but be either realized or not, and only one of them is realized, whether the implication is from both sides or from one side. And just as one of them is part of the truth of the conjunctive conditional, so too the truth of the conjunctive conditional is among the necessaries of one of them, because one of them is not realized unless this composite is not realized, which is the existence of the antecedent with the non-existence of the consequent. And the negation of this composite is among what realizes the conjunctive conditional between the antecedent and consequent; for the antecedent is such that its existence without the existence of the consequent is impossible, otherwise the composite would be realized on the assumption that it is not realized. And likewise the contradictory of the consequent, for its existence without the contradictory of the antecedent is impossible, otherwise the composite would be realized on the assumption that it is not realized. In summary, the composite is not negated, any composite it may be, unless the implication is realized between the parts of that composite, because the negation of that composite is the negation of the composite previously mentioned. And its negation in reality is only by the realization of one of them, which is either the contradictory of the antecedent or the identical of the consequent in all forms; and all these are among the necessaries in the conditional syllogisms. Then the conditional premise in these syllogisms may be clear in implication, as in the example that passed, and it may not be, so the syllogism needs to demonstrate it. And its demonstration is by the negation of the composite in reality. And that composite is either composed of things that agree in existence, or of things that agree in non-existence, or of things that differ in existence and non-existence. That is because implication is either between the existence of a thing and the existence of another, as passed, or between the non-existence of a thing and the non-existence of another, or between the existence of a thing and the non-existence of another. If it is the first, then that composite is composed of things that differ in existence and non-existence, and it is said regarding its negation: non-existence cannot but either include these things or not; if it includes them, it is apparent. And if it does not, then similarly by necessity of the removal of that non-existence, otherwise the inclusion would be among the necessities of the existence of that thing in general; and that is impossible. Or it is said: existence cannot but either include them or not; if it includes [it is apparent], and if it does not, then similarly by necessity of the removal of that existence, otherwise the inclusion would be among the necessities of the non-existence of that thing; and that is impossible. And if it is the second, then similarly, for the composite is composed of things that differ in existence and non-existence as well, and it is possible to say regarding its negation what we said in the first. And it is also possible to say: the negation of the composite by the negation of the first part only is more specific than its negation by a certain part in realization, and its negation by the second part is possible just as its negation by the first part is possible. And since it is more specific than it, it cannot be identical with it in realization such that this cannot be realized without that, and vice versa. And if it is not identical with it in realization, and its negation by a certain part has been realized when its negation by the [234w] first part is realized, then it has been realized when there is no realization. And similarly in general, otherwise it would be identical with it in realization; and it is not so. Or it is said: the negation of the composite by the negation of the second part only is more specific than its negation by a certain part in realization; and its negation by the first part is possible just as its negation by the second part is possible, and so on to the end of what has passed. Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of conditional propositions (al-muttasila) and their role in conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). The author first categorizes conditional premises by quantity (specific, general, universal, particular). Then he presents two classic forms of conditional syllogisms: modus ponens (affirming the antecedent) and modus tollens (denying the consequent), using the example of 'If the sun is shining, then day exists'. He emphasizes that a conditional (muttasila) is true only when either the contradictory of the antecedent or the identical of the consequent obtains—meaning that it is impossible for the antecedent to be true and the consequent false. The truth condition is that the conjunction of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent cannot be realized. The author then explains that the negation of this composite (majmu') is what verifies the conditional. He extends this to the contradictory of the consequent: if the consequent is false, its contradictory requires the contradictory of the antecedent. The summary is that any composite's negation implies the implicative relation between its parts. Then he turns to the major premise: some conditionals are self-evident in their implication (bayyina al-luzum), while others require demonstration. The demonstration proceeds by negating the composite in reality. The composite can be composed of items agreeing in existence, agreeing in non-existence, or differing in existence and non-existence. He explains the three cases of implication: between two existences, between two non-existences, or between existence and non-existence. For the first case (existence to existence), the composite consists of things differing in existence and non-existence. To demonstrate the implication, one shows that non-existence either includes all parts or not; if it includes them, the implication is clear; if not, then the inclusion would be necessary but impossible. Similarly, existence either includes them or not. The argument uses a reductio: if the inclusion were not necessary, it would break the implication. The second case (non-existence to non-existence) is analogous, with the composite still differing in existence and non-existence. The author then provides a more detailed argument about specificity: negating the composite by negating only the first part is more specific than negating it by some part, because the second part could also negate it. Since the specific cannot be identical with the more general in realization, the negation by the first part is not the same as negation by some part. The passage ends mid-sentence introducing the third case (existence to non-existence). This explanation is typical of advanced logic texts that rigorously establish the conditions for valid conditional inference.

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a conjunctive conditional (متصلة) to be true, according to the author?
A) Both the antecedent and consequent are true.
B) The antecedent is false or the consequent is true.
C) The composite of the antecedent and the negation of the consequent is impossible.
D) The antecedent and consequent are both necessary.

Key Takeaway
A conditional is true only when the conjunction of its antecedent and the logical opposite of its consequent is impossible; this is the core of implication in conditional syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional proposition from fiqh (e.g., 'If the prayer is valid, then wudu is not required') and test its truth by determining whether the composite of antecedent and negation of consequent is impossible. State your reasoning.
?
In the author's example 'Whenever the sun is shining, then day exists', verify the truth of the conditional using the method of negation of the composite. Explain how you would demonstrate it if it were not self-evident.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Negating the Sum in Syllogistic Necessity
Author analyzes three types of sum negation for syllogistic necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of sum negation required to prove syllogistic necessity based on the nature of the necessary relation (luzum) and analyze the logical steps in the negation procedure.

Core Idea
The negation of the sum reveals necessity through three patterns of existence and non-existence.

This passage dives into the technical core of syllogistic proof by examining how negating the sum (مجموع) of conditions establishes necessity. The author categorizes necessity into three types—existence-existence, non-existence-non-existence, and existence-non-existence—and shows how the composition of the sum changes accordingly. Understanding this classification is essential for verifying that a syllogism's conclusion follows necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of necessity
Determine whether the necessary relation (luzum) is between existence and existence, non-existence and non-existence, or existence and non-existence. This classification drives the structure of the sum.
The author gives three cases: existence-existence, non-existence-non-existence, and existence-non-existence.
2
Classify the sum composition
Based on the necessity type, the sum (majmu') will be composed of things that agree in existence, agree in non-existence, or differ in existence and non-existence. For existence-existence, the sum differs (involves both); for non-existence-non-existence, similar; for existence-non-existence, the sum agrees in one aspect.
For the first two types, the sum is composed of things differing in existence and non-existence; for the third, it is composed of things agreeing in existence or agreeing in non-existence.
3
Apply the negation method
For each sum type, consider whether negation is by a specific part or by the whole. Check for identity conditions: Is the negation by a part identical in actuality to the negation by any part? The author explores these subcases in detail.
The negation methods involve checking if the negation of the sum by a specific part is more specific than by some part, and whether identity in actuality holds.
4
Handle the objection
The author addresses a potential objection that these negation methods undermine primary propositions (awwaliyyat). He responds that the methods apply only to sums that can actually be negated, not absolutely, so the objection fails.
The key is that our discourse is about what can be negated in actual fact, not about absolute negation.

فيقال في انتفائه إن العدم لا يخلو من أن يكون شاملا لهذه الأشياء، أو لا يكون، فإن كان شاملا فظاهر. وإن لم يكن فكذلك ضرورة ارتفاع ذلك العدم إلا لكان الشمول من لوازم وجود ذلك الشيء في الجملة؛ وذلك لا يمكن أو يقال إن الوجود لا يخلو من أن يكون شاملا ، أو لا يكون فإن كان شاملًا فظاهر وإن لم يكن فكذلك ضرورة ارتفاع ذلك الوجود وإلا لكان الشمول من لوازم عدم ذلك الشيء؛ وذلك لا يمكن. وإن كان الثاني فكذلك فإن المجموع مركب من الأشياء المختلفة في الوجود والعدم أيضًا فيمكن أن يقال في انتفائه ما قلناه في الأول. ويمكن أيضًا أن يقال انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء الجزء الأول منه لا غير أخص من انتفائه بجزء ما في التحقق، وقد أمكن انتفاؤه بالجزء الثاني منه كما يمكن انتفاؤه بالجزء الأول. ولما كان أخص منه فلا يمكن أن يكون متحدا معه في التحقق حتى لا يمكن أن يتحقق هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وإذا لم يكن متحدا معه في التحقق وقد تحقق انتفاؤه بجزء ما عند تحقق انتفائه بالجزء الأول؛ فقد تحقق عند عدم التحقق. وكذلك في الجملة وإلا لكان متحدا معه في التحقق ؛ وليس كذلك. أو يقال انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء الجزء الثاني منه لا الأشياء المتفقة في العدم فيمكن أن يقال في انتفاء كل واحد منهما ما قلناه في الثاني. ويمكن أن يقال انتفاء المجموع بانتفاء الجزء الأول لا غير؛ إما أن يكون متحدا مع انتفائه بجزء ما على الإطلاق، أو لا أو بالكل منه. ولو كان كذلك لكان المحال اللازم على
As previously mentioned, and sometimes it is not, so the syllogism needs to be explained. Its explanation is by the negation of the sum in the real [proposition]. That sum is either composed of things that agree in existence, or of things that agree in non-existence, or of things that differ in existence and non-existence. That is because necessity is either between the existence of a thing and the existence of another, as previously, or between the non-existence of a thing and the non-existence of another, or between the existence of a thing and the non-existence of another. If it is the first, then that sum is composed of things that differ in existence and non-existence. It is said in its negation: non-existence is either inclusive of these things or not; if it is inclusive, it is obvious. If not, similarly by necessity of the removal of that non-existence, unless the inclusion were a concomitant of the existence of that thing in general; that is not possible. Or it is said: existence is either inclusive or not; if it is inclusive, it is obvious; if not, similarly by necessity of the removal of that existence, unless the inclusion were a concomitant of the non-existence of that thing; that is not possible. If it is the second, then similarly, for the sum is composed of things that differ in existence and non-existence as well. It can be said regarding its negation what we said in the first. It can also be said: the negation of the sum by the negation of the first part only is more specific than its negation by some part in actuality, and its negation by the second part is possible just as its negation by the first part is possible. Since it is more specific than it, it cannot be identical with it in actuality, such that this cannot occur without that, and vice versa. If it is not identical with it in actuality, and its negation by some part has occurred at the time of its negation by the first part, then it has occurred at the time of non-occurrence. And similarly in general, unless it would be identical with it in actuality; it is not so. Or it is said: the negation of the sum by the negation of the second part only is more specific than its negation by some part in actuality, and its negation by the first part is possible just as its negation by the second part is possible, and so on as before. If it is the third, then that sum is composed of things that agree in existence or of things that agree in non-existence. It can be said regarding the negation of each of them what we said in the second. It can also be said: the negation of the sum by the negation of the first part only — either it is identical with its negation by some part absolutely, or not; if it is not identical, it is obvious. If it is identical, then similarly, it is not possible that the negation of the sum by the negation of the second part only be identical with its negation by some part absolutely, otherwise its negation by the negation of the first part only would be identical with its negation by the negation of the second part only; that is not possible. It can also be said: the negation of some parts without others is either contrary to the identity between it and that which necessitates the negation of the sum, or not; if it is contrary, it is obvious. If not, similarly, then necessarily that thing occurs at that time. That is because the identity between them in actuality is either actual at that time, or not: if it is not actual, then that thing has occurred as previously. If it is actual, then that thing has occurred similarly, otherwise the negation of some parts without others would be contrary to the identity between them; the assumption is otherwise. The best approach in all is to say: upon the assumption of the negation of that which necessitates the negation of the sum, if it is negated on that assumption, it is negated in actual fact, whether that assumption is actual in fact or not. For if you say: the negation of the sum by these methods is among what undermines primary propositions, we say: it would be so only if each of these methods was universal in every sum that can be negated in actual fact, and not possible otherwise; it is not so. For our discourse is not about the negation of the sum absolutely, but about what can be negated in actual fact: either by some parts or by the whole. If it were so, then the impossible would be necessary.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of how to prove the necessity of a syllogism by negating the sum (majmu') in a real proposition. The author first states that sometimes the syllogism requires explanation, and the explanation comes through negating the sum. He then classifies the sum into three types based on the nature of the necessity (luzum): between existence and existence, non-existence and non-existence, or existence and non-existence. For each type, he methodically explores the ways negation can be performed, often splitting into subcases (e.g., whether the negation of the sum by a specific part is more specific than by any part, leading to considerations of identity in actuality). He also addresses a potential objection that this method undermines primary propositions, arguing that the method only applies to sums that can actually be negated, not absolutely. The reasoning is highly technical, aimed at establishing rigorous conditions for syllogistic necessity.

Key Takeaway
The negation of the sum in a real proposition requires identifying the type of necessity and its corresponding sum composition, then applying the appropriate negation method, always considering actuality.

General Application of Negation Methods to All Conditionals
Author extends the previous analysis of negation to all types of conditional propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the negation method (modus tollens) to any type of conditional proposition—whether composed of categoricals, conjunctive conditionals, or disjunctive conditionals—and identify the resulting syllogistic form.

Core Idea
Negation of the consequent works the same for all conditional types, not just simple ones.

The author has previously analyzed negation methods for conditionals composed of two categoricals. Now he shows that these same methods apply universally, whether the conditional is made of categoricals, conjunctive conditionals (muttaṣila), or disjunctive conditionals (munfaṣila). He provides detailed examples to illustrate how modus tollens functions in each case, and notes that the second premise need not be categorical. Understanding this extension is crucial for handling complex logical arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of conditionals discussed
The author lists three types: composed of two categoricals (ḥamliyyāt), two conjunctive conditionals (muttaṣilatayn), and two disjunctive conditionals (munfaṣilatayn). Note the Arabic terms.
2
Extract the common logical form
In each case, the reasoning follows modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. Here P and Q are themselves conditional propositions. Recognize that the method of negating the consequent is invariant.
3
Analyze the example for conjunctive conditionals
The author gives: 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists; so whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not shining.' This is contrapositive. Then he reformulates it as a conditional syllogism: 'Whenever this conditional is actualized, that conditional is actualized; but this conditional is actualized; so that conditional is actualized' (modus ponens) or 'but that conditional is not actualized; so this conditional is not actualized' (modus tollens).
4
Examine the example with two conjunctive conditionals
He gives: 'If whenever man exists, actual motion exists, then whenever actual motion does not exist, man does not exist; but it is not the case that whenever actual motion does not exist, man does not exist; therefore, it is not the case that whenever man exists, actual motion exists.' This is modus tollens where antecedent and consequent are themselves universal conditionals.
5
Examine the example with two disjunctive conditionals
Similarly: 'If every body is either moving or at rest, then some substance is either moving or at rest; but it is not the case that some substance is either moving or at rest; therefore, it is not the case that every body is either moving or at rest.' Again modus tollens.
The author notes that the second premise need not be categorical; it could be a conditional itself.

Then verification by these methods and the like is not specific to the conditional composed of two categoricals as in our saying 'Whenever the sun is shining then daytime exists,' but rather is general encompassing all, whether it is composed of two categoricals as before or of two conditionals (muttaṣila) as in our saying 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists, so whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not shining' and this is equivalent to our saying 'Whenever this conditional is actualized, that conditional is actualized, but this conditional is actualized, so that conditional is actualized' or we say 'but that conditional is not actualized, so this conditional is not actualized.' And this is truly the first syllogism because at that time the conditional is composed of two categoricals. Then the conditional among the implications in this syllogism: either it is among the implications in the minor premise specifically, or in the major—that is not necessary. As for the second premise, it is not necessary that it be categorical because of the possibility that the composite of two conditionals is affirmative and negative as in our saying 'If whenever man exists, actual motion exists, then whenever actual motion does not exist, man does not exist, but it is not at all the case that whenever actual motion does not exist, man does not exist, so it is not at all the case that whenever man exists, actual motion exists.' This is when the conditional is composed of two conditionals (muttaṣilatayn). As for when it is composed of two disjunctives (munfaṣilatayn), that is like our saying 'If it is the case that every body is either moving or at rest, then some substance is either moving or at rest, but it is not at all the case that some substance is either moving or at rest, so it is not the case that every body is either moving or at rest.' And this is equivalent to our saying 'Whenever this disjunctive is actualized, that disjunctive is actualized, but that disjunctive is not actualized, so this disjunctive is not actualized.'
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage extends the previous analysis of negation methods to all types of conditional propositions. The author first states that these methods are not restricted to conditionals made of two categoricals (حمليتين), such as 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists.' They apply generally, whether the conditional is composed of two categoricals, two conjunctive conditionals (متصلتين), or two disjunctive conditionals (منفصلتين). He then provides an example of a conjunctive conditional: 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists; therefore, whenever daytime does not exist, the sun is not shining.' This is equivalent to saying that if the first conditional (the one about sun and daytime) is true, then the second (its contrapositive) is also true. The syllogism here is a true first-figure syllogism because the conditional in it is composed of two categoricals. He then discusses the implications: the conditional proposition in this syllogism may be an implication in the minor premise specifically or in the major, but that distinction is not necessary. He further notes that the second premise need not be categorical, as the composite could involve two conditionals that are affirmative and negative. His example: 'If whenever man exists, actual motion exists, then whenever actual motion does not exist, man does not exist; but it is not the case that whenever actual motion does not exist, man does not exist; therefore, it is not the case that whenever man exists, actual motion exists.' This is a modus tollens form with conditionals. Finally, he addresses disjunctive conditionals: 'If it is the case that every body is either moving or at rest, then some substance is either moving or at rest; but it is not the case that some substance is either moving or at rest; therefore, it is not the case that every body is either moving or at rest.' This again is a modus tollens form but with disjunctives. In all cases, the method is the same: the negation of the consequent leads to the negation of the antecedent.

Key Takeaway
The method of negating the consequent (modus tollens) works universally for all conditional propositions, whether they are composed of categoricals, conjunctive conditionals, or disjunctive conditionals.

Basic Conditional Syllogism from Two Categoricals
Author introduces basic conditional syllogism form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a conditional syllogism composed of two categorical propositions and reduce it to its underlying categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Every conditional syllogism hides a categorical core.

The author begins with the simplest conditional: two categorical propositions linked by a conditional connective. This forms the foundation for all more complex conditionals. Understanding this basic form is essential for analyzing all other types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Locate the conditional statement: 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists.' It consists of two categorical parts: 'the sun is rising' and 'day exists'.
2
Recognize the minor premise
The syllogism may have either an affirmation of the antecedent (sun is rising) or a denial of the consequent (day does not exist).
The passage shows both modus ponens and modus tollens.
3
Apply the conditional rule
If the antecedent is true, the consequent follows (modus ponens). If the consequent is false, the antecedent is false (modus tollens).
These are the two valid forms of conditional inference.
4
Reduce to categorical syllogism
Convert the conditional 'Whenever A, B' into the categorical 'All A are B'. Then the reasoning becomes: All A are B, this is A, therefore this is B (Barbara).
This is what the author calls 'the first syllogism in reality'.

And similarly if it is the reverse.

Commentary
This passage is a classification and reduction of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the method of verification applies not only to conditionals composed of two categoricals but to all types. He then gives the basic example of a conditional with two categoricals: 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists' and its contrapositive. He then shows how a conditional composed of two conditionals reduces to the basic form: if a conditional implies another conditional, and the first is true, the second follows; or if the second is false, the first is false. This is called the first syllogism in reality because the inner conditionals are composed of categoricals. Next, he considers the case where the conditional is composed of two conditionals that are affirmative and negative, giving the example with human and moving. Then he treats the composition from two disjunctives, showing how it also reduces to a conditional form. After that, he discusses mixed compositions: categorical with conditional, categorical with disjunctive, and conditional with disjunctive, each with an example. Throughout, he emphasizes that all these forms are essentially reducible to the first syllogism. The examples are from natural philosophy (sun/day), ontology (human/moving), logic (body moving/resting), arithmetic (number/even-odd), and accident (number/accident). The author intends to show the universality of the syllogistic form.

حمليتين
القياس الأول بالحقيقة

The Syllogism
intermediate

Conditional from Two Conditionals
Author analyzes conditional composed of two conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a conditional syllogism whose main premise is composed of two conditionals to a simple conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
A conditional of conditionals is reducible to a single conditional through the first syllogism.

The author extends the reduction method to conditionals where the antecedent and consequent are themselves conditionals. The reasoning still follows modus ponens/tollens, but the inner conditionals are analyzed as categoricals. This demonstrates that all conditional forms are ultimately under one framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conditional
Find the compound conditional: 'If (whenever man exists, the actually moving exists) then (whenever the actually moving does not exist, man does not exist)'.
The antecedent and consequent are each conditionals.
2
Analyze inner conditionals as categoricals
Convert each inner conditional to a universal categorical: 'whenever man exists, the actually moving exists' becomes 'All times man exists are times the actually moving exists'.
This is the key step to apply the first syllogism.
3
Apply the conditional rule
The syllogism uses modus tollens: the consequent (whenever the actually moving does not exist, man does not exist) is denied, so the antecedent is denied.
The minor premise states the consequent is false.
4
Reduce to the first syllogism
The whole argument is equivalent to: If categorical A then categorical B; but not B; therefore not A. This is the basic categorical form.
The author calls this the 'first syllogism in reality'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

طالعة فالنهار موجود بل يكون عاما يعم الكل سواء كانت مركبة من حمليتين كما مر أو من شرطيتين متصلتين كما في قولنا كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا فكلما لم يكن النهار موجودا لم يكن الشمس طالعة / مركبة من الحمليتين حينئذ. ثم الشرطية من اللوازم في هذا القياس فإما أن يكون من اللوازم في الصغرى على التعيين ، أو في الكبرى فذلك غير لازم. وأما المقدمة الثانية فلا يلزم أن تكون حملية لاحتمال أن يكون المركب من الشرطيتين موجبة وسالبة كما في قولنا إن كان كلما كان الإنسان موجودا كان المتحرك بالفعل موجودًا فكلما لم يكن المتحرك بالفعل موجودًا لم يكن الإنسان موجودا، لكن ليس البتة كلما لم يكن المتحرك بالفعل موجودًا لم يكن الإنسان موجودا فليس البتة كلما كان الإنسان موجودًا كان المتحرك بالفعل موجودًا : هذا إذا كانت الشرطية فيه مركبة من المتّصلتين. فأما إذا كانت مركبة من المنفصلتين فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كل جسم إما متحرّكًا وإما ساكنا كان بعض الجوهر إما متحركًا وإما ساكنا، لكن ليس بعض الجوهر البتة إما متحرّكًا وإما ساكنا فليس كل جسم إما متحركًا وإما ساكنا، وإنه في قوة قولنا كلما كانت هذه المنفصلة متحققة كانت تلك المنفصلة متحققة لكن تلك المنفصلة ليست بمتحققة فكذلك هذه. وأما إذا كانت الشرطية فيه مركبة من الحملية والمتصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا، لكن طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا . وكذلك إذا كان على العكس كما إذا كانت مركبة منفصلة وحملية. وأما إذا كانت مركبة من الحملية والمنفصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان هذا عددًا فهو إما زوج وإما فرد لكنه عدد فهو إما زوج وإما فرد. وكذلك إذا كان على العكس. وإن كانت مركبة من المتصلة والمنفصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلَّما كان هذا عددًا كان عرضًا فإما أن لا يكون هذا عددًا وإما أن يكون عرضًا، لكنه كلما كان هذا عددًا كان عرضًا فهو إذن إما أن لا يكون عددًا وإما أن يكون عرضًا مانعا من الخلوّ ، وعلى هذا إذا كان على العكس.
The estimation in what we mentioned is necessary upon a possible estimation; and that is impossible, unlike what is necessary upon an impossible estimation. Then verification by these ways and the like is not specific to the conditional composed of two categoricals, as in our saying 'Whenever the sun is rising, then day exists', but rather it is general, encompassing all, whether it is composed of two categoricals as has passed, or of two conditionals as in our saying 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists, and whenever day does not exist, the sun is not rising' — and it is in the power of our saying 'Whenever this conditional is true, that conditional is true; but this is true, so likewise the conditional', or we say 'But that conditional is not true, so likewise this'; and this is the first syllogism in reality because the conditional in it is composed of two categoricals at that time. Then the conditional from the necessary consequences in this syllogism: either it is from the necessary consequences in the minor premise specifically, or in the major premise, and that is not necessary. As for the second premise, it is not necessary that it be categorical, because it is possible that the compound from two conditionals be affirmative and negative as in our saying: 'If whenever man exists, the actually moving exists, then whenever the actually moving does not exist, man does not exist; but not at all whenever the actually moving does not exist, man does not exist; therefore not at all whenever man exists, the actually moving exists.' This is when the conditional in it is composed of two conditionals. As for if it is composed of two disjunctives, that is like our saying: 'If every body is either moving or resting, then some substance is either moving or resting; but some substance is not at all either moving or resting; so not every body is either moving or resting.' And it is in the power of our saying 'Whenever this disjunctive is true, that disjunctive is true; but that disjunctive is not true; so likewise this.' As for if the conditional in it is composed of categorical and conditional, that is like our saying: 'If sunrise is a cause for the existence of day, then whenever the sun is rising, day exists; but sunrise is a cause for the existence of day; so whenever the sun is rising, day exists.' And similarly if it is the reverse, as if it is composed of disjunctive and categorical. As for if it is composed of categorical and disjunctive, that is like our saying: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd; but it is a number; so it is either even or odd.' And similarly if it is the reverse. And if it is composed of conditional and disjunctive, that is like our saying: 'If whenever this is a number, it is an accident, then either it is not a number or it is an accident; but whenever this is a number, it is an accident; therefore it is either not a number or it is an accident, exclusive of emptiness.' And similarly if it is the reverse.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a classification and reduction of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the method of verification applies not only to conditionals composed of two categoricals but to all types. He then gives the basic example of a conditional with two categoricals: 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists' and its contrapositive. He then shows how a conditional composed of two conditionals reduces to the basic form: if a conditional implies another conditional, and the first is true, the second follows; or if the second is false, the first is false. This is called the first syllogism in reality because the inner conditionals are composed of categoricals. Next, he considers the case where the conditional is composed of two conditionals that are affirmative and negative, giving the example with human and moving. Then he treats the composition from two disjunctives, showing how it also reduces to a conditional form. After that, he discusses mixed compositions: categorical with conditional, categorical with disjunctive, and conditional with disjunctive, each with an example. Throughout, he emphasizes that all these forms are essentially reducible to the first syllogism. The examples are from natural philosophy (sun/day), ontology (human/moving), logic (body moving/resting), arithmetic (number/even-odd), and accident (number/accident). The author intends to show the universality of the syllogistic form.

Key Terms
شرطية
متصلتين
القياس الأول بالحقيقة

A) It must be categorical.

C) It is not necessary that it be categorical.
D) It must be negative.

Key Takeaway
A conditional composed of two conditionals reduces to the first syllogism, demonstrating the universality of the categorical core.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Conditional from Two Disjunctives
Author analyzes conditional composed of two disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a conditional syllogism composed of two disjunctive premises to a simple conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogisms are also reducible to the basic conditional form.

The author now treats conditionals where both parts are disjunctive (either-or) propositions. By showing that these also reduce to the same 'first syllogism', he demonstrates that all types of conditionals are variations of a single logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall conditional
Find the compound: 'If every body is either moving or resting, then some substance is either moving or resting.' The antecedent and consequent are disjunctive propositions.
Disjunctives are propositions of the form 'either A or B'.
2
Recognize the disjunctive propositions
'Every body is either moving or resting' is a universal disjunctive; 'some substance is either moving or resting' is a particular disjunctive.
Disjunctives can be exclusive or inclusive; here they seem inclusive.
3
Apply the conditional rule (modus tollens)
The minor premise denies the consequent: 'some substance is not at all either moving or resting.' Therefore, the antecedent is denied: 'not every body is either moving or resting'.
This is the same modus tollens form as before.
4
Reduce to the first syllogism
The inner disjunctives can be analyzed as categoricals: 'Every body is either moving or resting' is equivalent to 'All bodies are either-moving-or-resting'. Thus the argument reduces to categorical logic.
The author emphasizes this by stating the argument is 'in the power of' a simple conditional.

The Syllogism

Mixed Conditional Compositions and Universal Reduction
Author enumerates mixed conditional compositions and their reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reduce any mixed conditional syllogism (categorical+conditional, categorical+disjunctive, conditional+disjunctive) to the basic form.

Core Idea
All conditional syllogisms, regardless of their internal composition, are variations of the first syllogism.

The author completes his survey by examining mixed compositions: categorical with conditional, categorical with disjunctive, and conditional with disjunctive. Each example shows that the underlying logical structure is the same modus ponens or tollens, reducible to categorical logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of composition
For each mixed type, note which components are categorical, conditional, or disjunctive. For example, 'If sunrise is a cause for day (categorical), then whenever the sun is rising, day exists (conditional).'
The author gives examples for each combination.
2
Determine the inference form
In the categorical+conditional example, the minor affirms the antecedent ('sunrise is a cause'), so modus ponens yields the consequent. In others, it may be modus tollens or different.
The form depends on which part is affirmed or denied.
3
Reduce the non-categorical part to categorical terms
Conditionals are reducible to categoricals as before; disjunctives can be treated as categoricals by considering the whole disjunction as a predicate.
This allows the whole argument to be expressed as a categorical syllogism.
4
Recognize the universal applicability
The author concludes by showing that even the most complex conditional (conditional+disjunctive) reduces to the same pattern. This proves that the method is general, not limited to simple forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is in the power of our saying 'Whenever this disjunctive is true, that disjunctive is true; but that disjunctive is not true; so likewise this.' As for if the conditional in it is composed of categorical and conditional, that is like our saying: 'If sunrise is a cause for the existence of day, then whenever the sun is rising, day exists; but sunrise is a cause for the existence of day; so whenever the sun is rising, day exists.' And similarly if it is the reverse, as if it is composed of disjunctive and categorical. As for if it is composed of categorical and disjunctive, that is like our saying: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd; but it is a number; so it is either even or odd.' And similarly if it is the reverse. And if it is composed of conditional and disjunctive, that is like our saying: 'If whenever this is a number, it is an accident, then either it is not a number or it is an accident; but whenever this is a number, it is an accident; therefore it is either not a number or it is an accident, exclusive of emptiness.' And similarly if it is the reverse.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a classification and reduction of conditional syllogisms. The author begins by stating that the method of verification applies not only to conditionals composed of two categoricals but to all types. He then gives the basic example of a conditional with two categoricals: 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists' and its contrapositive. He then shows how a conditional composed of two conditionals reduces to the basic form: if a conditional implies another conditional, and the first is true, the second follows; or if the second is false, the first is false. This is called the first syllogism in reality because the inner conditionals are composed of categoricals. Next, he considers the case where the conditional is composed of two conditionals that are affirmative and negative, giving the example with human and moving. Then he treats the composition from two disjunctives, showing how it also reduces to a conditional form. After that, he discusses mixed compositions: categorical with conditional, categorical with disjunctive, and conditional with disjunctive, each with an example. Throughout, he emphasizes that all these forms are essentially reducible to the first syllogism. The examples are from natural philosophy (sun/day), ontology (human/moving), logic (body moving/resting), arithmetic (number/even-odd), and accident (number/accident). The author intends to show the universality of the syllogistic form.

Key Terms
حمليتين
متصلتين
منفصلتين
شرطية

A) Modus ponens
B) Modus tollens

Key Takeaway
All conditional syllogisms, whether pure or mixed, reduce to the first syllogism, confirming the universality of the categorical foundation.

Compound Conditional Syllogisms in Demonstration
Author explains how to form compound conditional syllogisms from categorical and conditional premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a compound conditional syllogism that combines a categorical, conditional, or disjunctive premise with a conditional major premise.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism can have a categorical, conditional, or disjunctive minor premise, yielding a valid conclusion via modus ponens.

The author demonstrates how compound conditional syllogisms are formed by mixing premise types. This is not a new rule but an extension of modus ponens to complex consequents. Understanding these composites prepares you for advanced demonstrations (burhan) used in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise is a conditional (shartiyya) that may contain another conditional or disjunction in its consequent. In the first example, the major is: 'If the sunrise is the cause of daytime, then whenever the sun shines, daytime exists.'
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise can be a categorical (hamliyya), a conditional (muttasila), or a disjunctive (munfasila). In the first example, the minor is the categorical 'the sunrise is the cause of daytime' – affirming the antecedent.
3
Apply modus ponens
Since the minor affirms the antecedent, the conclusion is the consequent. In the first example, the consequent is itself a conditional: 'whenever the sun shines, daytime exists'. The syllogism is valid.
This works even when the consequent is complex.
4
Check the other composite forms
The second example uses a categorical major and disjunctive minor: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd; but it is a number; therefore it is either even or odd.' The third uses a conditional major and disjunctive minor: 'If whenever this is a number it is an accident, then either it is not a number or it is an accident; but whenever this is a number it is an accident; therefore it is either not a number or an accident.' Both follow the same pattern.
Ensure the minor corresponds exactly to the antecedent as stated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا كانت الشرطية فيه مركبة من الحملية والمتصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا، لكن طلوع الشمس علة لوجود النهار فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودًا . وكذلك إذا كان على العكس كما إذا كانت مركبة منفصلة وحملية. وأما إذا كانت مركبة من الحملية والمنفصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان هذا عددًا فهو إما زوج وإما فرد لكنه عدد فهو إما زوج وإما فرد. وكذلك إذا كان على العكس. وإن كانت مركبة من المتصلة والمنفصلة فذلك مثل قولنا إن كان كلَّما كان هذا عددًا كان عرضًا فإما أن لا يكون هذا عددًا وإما أن يكون عرضًا، لكنه كلما كان هذا عددًا كان عرضًا فهو إذن إما أن لا يكون عددًا وإما أن يكون عرضًا مانعا من الخلوّ ، وعلى هذا إذا كان على العكس.
And as for when the conditional premise in it is composed of the categorical and the conditional, that is like our saying: 'If the sunrise is the cause of the existence of daytime, then whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists; but the sunrise is the cause of the existence of daytime, therefore whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists.' Likewise if it is reversed, as when it is composed of a disjunctive and a categorical. And as for when it is composed of the categorical and the disjunctive, that is like our saying: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd; but it is a number, so it is either even or odd.' Likewise if it is reversed. And if it is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive, that is like our saying: 'If whenever this is a number it is an accident, then either this is not a number or it is an accident; but whenever this is a number it is an accident, so it is therefore either not a number or an accident, excluding both emptiness.' And likewise if it is reversed.

Commentary
This passage explains how to construct compound conditional syllogisms by combining different types of premises. The author demonstrates three composite forms: (1) categorical with conditional (mutasila), (2) categorical with disjunctive (munfasila), and (3) conditional with disjunctive. Each is illustrated with an example and its reversed counterpart (i.e., swapping the major and minor premises or changing the order). The first example uses a natural causality: sunrise causes daytime. The second uses numerical classification (even/odd). The third uses the example that if a number is always an accident, then the disjunctive conclusion follows. The key point is that the compound premise maintains the logical form of a conditional syllogism, where the minor premise is a conditional or disjunctive statement and the major premise posits or denies a part. The scholar is demonstrating how these composite forms are valid and useful in demonstrations (burhan).

The Principle of Antagonism and Implication
Author states the logical principle that a thing's contradictory is implied by its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship between logical implication and contradiction and apply it to evaluate everyday and theological arguments.

Core Idea
Contradiction and implication are two sides of the same logical coin: each is impossible without the other.

Logic teaches us that nothing can both exist and not exist at the same time. But how do we prove such a deep truth? The author reveals a fundamental principle: two things are mutually exclusive (mutanāfī) only if each implies the negation of the other. This principle is not merely abstract; it underpins the way we reason about Allah's attributes, the validity of proofs in kalām, and even the faulty logic we hear in everyday arguments. Let us unpack this elegant idea step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core link
The author states: a thing is not contradictory to another unless it implies what contradicts that other. This means contradiction (antagonism) and implication are inseparable. If A and B are contradictory, then A implies not-B. If it didn't, A could coexist with not-B, violating the principle of non-contradiction.
Contradictories are pairs like existence and non-existence.
2
Justify the impossibility of coexistence
If A implies not-B, then if both A and B existed, A would imply not-B, so B and not-B would coexist – impossible. Thus, the link between implication and contradiction prevents logical inconsistency.
3
Trace the infinite chain
Every existent is either an implicans (malzūm) or an implicatum (lāzim) of another. Since existence itself is an implicans of everything, the chain of implications is infinite. Consequently, the number of contradictions is also infinite, each corresponding to a step in this chain.
This echoes the principle that the world is contingent and requires a necessary being.
4
Reflect on common usage
People often say: 'If you had ability, you would have done it; you lacked ability, so you didn't do it.' This is an invalid exceptive syllogism (denying the antecedent). However, if ability and the act are necessarily linked (mutual implication), then the conclusion becomes valid. The author uses this to show how hidden assumptions affect logical form.
The invalid form becomes valid if the major premise is a biconditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول إن الشيء لا يكون منافيًا للشيء إلا وأن يكون مستلزما لما يناقضه. ولا يكون مستلزما للشيء أيضًا إلا وأن يكون منافيا لما يناقضه وإلا يلزم أن يمكن الاجتماع بين الشيء ونقيضه: وذلك لا يمكن فلا شيء إلا وأن يكون بينه وبين غير لازمه لأنه إما أن يكون ملزوما للغير أو لازما للغير. ولا يلزم أن يكون ذلك الغير شيئًا واحدًا؛ ولا نهاية لملزومات الشيء ولوازمه لما كان ملزومية كل موجود ملزوما لوجود هذا الشيء أي شيء كان. وملزومية هذه الملزومية من جملة ما له وجود أيضًا. ولما كانت الملزومات غير متناهية كانت اللوازم كذلك فإن الشيء لا يكون ملزوما للشيء إلا وأن يكون عدمه لازما لعدم ذلك الشيء. وعلى حسب هذا التعدد يلزم تعدد المنافات بين الشيء وغيره لما مرَّ ، وعلى حسب تعدد كل واحدة منهما بعدد القياسات الاستثنائية؛ وإنها مستعملة بين جميع الناس وأكثر استعمالات العامة على وجه يوهم أن لا يكون منتجا كما يقال مثلا لو قدرت على ذلك الأمر لفعلت ذلك كما ينبغي، لكن ما قدرت والاستثناء في مثل هذا القياس هو استثناء نقيض المقدم. وإنه لا ينتج كما مر غير أنهم إنما يستعملونه لبيان أن الامتناع عين ذلك الفعل لعدم القدرة؛ وذلك لا يحصل إلا وأن يكون ذلك الفعل مع القدرة عليه أمران متلازمان فيكون استعمالهم على اعتقاد أنه كذلك. ولو كان كذلك لكان ذلك الاستثناء مناسبًا للمطلوب، ومنتجًا إياه، فاعتبر بما عرفت.
If you know this, then we say: a thing is not contradictory to another thing unless it implies what contradicts it. And it does not imply a thing either unless it is contradictory to what contradicts it; otherwise it would be necessary that coexistence between a thing and its contradictory be possible. That is impossible. So nothing exists unless there is between it and that which is not its necessary implication; because it is either an implicans of another or an implicatum of another. And it is not necessary that that other be one thing. And there is no end to the implicantia of a thing and its implicata because the implicancy of every existent is an implicans of the existence of this thing, whatever it is. And the implicancy of this implicancy is also among that which has existence. And since the implicantia are infinite, the implicata are likewise. For a thing is not an implicans of another unless its non-existence is an implicatum of the non-existence of that other. And in proportion to this multiplicity, there follows a multiplicity of contradictions between a thing and its other, as previously mentioned. And in proportion to the multiplicity of each of them, [there are] the number of exceptive syllogisms. And they are used among all people, and most uses of the common folk are in a way that gives the illusion that it does not produce [a conclusion], as for example it is said: 'If you had power over that matter, you would have done it properly, but you did not have power.' And the exception in such a syllogism is the exception of the contradictory of the antecedent. And it does not produce [a conclusion] as previously mentioned; but they only use it to explain that the impossibility is identical to that act due to lack of ability. And that does not obtain unless that act together with the ability over it are two mutually implicating matters. So their use is based on the belief that it is so. And if it were so, that exception would be appropriate for the desired conclusion, and productive of it. So consider what you have known.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental logical principle: For two things to be contradictory (mutanāfi), one must imply the negation of the other. The author argues that if a thing implies something without being contradictory to its opponent, then the thing and its own contradictory could coexist, which is impossible. Therefore, every thing stands in a relation of either being an implicans (mulzum) or an implicatum (lāzim) with respect to others. The author then explores the infinite regress: since every existent implies another, and the implication chain has no end, the number of implications is infinite. Correspondingly, the number of contradictions is also infinite, and each corresponds to an exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī). The author notes that common people often use such syllogisms in a way that seems non-productive, such as saying, 'If you had the ability, you would have done it; but you did not have the ability.' This is an invalid form (denying the antecedent), but people use it to show that the impossibility of the act is due to lack of ability, which assumes a necessary connection between ability and the act. If that assumption holds, then the syllogism becomes productive. The author urges the reader to reflect on this.

Key Terms
مناف
مستلزم
ملزوم
لازم

Key Takeaway
Every logical opposition implies a chain of necessary implications, and vice versa.
