Classifying the Disjunctive Syllogism
Author classifies the istithna'i syllogism into muttasil and munfasil, then divides munfasil into real exclusive disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a disjunctive syllogism according to whether the disjunction is real (exclusive) or unreal, and whether it has two parts or more.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogisms require careful classification of the disjunction.

In this lesson, we examine how logicians classify the disjunctive syllogism (qiyas munfasil). The key distinction is between real exclusive disjunctions (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) and unreal ones. Within real disjunctions, we further distinguish two-part from multi-part, and within two-part, contradictory vs non-contradictory. This parsing exercise helps you recognize the structure of arguments found in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the family of conditional syllogisms
The author notes that the exceptive syllogism (istithna'i) is also called conditional (sharti) because it contains a conditional premise. This covers both conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or) types.
'Sharti' here is a broader category than in some later texts.
2
Distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive
The conditional syllogism can be muttasil (conjunctive) where the minor premise is conditional, and munfasil (disjunctive) where the minor premise is disjunctive. This passage focuses on the disjunctive type.
3
Sort disjunctions by real vs unreal
Real (haqiqiyya) disjunctions are those that prevent both conjunction (coexistence) and emptiness (simultaneous absence) — i.e., exclusive 'or'. Unreal ones are non-exclusive (inclusive 'or').
The Arabic term is mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw.
4
Divide real disjunctions by number of parts
Real disjunctions can have two parts (juz'ayn) or many parts. Two-part disjunctions are the simplest and most common.
5
For two-part real disjunctions, check contradictoriness
If the two parts differ by affirmation and negation (e.g., 'A' and 'not-A'), they are contradictory. If not (e.g., 'even' and 'odd'), they are non-contradictory but still exclusive.
This distinction affects the logic of the syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن القياس الاستثنائي يسمى شرطيا ] وأما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه يسمى شرطيا لما مر من قبل أنه مشتمل على مقدمة. والقياس الشرطي قد يكون متصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه متّصلة، وقد يكون منفصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه منفصلة. والمنفصلة على أقسام متعددة؛ وذلك التعدد على حسب التعدد في المنفصلات حقيقية كانت المنفصلات أو غير حقيقية. أما الحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو فإنها إما أن تكون ذات جزأين، أو ذات أجزاء كثيرة وما تكون ذات جزأين فإما أن يكون جزءاه مختلفين بالسلب والإيجاب على سبيل التناقض، وإما أن يكونا مختلفين لا على سبيل التناقض . أما الأول وهو الذي تكون ذات جزأين فذلك مثل قولنا هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا فاستثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر فتكون النتيجة هي بالمعنى نفس الاستثناء كما في قولنا لكنه كذا ينتج فإذن ليس لا كذا وهذا ليس شيئًا أعرف من الاستثناء الذي كان جزء قياس. وكذلك استثناء نقيض أيهما كان كما في قولنا لكنه ليس لا كذا ينتج فهو إذن كذا؛ وهذا الاستثناء أيضًا لا يكون بعيدا من أن لا يكون أعرف من النتيجة وأسبق إلى الذهن وإنما ينتفع بذلك في أكثر الأمر من قياسات مؤلفة من متّصلة ومنفصلة كما في قولهم هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا، فإن لم يكن كذا فـ (ج) د ، لكن ليس (ج) د فهو إذن كذا لكن هذا القياس
Section: On that the exceptive syllogism is called conditional. As for the exceptive syllogism, it is called conditional because, as previously mentioned, it contains a premise. The conditional syllogism may be conjunctive (muttasil), which is that whose minor premise is conjunctive, and it may be disjunctive (munfasil), which is that whose minor premise is disjunctive. The disjunctive has multiple divisions; and that multiplicity is according to the multiplicity among the disjunctions, whether the disjunctions are real or unreal. As for the real (haqiqiyya), which is that which prevents both conjunction and emptiness (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), it is either of two parts or of many parts. And that which is of two parts: either its two parts differ by negation and affirmation in the manner of contradiction, or they differ not in the manner of contradiction. As for the first, which is of two parts, that is like our saying: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such'; then positing either one of them produces the negation of the other, so the conclusion is in meaning the same as the premise, as in our saying: 'But it is such-and-such' produces 'Therefore it is not not such-and-such', and this is nothing more known than the premise that was part of the syllogism. Likewise, positing the negation of either one produces the other, as in our saying: 'But it is not not such-and-such' produces 'Therefore it is such-and-such'. And this exception also is not far from being more known than the conclusion and more prior to the mind. And benefit is derived from that mostly in syllogisms composed of conjunctive and disjunctive [premises], as in their saying: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such; if it is not such-and-such, then (ج) is d; but (ج) is not d; therefore it is such-and-such.' But this syllogism...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This section opens with a heading clarifying why the exceptive syllogism is called 'conditional' (sharti): because it contains a conditional premise. The author then distinguishes two types: the conjunctive (muttasil) where the minor premise is a conditional 'if...then...', and the disjunctive (munfasil) where the minor premise is a disjunction. The focus then turns to the disjunctive type. The author divides disjunctions into real (haqiqiyya) and unreal. The real disjunction is the exclusive one (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), which either has two parts or many parts. For two-part exclusive disjunctions, there are two subcases: (a) the parts are contradictory (e.g., 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such'), and (b) the parts are not contradictory (e.g., 'This is either even or odd'). In case (a), positing one part directly yields the negation of the other, and vice versa. The conclusion is as evident as the premise. The author remarks that such immediate inferences are used in compound syllogisms combining conjunctive and disjunctive premises. An example is given: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such; if it is not such-and-such, then (ج) is d; but (ج) is not d; therefore it is such-and-such.' The text breaks off mid-example.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
شرطي
مانعة الجمع والخلو
جزءين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, a real disjunction is called:
A) mani'at al-jam'
B) mani'at al-khuluw
C) mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw
D) mani'at al-jam' aw al-khuluw

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive syllogism is classified by the kind of disjunction: real (exclusive), unreal, two-part, many-part, contradictory or not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify the number of parts and whether the parts are contradictory in the statement: 'The action is either obligatory or prohibited.'

The Syllogism
intermediate

Immediate Inference in Contradictory Disjunctions
Author explains the immediate inference in two-part contradictory disjunctions and its use in compound syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion of a disjunctive syllogism when the parts are contradictory and one part is affirmed or denied.

Core Idea
In a contradictory disjunction, affirming one part negates the other.

This lesson focuses on the simplest form of disjunctive syllogism: when the two parts are contradictory, like 'A or not A'. The passage shows that positing either part directly yields the negation of the other. This immediate inference is self-evident and forms the building block for more complex compound syllogisms used in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Recognize a two-part contradictory disjunction
Look for a disjunction where the parts are direct opposites, e.g., 'This is B or not B'. The parts differ by affirmation and negation.
Example from the text: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such'.
2
Affirm one part and derive the conclusion
If you affirm one part (e.g., 'It is B'), then you conclude that the other part is false: 'It is not not-B'. The conclusion is the negation of the other part.
The author says: 'positing either one of them produces the negation of the other'.
3
Deny one part and derive the conclusion
If you deny one part (e.g., 'It is not not-B'), then you affirm the other: 'It is B'. This is the inverse.
Example from text: 'But it is not not such-and-such' produces 'Therefore it is such-and-such'.
4
Observe the self-evident nature of the inference
The author notes that the conclusion is as known as the premise; it is not epistemically prior. This inference is used in compound syllogisms, as in the example: 'This is either A or not A; if not A then C is D; but C is not D; therefore A.'
This combination shows how disjunctive and conditional premises work together.

فصل : [ في أن القياس الاستثنائي يسمى شرطيا ] وأما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه يسمى شرطيا لما مر من قبل أنه مشتمل على مقدمة. والقياس الشرطي قد يكون متصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه متّصلة، وقد يكون منفصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه منفصلة. والمنفصلة على أقسام متعددة؛ وذلك التعدد على حسب التعدد في المنفصلات حقيقية كانت المنفصلات أو غير حقيقية. أما الحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو فإنها إما أن تكون ذات جزأين، أو ذات أجزاء كثيرة وما تكون ذات جزأين فإما أن يكون جزءاه مختلفين بالسلب والإيجاب على سبيل التناقض، وإما أن يكونا مختلفين لا على سبيل التناقض . أما الأول وهو الذي تكون ذات جزأين فذلك مثل قولنا هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا فاستثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر فتكون النتيجة هي بالمعنى نفس الاستثناء كما في قولنا لكنه كذا ينتج فإذن ليس لا كذا وهذا ليس شيئًا أعرف من الاستثناء الذي كان جزء قياس. وكذلك استثناء نقيض أيهما كان كما في قولنا لكنه ليس لا كذا ينتج فهو إذن كذا؛ وهذا الاستثناء أيضًا لا يكون بعيدا من أن لا يكون أعرف من النتيجة وأسبق إلى الذهن وإنما ينتفع بذلك في أكثر الأمر من قياسات مؤلفة من متّصلة ومنفصلة كما في قولهم هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا، فإن لم يكن كذا فـ (ج) د ، لكن ليس (ج) د فهو إذن كذا لكن هذا القياس
Section: On that the exceptive syllogism is called conditional. As for the exceptive syllogism, it is called conditional because, as previously mentioned, it contains a premise. The conditional syllogism may be conjunctive (muttasil), which is that whose minor premise is conjunctive, and it may be disjunctive (munfasil), which is that whose minor premise is disjunctive. The disjunctive has multiple divisions; and that multiplicity is according to the multiplicity among the disjunctions, whether the disjunctions are real or unreal. As for the real (haqiqiyya), which is that which prevents both conjunction and emptiness (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), it is either of two parts or of many parts. And that which is of two parts: either its two parts differ by negation and affirmation in the manner of contradiction, or they differ not in the manner of contradiction. As for the first, which is of two parts, that is like our saying: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such'; then positing either one of them produces the negation of the other, so the conclusion is in meaning the same as the premise, as in our saying: 'But it is such-and-such' produces 'Therefore it is not not such-and-such', and this is nothing more known than the premise that was part of the syllogism. Likewise, positing the negation of either one produces the other, as in our saying: 'But it is not not such-and-such' produces 'Therefore it is such-and-such'. And this exception also is not far from being more known than the conclusion and more prior to the mind. And benefit is derived from that mostly in syllogisms composed of conjunctive and disjunctive [premises], as in their saying: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such; if it is not such-and-such, then (ج) is d; but (ج) is not d; therefore it is such-and-such.' But this syllogism...

Commentary
This section opens with a heading clarifying why the exceptive syllogism is called 'conditional' (sharti): because it contains a conditional premise. The author then distinguishes two types: the conjunctive (muttasil) where the minor premise is a conditional 'if...then...', and the disjunctive (munfasil) where the minor premise is a disjunction. The focus then turns to the disjunctive type. The author divides disjunctions into real (haqiqiyya) and unreal. The real disjunction is the exclusive one (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), which either has two parts or many parts. For two-part exclusive disjunctions, there are two subcases: (a) the parts are contradictory (e.g., 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such'), and (b) the parts are not contradictory (e.g., 'This is either even or odd'). In case (a), positing one part directly yields the negation of the other, and vice versa. The conclusion is as evident as the premise. The author remarks that such immediate inferences are used in compound syllogisms combining conjunctive and disjunctive premises. An example is given: 'This is either such-and-such or not such-and-such; if it is not such-and-such, then (ج) is d; but (ج) is not d; therefore it is such-and-such.' The text breaks off mid-example.

The Syllogism

Definition and Types of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines conditional syllogism and lists its two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conjunctive and disjunctive conditional syllogisms and identify their key components.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms derive conclusions by excluding a part of a conditional premise.

This lesson introduces the conditional syllogism, its name, and its two main types: conjunctive and disjunctive. You will see how the minor premise determines the type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
In a conditional syllogism, the major premise is a conditional statement (either conjunctive or disjunctive). Locate that premise in the Arabic text.
The passage says the syllogism is called conditional because it contains a premise.
2
Determine if the minor premise is conjunctive or disjunctive
The author states: the conditional syllogism may be conjunctive (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil) depending on the minor premise. Check the nature of the minor premise.
3
Understand the role of the minor premise
In a conjunctive conditional, the minor premise affirms or denies part of the consequent; in a disjunctive, it excludes one part.
This is the heart of the istithna' operation.
4
Apply to an example
Consider: 'If it is a number, it is either even or odd. It is even. Therefore it is not odd.' This is a disjunctive conditional syllogism.
More on this in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن القياس الاستثنائي يسمى شرطيا ] وأما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه يسمى شرطيا لما مر من قبل أنه مشتمل على مقدمة. والقياس الشرطي قد يكون متصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه متّصلة، وقد يكون منفصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه منفصلة. والمنفصلة على أقسام متعددة؛ وذلك التعدد على حسب التعدد في المنفصلات حقيقية كانت المنفصلات أو غير حقيقية. أما الحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو فإنها إما أن تكون ذات جزأين، أو ذات أجزاء كثيرة وما تكون ذات جزأين فإما أن يكون جزءاه مختلفين بالسلب والإيجاب على سبيل التناقض، وإما أن يكونا مختلفين لا على سبيل التناقض . أما الأول وهو الذي تكون ذات جزأين فذلك مثل قولنا هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا فاستثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر فتكون النتيجة هي بالمعنى نفس الاستثناء كما في قولنا لكنه كذا ينتج فإذن ليس لا كذا وهذا ليس شيئًا أعرف من الاستثناء الذي كان جزء قياس. وكذلك استثناء نقيض أيهما كان كما في قولنا لكنه ليس لا كذا ينتج فهو إذن كذا؛ وهذا الاستثناء أيضًا لا يكون بعيدا من أن لا يكون أعرف من النتيجة وأسبق إلى الذهن وإنما ينتفع بذلك في أكثر الأمر من قياسات مؤلفة من متّصلة ومنفصلة كما في قولهم هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا، فإن لم يكن كذا فـ (ج) د ، لكن ليس (ج) د فهو إذن كذا لكن هذا القياس أيضا مما يتم بشرطية متصلة وحدها. ولا يبعد أن لا يحتاج فيها إلى المنفصلة بوجه فإذن هذا القسم من المنفصلات لا ينفع استعمالها كثير نفع بل يجب أن يكون الجزءان غير متقابلين على هذا الوجه كما في قولنا إن كان هذا عددًا فهو إما زوج وإما فرد؛ فإن استثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر كما يقال إنه زوج ينتج إنه ليس بفرد فكذلك استثناء تقيض أيهما كان فإنه ينتج عين الآخر كما يقال إنه ليس بزوج فينتج أنه فرد مثلا.
Section: [On that the assertoric syllogism is called conditional] As for the assertoric syllogism, it is called conditional because, as previously mentioned, it contains a premise. The conditional syllogism may be conjunctive, whose minor premise is conjunctive, and may be disjunctive, whose minor premise is disjunctive. The disjunctive premise has multiple divisions; that multiplicity is according to the multiplicity in disjunctions, whether they are true disjunctions or not. As for the true disjunction, which prevents both union and void, it is either of two parts or of many parts. What is of two parts: either its two parts differ by negation and affirmation in the way of contradiction, or they differ not in the way of contradiction. As for the first, which is of two parts, that is like our saying: 'This is either such-and-such or it is not such-and-such.' So the exclusion of exactly one of them yields the contradictory of the other, so the result is, in meaning, the same as the excluded part, as in our saying: 'But it is such-and-such' yields 'Therefore it is not not such-and-such.' And this is nothing more known than the excluded part which was a part of the syllogism. Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of either of them, as in our saying: 'But it is not not such-and-such' yields 'Therefore it is such-and-such.' And this exclusion too is not far from being more known than the result and more precedent in the mind. Rather, one benefits from that mostly in syllogisms composed of conjunctive and disjunctive [premises], as in their saying: 'This is either such-and-such or it is not such-and-such. If it is not such-and-such, then (j) d. But (j) d is not. Therefore it is such-and-such.' But this syllogism also is completed by a conjunctive conditional alone. And it is not unlikely that one does not need the disjunctive at all. So this section of disjunctions is not of much benefit; rather, the two parts must be not opposed in this way, as in our saying: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.' For the exclusion of exactly one of them yields the contradictory of the other, as is said: 'It is even' yields 'It is not odd.' Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of either of them yields the other exactly, as is said: 'It is not even' yields 'It is odd,' for example.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and explains its two types: conjunctive and disjunctive. The author focuses on the true disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) which prevents both union and void (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww). This is subdivided into those with two parts and those with many parts. For two-part disjunctions, the parts may be contradictory (e.g., 'either A or not A') or non-contradictory (e.g., 'either even or odd'). The author argues that the contradictory type is not useful because the same result can be obtained through a conjunctive conditional alone, and the exclusion is no more informative than the premise. Therefore, the useful two-part disjunction is the non-contradictory one. An example from arithmetic is given: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.' In this case, excluding one part yields the contradictory of the other (e.g., 'it is even' yields 'it is not odd'), and excluding the contradictory of one part yields the other part (e.g., 'it is not even' yields 'it is odd'). This establishes the inference pattern for two-part disjunctive syllogisms.

القياس الشرطي
متصلة
منفصلة

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms are classified by their minor premise: conjunctive or disjunctive.

True Disjunction and Its Subdivisions
Author explains true disjunction and its two-part types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a true disjunction and classify it by number of parts and the relationship between parts.

Core Idea
A true disjunction excludes both conjunction and void; its parts can be contradictory or non-contradictory.

After defining conditional syllogisms, the author explores disjunctive premises in detail. A true disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) prevents both parts being true together and both false. This lesson breaks down its subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Define true disjunction
A true disjunction is one that prevents both the union (jam') of the parts and the void (khuluww) — i.e., parts cannot both be true and cannot both be false.
This is the standard definition after al-Farabi.
2
Determine the number of parts
The author says a true disjunction is either of two parts (dhāt juz'ayn) or of many parts. Identify which.
He focuses on two-part disjunctions.
3
Examine the relationship between parts
For a two-part disjunction, the parts may be different by negation and affirmation (i.e., contradictory, like A and not A) or different in another way (non-contradictory, like even and odd).
This distinction is crucial for utility.
4
Apply to an example
Consider: 'This number is either even or odd.' This is a true disjunction with two non-contradictory parts.

Section: [On that the assertoric syllogism is called conditional] As for the assertoric syllogism, it is called conditional because, as previously mentioned, it contains a premise. The conditional syllogism may be conjunctive, whose minor premise is conjunctive, and may be disjunctive, whose minor premise is disjunctive. The disjunctive premise has multiple divisions; that multiplicity is according to the multiplicity in disjunctions, whether they are true disjunctions or not. As for the true disjunction, which prevents both union and void, it is either of two parts or of many parts. What is of two parts: either its two parts differ by negation and affirmation in the way of contradiction, or they differ not in the way of contradiction. As for the first, which is of two parts, that is like our saying: 'This is either such-and-such or it is not such-and-such.' So the exclusion of exactly one of them yields the contradictory of the other, so the result is, in meaning, the same as the excluded part, as in our saying: 'But it is such-and-such' yields 'Therefore it is not not such-and-such.' And this is nothing more known than the excluded part which was a part of the syllogism. Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of either of them, as in our saying: 'But it is not not such-and-such' yields 'Therefore it is such-and-such.' And this exclusion too is not far from being more known than the result and more precedent in the mind. Rather, one benefits from that mostly in syllogisms composed of conjunctive and disjunctive [premises], as in their saying: 'This is either such-and-such or it is not such-and-such. If it is not such-and-such, then (j) d. But (j) d is not. Therefore it is such-and-such.' But this syllogism also is completed by a conjunctive conditional alone. And it is not unlikely that one does not need the disjunctive at all. So this section of disjunctions is not of much benefit; rather, the two parts must be not opposed in this way, as in our saying: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.' For the exclusion of exactly one of them yields the contradictory of the other, as is said: 'It is even' yields 'It is not odd.' Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of either of them yields the other exactly, as is said: 'It is not even' yields 'It is odd,' for example.

Inference in Non-Contradictory Two-Part Disjunctions
Author explains inference for non-contradictory two-part disjunctions with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the inference rules for a two-part true disjunction with non-contradictory parts and explain why contradictory parts are not useful.

Core Idea
In a non-contradictory two-part disjunction, excluding one part yields the contradictory of the other, and excluding the contradictory yields the other part.

The author now demonstrates the inference patterns for useful two-part disjunctions, contrasting the useless contradictory disjunction (A or not A) with the useful non-contradictory one (even or odd). This lesson focuses on the valid inferences and their application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Find a true disjunction with two parts that are not contradictory. Example: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.'
The disjunction is within the consequent of a conditional.
2
Apply exclusion of one part
Exclude one part (e.g., 'it is even'). The result is the contradictory of the other part: 'it is not odd.'
The author: 'استثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر.'
3
Apply exclusion of the contradictory of one part
Exclude the contradictory of one part (e.g., 'it is not even'). The result is the other part exactly: 'it is odd.'
The author: 'استثناء تقيض أيهما كان فإنه ينتج عين الآخر.'
4
Compare with contradictory disjunction
In a contradictory disjunction like 'it is such-and-such or it is not such-and-such', the same result can be obtained from a conjunctive conditional alone, making the disjunction redundant.
This is why non-contradictory disjunctions are more beneficial.

فصل : [ في أن القياس الاستثنائي يسمى شرطيا ] وأما القياس الاستثنائي فإنه يسمى شرطيا لما مر من قبل أنه مشتمل على مقدمة. والقياس الشرطي قد يكون متصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه متّصلة، وقد يكون منفصلا وهو الذي تكون صغراه منفصلة. والمنفصلة على أقسام متعددة؛ وذلك التعدد على حسب التعدد في المنفصلات حقيقية كانت المنفصلات أو غير حقيقية. أما الحقيقية وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو فإنها إما أن تكون ذات جزأين، أو ذات أجزاء كثيرة وما تكون ذات جزأين فإما أن يكون جزءاه مختلفين بالسلب والإيجاب على سبيل التناقض، وإما أن يكونا مختلفين لا على سبيل التناقض . أما الأول وهو الذي تكون ذات جزأين فذلك مثل قولنا هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا فاستثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر فتكون النتيجة هي بالمعنى نفس الاستثناء كما في قولنا لكنه كذا ينتج فإذن ليس لا كذا وهذا ليس شيئًا أعرف من الاستثناء الذي كان جزء قياس. وكذلك استثناء نقيض أيهما كان كما في قولنا لكنه ليس لا كذا ينتج فهو إذن كذا؛ وهذا الاستثناء أيضًا لا يكون بعيدا من أن لا يكون أعرف من النتيجة وأسبق إلى الذهن وإنما ينتفع بذلك في أكثر الأمر من قياسات مؤلفة من متّصلة ومنفصلة كما في قولهم هذا إما أن يكون كذا وإما أن لا يكون كذا، فإن لم يكن كذا فـ (ج) د ، لكن ليس (ج) د فهو إذن كذا لكن هذا القياس أيضا مما يتم بشرطية متصلة وحدها. ولا يبعد أن لا يحتاج فيها إلى المنفصلة بوجه فإذن هذا القسم من المنفصلات لا ينفع استعمالها كثير نفع بل يجب أن يكون الجزءان غير متقابلين على هذا الوجه كما في قولنا إن كان هذا عددًا فهو إما زوج وإما فرد؛ فإن استثناء عين أيهما كان ينتج نقيض الآخر كما يقال إنه زوج ينتج إنه ليس بفرد فكذلك استثناء تقيض أيهما كان فإنه ينتج عين الآخر كما يقال إنه ليس بزوج فينتج أنه فرد مثلا.
Section: [On that the assertoric syllogism is called conditional] As for the assertoric syllogism, it is called conditional because, as previously mentioned, it contains a premise. The conditional syllogism may be conjunctive, whose minor premise is conjunctive, and may be disjunctive, whose minor premise is disjunctive. The disjunctive premise has multiple divisions; that multiplicity is according to the multiplicity in disjunctions, whether they are true disjunctions or not. As for the true disjunction, which prevents both union and void, it is either of two parts or of many parts. What is of two parts: either its two parts differ by negation and affirmation in the way of contradiction, or they differ not in the way of contradiction. As for the first, which is of two parts, that is like our saying: 'This is either such-and-such or it is not such-and-such.' So the exclusion of exactly one of them yields the contradictory of the other, so the result is, in meaning, the same as the excluded part, as in our saying: 'But it is such-and-such' yields 'Therefore it is not not such-and-such.' And this is nothing more known than the excluded part which was a part of the syllogism. Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of either of them, as in our saying: 'But it is not not such-and-such' yields 'Therefore it is such-and-such.' And this exclusion too is not far from being more known than the result and more precedent in the mind. Rather, one benefits from that mostly in syllogisms composed of conjunctive and disjunctive [premises], as in their saying: 'This is either such-and-such or it is not such-and-such. If it is not such-and-such, then (j) d. But (j) d is not. Therefore it is such-and-such.' But this syllogism also is completed by a conjunctive conditional alone. And it is not unlikely that one does not need the disjunctive at all. So this section of disjunctions is not of much benefit; rather, the two parts must be not opposed in this way, as in our saying: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.' For the exclusion of exactly one of them yields the contradictory of the other, as is said: 'It is even' yields 'It is not odd.' Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of either of them yields the other exactly, as is said: 'It is not even' yields 'It is odd,' for example.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) and explains its two types: conjunctive and disjunctive. The author focuses on the true disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) which prevents both union and void (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluww). This is subdivided into those with two parts and those with many parts. For two-part disjunctions, the parts may be contradictory (e.g., 'either A or not A') or non-contradictory (e.g., 'either even or odd'). The author argues that the contradictory type is not useful because the same result can be obtained through a conjunctive conditional alone, and the exclusion is no more informative than the premise. Therefore, the useful two-part disjunction is the non-contradictory one. An example from arithmetic is given: 'If this is a number, then it is either even or odd.' In this case, excluding one part yields the contradictory of the other (e.g., 'it is even' yields 'it is not odd'), and excluding the contradictory of one part yields the other part (e.g., 'it is not even' yields 'it is odd'). This establishes the inference pattern for two-part disjunctive syllogisms.

Key Terms
استثناء عين
استثناء نقيض
زوج
فرد

D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
For a useful two-part disjunctive syllogism, the parts must be non-contradictory, and the inference rules are: exclude one part → get contradictory of the other; exclude contradictory of one part → get the other part.

Inference Rules for Many-Part Disjunctions
Many-part disjunction inference rules and an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the inference rules for disjunctive syllogisms with multiple finite disjuncts and evaluate the objection about multiple conclusions.

Core Idea
Many-part disjunctions generalize two-part rules but raise subtle questions about single conclusions.

We now extend disjunctive syllogisms to disjunctions with many finite parts. The author presents two inference rules: excluding a part yields the negation of all remaining parts, and excluding the negation of a part yields a disjunction of the remaining parts. He then addresses an objection: does a syllogism produce multiple conclusions? Let's work through the rules and the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the many-part disjunction
The author uses a number example: 'This number is either even-even, even-odd, or even-even-and-odd.' For an Islamic example, consider: 'This action is either wajib, mubah, makruh, or haram.' The disjuncts must be finite.
The example is illustrative; any exclusive and exhaustive finite set works.
2
Apply the first rule: excluding a part
If we affirm one disjunct (e.g., 'it is wajib'), we conclude that all other disjuncts are false: 'it is not mubah, not makruh, not haram.' The text calls this 'naqīḍ al-bawāqī' (contradictory of the remaining parts).
This yields multiple negations, which the objector later challenges.
3
Apply the second rule: excluding the negation of a part
If we deny one disjunct (e.g., 'it is not wajib'), we conclude that one of the remaining disjuncts is true: 'it is either mubah, makruh, or haram.' The conclusion is itself a disjunction.
This is called 'fard min afrād al-bawāqī' (one individual from the remaining parts).
4
Analyze the objector's challenge
The objector raises two interpretations of 'naqīḍ al-bawāqī': (a) multiple separate negations -> multiple conclusions, which violates the definition of a syllogism; (b) the negation of the disjunction of remaining parts -> a false claim, because affirming one part prevents the others, so the negated disjunction is actually true (contradicting the objector's claim).
The objector concludes the syllogism is invalid under either interpretation.
5
Examine the author's response (incomplete)
The author begins his answer: 'The syllogism is not conditioned on not producing more than one result essentially; rather, its condition is to produce one result.' The passage cuts off; the full response likely clarifies that multiple conclusions can be considered one disjunctive result.
The missing end of the passage must be verified from the original source.

As for the second, which is of many parts, that is either with finite parts, as in our saying: 'This number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even-and-odd.' Then the exclusion of any one part yields the contradictory of the remaining parts, as is said: 'But it is even-even' yields 'It is not even-odd, and it is not even-even-and-odd.' Likewise, the exclusion of the contradictory of any part yields one of the remaining parts, as is said: 'But it is not even-even' yields 'It is either even-odd or even-even-and-odd.' As for the contradictory of the remaining parts in this form, it is understood in two ways: one is that the result is not one result but more, according to the remaining parts; the second is that it yields the contradictory of the disjunction composed of those parts. Commentary
This passage extends the discussion to disjunctions with many finite parts. The author uses the example of a number being classified into three types: even-even (divisible by 4?), even-odd (even but not divisible by 4?), and even-even-and-odd (impossible combination? The example is illustrative). The inference rules are: excluding any one part yields the negation of all the remaining parts; excluding the negation of one part yields one of the remaining parts (a disjunctive conclusion). Then a potential objector raises a problem: if the exclusion yields more than one conclusion, then a single syllogism has multiple conclusions simultaneously, which violates the definition of a syllogism. Alternatively, if we take the second way (the result is the contradictory of the disjunction of remaining parts), the result would be false because the original part prevents the others. For example, if the number is even-even, it is not even-odd nor even-even-and-odd, so the statement 'it is not either even-odd or even-even-and-odd' is actually true? Wait: the objector says it is false because being even-even prevents both, so the disjunctive negation is true? Actually the objector claims the result would be false: 'it is not the case that it is either even-odd or even-even-and-odd' is true if it is even-even, so it seems the objector might be mistaken. The author's answer is that the syllogism is not required to produce exactly one conclusion; it only needs to produce one result (perhaps meaning a definite result). The passage cuts off before completing the answer. The response likely clarifies that the multiple conclusions are essentially one disjunctive conclusion, or that the inference is valid in a specific sense.

Finite and Infinite Parts in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author distinguishes finite and infinite-part disjunctives and their valid inference patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between disjunctives with finite parts and those with infinite parts, and explain why finite parts are necessary for practical syllogistic use.

Core Idea
A multi-part disjunction yields conclusions only when its parts are finite.

The author extends the discussion of disjunctive syllogisms to those with many parts. He immediately classifies them into those with finite parts and those with infinite parts. Understanding this distinction is the first key to using such syllogisms correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification
Find where the author divides multi-part disjunctives into two types based on the number of parts. The text says: 'that is either its parts are finite... or if the parts are infinite'.
2
Examine the finite example
The author gives the example of even numbers classified as even-even, even-odd, or even-even and odd. Count the parts: they are three, hence finite.
3
Contrast with infinite parts
The author states that if parts are infinite, extraction is useless. Why? Because affirming one part leaves an indefinite remainder that cannot be exhaustively stated.
4
Understand the practical implication
The benefit of disjunctive syllogisms is to reach a single definite conclusion through successive extractions. This is impossible with infinite parts because you can never close all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني وهو الذي تكون ذات أجزاء كثيرة فذلك إما أن يكون أجزاؤه متناهية كما في قولنا هذا الزوج إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج و فرد: وحينئذ يكون استثناء أي جزء منها ينتج نقيض البواقي كما يقال لقائل أن يقول إن هذا لا يكون قياسا؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان منتجا أكثر من نتيجة واحدة كان للقياس الواحد نتائج كثيرة لا يكون واحد منها قبل الغير ولا بعده أصلا. وإذا كان منتجًا على الوجه الآخر كانت النتيجة المنفصلة. لكنا نقول في الجواب إن القياس لا يكون مشروطا بشرط أن لا ينتج بالذات أكثر من نتيجة واحدة بل كان من شرطه أن ينتج نتيجة واحدة. وكونه منتجا أكثر من نتيجة واحدة لا يكون مانعا عن كونه منتجا نتيجة فلا يمتنع أن يستأنف من هذه النتيجة ومن نقيض البعض من الأجزاء قياس حتى يتأدى إلى أن ينتج أكثر من نتيجة واحدة؛ ويكون القياس متعددا حسب تعدد الأجزاء. وأما إذا كانت الأجزاء غير متناهية فليس ينتفع في الاستثناء من مثل هذه الشرطية بوجه من الوجوه. وذلك لأن استثناء عين واحد من الأجزاء لا ينتج شيئًا يمكن أن ينطق به. اللهم إلا أن تكون النتيجة فليس شيء مما عدا هذا المعين؛ وحينئذ تكون النتيجة عن قياس ذات المترادفة بالاستثناءات المتوالية متهيئة إلى قسم واحد ونتيجة واحدة؛ وهذا مما لا سبيل إليه في استعمال القياس الاستثنائي من منفصلات ذوات أجزاء غير متناهية.
As for the second [type], which has many parts, that is either its parts are finite, as in our saying 'This number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd': then extracting any part yields the negation of the rest, as it is said 'But it is even-even' yields 'It is not even-odd, and it is not even-even and odd.' Extracting the negation of any part yields one individual from the remaining parts, as it is said 'But it is not even-even' yields 'It is either even-odd or even-even and odd.' As for the negation of the rest in this case, it is understood in two ways: one that the result is not one result but more according to the remaining parts, and the second that it produces the negation of the disjunction composed of those parts. If that were the case, one could say that this is not a syllogism; because if it produces more than one result, a single syllogism would have many results none of which is prior to or posterior to another at all. And if it produces in the other way, the result is false, for it is not true that this number is not either even-odd or even-even and odd; because its being even-even prevents it from being even-odd and prevents it from being even-even and odd. So the extraction does not prevent it, nor is it the negation of what was extracted; for a categorical does not contradict a disjunction. But we say in answer: The syllogism is not conditioned by the condition that it does not essentially produce more than one result; rather its condition is to produce one result. And its producing more than one result does not prevent it from producing one result. And because it produces the results insofar as it is potentially multiple; that is, when you say 'But it is even-even', you need to realize in yourself another premise, namely that what is even-even is not even-odd, so it produces one result, and that what is even-even is not even-even and odd, so it produces another result. And similarly if the divisions are more than three: this is the statement concerning extraction of the identical. As for extraction of the negation as mentioned, our saying 'Either even-odd or even-even and odd' is the proximate result; there is no obstacle to starting from this result and from the negation of some of the parts a syllogism until it leads to producing more than one result; and the syllogisms become multiple according to the multiplicity of parts. And if the parts are infinite, then no benefit is gained in extraction from such a conditional in any way. Because extracting one identical part from the parts produces nothing that can be uttered. Unless the result is 'there is nothing besides this particular'; then the result is from a two-part syllogism, as if he said 'It is either two or other than two, but it is two, so it is not other than two.' Likewise, if the extraction is of the negation of some of those parts, it also produces no limited thing that can be uttered, unless it is said that it produces something from the rest. And this too is in reality from a two-part syllogism, as if he said 'It is either two or something that is other than two.' Then the achieved benefit in extraction from disjunctions is to complete successive syllogisms by consecutive extractions, settling into one division and one result; and there is no way to this in the use of conditional syllogism from disjunctions with infinite parts.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the second type of disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil), specifically focusing on disjunctions that have more than two parts. The author first distinguishes between finite and infinite parts. For finite parts, he provides an example using the classification of even numbers: every even number is either even-even (divisible by 4), even-odd (divisible by 2 but not 4), or even-even and odd (contradiction? Actually, even-even and odd is impossible, but the example is illustrative). He then explains two inference rules: (1) affirming one part (istithna' al-'ayn) yields the negation of all the remaining parts; (2) negating one part (istithna' al-naqid) yields a disjunction of the remaining parts (one of them, not determined which). The author then considers an objection: if affirming a part yields multiple negative conclusions (one for each remaining part), then it seems a single syllogism produces many conclusions, which violates the definition of a syllogism as producing a single conclusion. Alternatively, if we interpret 'negation of the rest' as the negation of the whole disjunction of the remaining parts, the result is false because the affirmation of one part does not imply the falsehood of the disjunction of the others (since the disjunction of the others is false, but the conclusion should be that they are all false individually, not their disjunction). The author replies by clarifying that the syllogism essentially produces one conclusion at a time; the multiple conclusions arise because the reasoning implicitly uses additional premises (e.g., 'what is even-even is not even-odd') and thus the single extraction actually enables multiple syllogisms. When the extraction is of a negation, the result is a disjunction of the remaining parts, which is a single proposition; one can then apply further extractions to narrow it down. Finally, for infinite parts, no useful conclusion can be drawn because affirming one part leaves an infinite remainder that cannot be exhaustively stated, and negating one part leaves an indefinite remainder. The only case where a finite conclusion emerges is when the disjunction is essentially binary (e.g., 'It is either two or not two'), which reduces to a two-part disjunction. The overall lesson is that disjunctive syllogisms with many parts are only practically useful when the parts are finite.

استثناء العين
نقيض البواقي

State why this finiteness allows you to conclude that if an action is not wajib, it could be one of the other four. What would happen if there were an infinite number of categories?

Affirming a Part in a Multi-Part Disjunction and the Objection of Multiple Conclusions
Author distinguishes finite and infinite-part disjunctives and their valid inference patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of affirming a part (istithna' al-'ayn) in a finite multi-part disjunctive syllogism, identify the objection that it yields multiple conclusions, and explain the author's resolution.

Core Idea
Affirming one part negates each remainder individually, which seems to violate single-conclusion rule but actually invokes additional premises.

The first rule for multi-part disjunctives is affirming one part. This produces separate negations for each remaining part. This appears to give multiple conclusions from one syllogism, which the author addresses by clarifying that it is essentially multiple syllogisms each with its own implicit premise.

Guided Path
1
Recall the finite disjunction
Use the example: 'This number is either even-even, even-odd, or even-even and odd.' Identify the three parts.
2
Perform the affirmation
Affirm 'it is even-even'. According to the author, the conclusion is: 'it is not even-odd' and 'it is not even-even and odd'. Write these as two separate conclusions.
3
Identify the objection
The text presents an objection: if a syllogism produces more than one result, it is not a proper syllogism. Find and quote the relevant sentence: 'if it produces more than one result, a single syllogism would have many results'.
4
Analyze the author's reply
The author replies that the syllogism itself produces one result at a time. The multiple conclusions come from additional premises. For example, to conclude 'not even-odd', you implicitly use the premise 'what is even-even is not even-odd'. Thus it is multiple syllogisms, not one.
5
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider: 'The invalid sale is either sale of ghurar, sale of riba, or sale of najash.' Affirm 'sale of ghurar'. The conclusion is: it is not sale of riba, and it is not sale of najash. Now explain why this is two separate inferences.

وأما الثاني وهو الذي تكون ذات أجزاء كثيرة فذلك إما أن يكون أجزاؤه متناهية كما في قولنا هذا الزوج إما زوج زوج وإما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج و فرد: وحينئذ يكون استثناء أي جزء منها ينتج نقيض البواقي كما يقال لكنه زوج زوج ينتج أنه لا يكون زوج فرد، ولا يكون زوج زوج و فرد كذلك. واستثناء نقيض أي جزء منها ينتج فردًا من أفراد البواقي كما يقال لكنه ليس بزوج زوج فينتج إما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج وفرد. وأما نقيض البواقي في هذه الصورة فإنه يفهم على وجهين: أحدهما أن تكون النتيجة ليست نتيجة واحدة بل أكثر على حسب الأجزاء الباقية ، وثانيهما أنه ينتج نقيض المنفصلة التي تكون مركبة من تلك الأجزاء. ولو كان كذلك لكان لقائل أن يقول إن هذا لا يكون قياسا؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان منتجا أكثر من نتيجة واحدة كان للقياس الواحد نتائج كثيرة لا يكون واحد منها قبل الغير ولا بعده أصلا. وإذا كان منتجًا على الوجه الآخر كانت النتيجة كاذبة فإنه ليس حقا إن هذا الزوج لا يكون إما زوج فرد أو زوج زوج وفرد؛ فإن كونه زوج زوج يمنع كونه زوج فرد، ويمنع كونه زوج زوج و فرد. فليس الاستثناء بمانع إياه ولا هو نقيض ما استثنى؛ فإن الحملية لا تناقض المنفصلة. لكنا نقول في الجواب إن القياس لا يكون مشروطا بشرط أن لا ينتج بالذات أكثر من نتيجة واحدة بل كان من شرطه أن ينتج نتيجة واحدة. وكونه منتجا أكثر من نتيجة واحدة لا يكون مانعا عن كونه منتجا نتيجة واحدة. ولأنه إنما ينتج النتائج من حيث إنه بالقوة متعدد؛ وذلك لأنك إذا قلت لكنه زوج زوج احتجت أن تدرك في نفسك مقدمة أخرى وهو أن ما يكون زوج زوج فهو ليس بزوج فرد؛ فينتج نتيجة، وأن ما يكون زوج زوج فهو ليس بزوج زوج و فرد؛ فينتج نتيجة أخرى. وعلى هذا إذا كانت الأقسام أكثر من ثلاثة: هذا هو القول في استثناء العين. وأما في استثناء النقيض كما مرَّ فقولنا إما زوج فرد وإما زوج زوج و فرد هو النتيجة القريبة فلا يمتنع أن يستأنف من هذه النتيجة ومن نقيض البعض من الأجزاء قياس حتى يتأدى إلى أن ينتج أكثر من نتيجة واحدة؛ ويكون القياس متعددا حسب تعدد الأجزاء. وأما إذا كانت الأجزاء غير متناهية فليس ينتفع في الاستثناء من مثل هذه الشرطية بوجه من الوجوه. وذلك لأن استثناء عين واحد من الأجزاء لا ينتج شيئًا يمكن أن ينطق به. اللهم إلا أن تكون النتيجة فليس شيء مما عدا هذا المعين؛ وحينئذ تكون النتيجة عن قياس ذات جزأين فكأنه قال إنه إما أن يكون العدد اثنين أو ما عدا الاثنين لكنه اثنان فليس ما عدا الاثنين. وكذلك إذا كان الاستثناء نقيض البعض من تلك الأجزاء فإنه لا ينتج أيضًا شيئاً محدوداً يمكن أن ينطق به إلا أن يقال إنه ينتج شيئًا من البواقي. وهذا أيضًا بالحقيقة عن قياس ذات جزأين فكأنه يقول إما أن يكون اثنين أو شيئًا مما يعدّه الاثنين. ثم الفائدة المحصلة في الاستثناء من المنفصلات هي استتمام القياسات المترادفة بالاستثناءات المتوالية متهيئة إلى قسم واحد ونتيجة واحدة؛ وهذا مما لا سبيل إليه في استعمال القياس الاستثنائي من منفصلات ذوات أجزاء غير متناهية.
As for the second [type], which has many parts, that is either its parts are finite, as in our saying 'This number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd': then extracting any part yields the negation of the rest, as it is said 'But it is even-even' yields 'It is not even-odd, and it is not even-even and odd.' Extracting the negation of any part yields one individual from the remaining parts, as it is said 'But it is not even-even' yields 'It is either even-odd or even-even and odd.' As for the negation of the rest in this case, it is understood in two ways: one that the result is not one result but more according to the remaining parts, and the second that it produces the negation of the disjunction composed of those parts. If that were the case, one could say that this is not a syllogism; because if it produces more than one result, a single syllogism would have many results none of which is prior to or posterior to another at all. And if it produces in the other way, the result is false, for it is not true that this number is not either even-odd or even-even and odd; because its being even-even prevents it from being even-odd and prevents it from being even-even and odd. So the extraction does not prevent it, nor is it the negation of what was extracted; for a categorical does not contradict a disjunction. But we say in answer: The syllogism is not conditioned by the condition that it does not essentially produce more than one result; rather its condition is to produce one result. And its producing more than one result does not prevent it from producing one result. And because it produces the results insofar as it is potentially multiple; that is, when you say 'But it is even-even', you need to realize in yourself another premise, namely that what is even-even is not even-odd, so it produces one result, and that what is even-even is not even-even and odd, so it produces another result. And similarly if the divisions are more than three: this is the statement concerning extraction of the identical. As for extraction of the negation as mentioned, our saying 'Either even-odd or even-even and odd' is the proximate result; there is no obstacle to starting from this result and from the negation of some of the parts a syllogism until it leads to producing more than one result; and the syllogisms become multiple according to the multiplicity of parts. And if the parts are infinite, then no benefit is gained in extraction from such a conditional in any way. Because extracting one identical part from the parts produces nothing that can be uttered. Unless the result is 'there is nothing besides this particular'; then the result is from a two-part syllogism, as if he said 'It is either two or other than two, but it is two, so it is not other than two.' Likewise, if the extraction is of the negation of some of those parts, it also produces no limited thing that can be uttered, unless it is said that it produces something from the rest. And this too is in reality from a two-part syllogism, as if he said 'It is either two or something that is other than two.' Then the achieved benefit in extraction from disjunctions is to complete successive syllogisms by consecutive extractions, settling into one division and one result; and there is no way to this in the use of conditional syllogism from disjunctions with infinite parts.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Negating a Part in a Multi-Part Disjunction and Subsequent Syllogisms
Author distinguishes finite and infinite-part disjunctives and their valid inference patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of negating one part (istithna' al-naqid) in a finite multi-part disjunctive syllogism and understand how the resulting disjunction can be used for further syllogisms.

Core Idea
Negating one part leaves a disjunction of the remaining parts, which can then be further analyzed.

The second rule for multi-part disjunctives is negating one part. This yields a single disjunctive conclusion, avoiding the objection of multiple conclusions. Moreover, that conclusion itself is a disjunction that can serve as the premise for another syllogism, allowing a chain of reasoning to narrow down to a single definitive result.

Guided Path
1
Recall the finite disjunction
Use the example: 'This number is either even-even, even-odd, or even-even and odd.' Identify the three parts.
2
Apply negation of one part
Negate 'it is even-even'. The conclusion according to the author is: 'it is either even-odd or even-even and odd'. This is a single disjunctive proposition.
3
Notice the lack of objection
Unlike affirming a part, negating a part yields exactly one conclusion, so it does not violate the single-conclusion requirement. The text does not raise an objection here.
4
Understand subsequent syllogisms
The conclusion itself is a disjunction. We can then apply further extractions to it. For example, from 'it is either even-odd or even-even and odd', we can negate 'even-odd' to conclude 'it is even-even and odd'. This shows the chain of reasoning.
5
Apply to a fiqh example
Consider: 'The kaffarah is either freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.' If we negate freeing a slave, we conclude it is either fasting two months or feeding sixty poor. Then from that we can negate fasting two months to conclude it is feeding sixty poor. This is a two-step syllogism.

As for the second [type], which has many parts, that is either its parts are finite, as in our saying 'This number is either even-even, or even-odd, or even-even and odd': then extracting any part yields the negation of the rest, as it is said 'But it is even-even' yields 'It is not even-odd, and it is not even-even and odd.' And because it produces the results insofar as it is potentially multiple; that is, when you say 'But it is even-even', you need to realize in yourself another premise, namely that what is even-even is not even-odd, so it produces one result, and that what is even-even is not even-even and odd, so it produces another result. And similarly if the divisions are more than three: this is the statement concerning extraction of the identical. As for extraction of the negation as mentioned, our saying 'Either even-odd or even-even and odd' is the proximate result; there is no obstacle to starting from this result and from the negation of some of the parts a syllogism until it leads to producing more than one result; and the syllogisms become multiple according to the multiplicity of parts. And if the parts are infinite, then no benefit is gained in extraction from such a conditional in any way. Because extracting one identical part from the parts produces nothing that can be uttered. Unless the result is 'there is nothing besides this particular'; then the result is from a two-part syllogism, as if he said 'It is either two or other than two, but it is two, so it is not other than two.' Likewise, if the extraction is of the negation of some of those parts, it also produces no limited thing that can be uttered, unless it is said that it produces something from the rest. And this too is in reality from a two-part syllogism, as if he said 'It is either two or something that is other than two.' Then the achieved benefit in extraction from disjunctions is to complete successive syllogisms by consecutive extractions, settling into one division and one result; and there is no way to this in the use of conditional syllogism from disjunctions with infinite parts.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the second type of disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil), specifically focusing on disjunctions that have more than two parts. The author first distinguishes between finite and infinite parts. For finite parts, he provides an example using the classification of even numbers: every even number is either even-even (divisible by 4), even-odd (divisible by 2 but not 4), or even-even and odd (contradiction? Actually, even-even and odd is impossible, but the example is illustrative). He then explains two inference rules: (1) affirming one part (istithna' al-'ayn) yields the negation of all the remaining parts; (2) negating one part (istithna' al-naqid) yields a disjunction of the remaining parts (one of them, not determined which). The author then considers an objection: if affirming a part yields multiple negative conclusions (one for each remaining part), then it seems a single syllogism produces many conclusions, which violates the definition of a syllogism as producing a single conclusion. Alternatively, if we interpret 'negation of the rest' as the negation of the whole disjunction of the remaining parts, the result is false because the affirmation of one part does not imply the falsehood of the disjunction of the others (since the disjunction of the others is false, but the conclusion should be that they are all false individually, not their disjunction). The author replies by clarifying that the syllogism essentially produces one conclusion at a time; the multiple conclusions arise because the reasoning implicitly uses additional premises (e.g., 'what is even-even is not even-odd') and thus the single extraction actually enables multiple syllogisms. When the extraction is of a negation, the result is a disjunction of the remaining parts, which is a single proposition; one can then apply further extractions to narrow it down. Finally, for infinite parts, no useful conclusion can be drawn because affirming one part leaves an infinite remainder that cannot be exhaustively stated, and negating one part leaves an indefinite remainder. The only case where a finite conclusion emerges is when the disjunction is essentially binary (e.g., 'It is either two or not two'), which reduces to a two-part disjunction. The overall lesson is that disjunctive syllogisms with many parts are only practically useful when the parts are finite.

Multiple Results and Infinite Parts in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains how disjunctive syllogisms can yield multiple results and why infinite parts are useless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a disjunctive syllogism with multiple parts and determine whether it can produce a definite conclusion.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogisms with infinite parts are useless; finite divisions can produce multiple conclusions through sequential exclusions.

Disjunctive syllogisms are powerful tools for reasoning about alternatives, but their usefulness depends on the number of parts. This passage explores what happens when a disjunction has more than two parts—can we still derive a conclusion? The author explains that multiple results are possible, but only if the parts are finite. Understanding this principle is crucial for avoiding fallacious reasoning in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Determine whether the conditional is exclusive (mani'at al-jam wal-khuluw) or inclusive. The passage assumes exclusive disjunction for the examples.
Exclusive disjunction: only one part can be true.
2
Distinguish between the two exclusion methods
The author treats istithna' al-'ayn (affirming one specific part) and istithna' al-naqid (denying one part). Note that affirming a part does not directly yield a conclusion; it requires an additional premise.
In exclusive disjunction, affirming one part leads to denying the rest, but only if the disjunction is truly exclusive.
3
Trace how multiple results arise
When a disjunction has three or more parts, excluding one part (by affirming or denying) can generate a new disjunction among the remaining parts. This can be repeated to produce multiple syllogisms, each yielding a distinct conclusion.
The author illustrates with the example of even numbers: even-even, even-odd, even-even-odd.
4
Recognize the problem of infinite parts
If the number of parts is infinite, excluding any one part (by affirmation or denial) leaves an infinite remainder that cannot be expressed as a single definite proposition. Thus, no useful conclusion can be stated.
The only exception is when the remainder is collectively expressed as 'other than this', but that effectively reduces the syllogism to two parts.
5
Apply to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, legal rulings (ahkam) are finite (e.g., five categories). Disjunctive reasoning works because the parts are enumerable. Infinite divisions (e.g., arbitrary numbers) are not used.
This lesson shows why jurists limit their classifications to manageable, finite sets.

More than one result does not prevent it from producing a single result. And because it produces results by virtue of being potentially multiple; that is because when you say 'but it is even-even' you need to perceive in yourself another premise, which is that what is even-even is not even-odd; so it produces a result, and that what is even-even is not even-even-odd; so it produces another result. And on this basis, if the divisions are more than three: this is the statement about excluding the very part. As for excluding the contradictory as mentioned, our statement 'either it is even-odd or even-even-odd' is the proximate result, so it is not impossible to start from this result and from the contradictory of some of the parts a syllogism until it leads to producing more than one result; and the syllogism is multiple according to the multiplicity of parts. And if the parts are infinite, then there is no benefit in excluding from such a conditional in any way. That is because excluding one very part from the parts does not produce something that can be articulated. Unless the result is 'it is nothing other than this specific one'; then the result is from a syllogism with two parts, as if he said 'it is either two or other than two, but it is two, so it is not other than two.' And similarly, if the exclusion is the contradictory of some of those parts, it also does not produce something limited that can be articulated, unless it is said that it produces something from the remainder. And this too is in reality from a two-part syllogism, as if he says 'it is either two or something that is not two.' Then the attained benefit in excluding from disjunctives is the completion of successive syllogisms by successive exclusions, converging to one division and one result; and this is impossible in the use of the hypothetical syllogism from disjunctives with infinite parts. This is the hypothetical syllogism from a real premise.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses the behavior of disjunctive conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-istithna'i al-munfasil) when the disjunction has more than two parts. The author first addresses the concern that such syllogisms might produce more than one result, noting that this does not prevent them from being productive of a single result; rather, they are potentially multiple in the sense that each exclusion can yield separate conclusions. He illustrates with the example of 'even-even' and 'even-odd' divisions of number, showing how one might need additional premises to derive specific results. For excluding the very part (istithna' al-'ayn), if there are more than three divisions, the same principle applies. For excluding the contradictory (istithna' al-naqid), the proximate result (like 'either even-odd or even-even-odd') can serve as a starting point for further syllogisms, each producing its own result. The author then warns that if the parts are infinite, no useful exclusion can be made because excluding one specific part yields no definite result—unless the only remaining possibility is what remains, which effectively reduces the syllogism to two parts by treating the infinite remainder as a single 'other'. Similarly, excluding the contradictory of a part also yields no finite result. The overall benefit of successive exclusion in disjunctives is to narrow down to one part and one conclusion, which is impossible with infinite parts. This explains why real disjunctive syllogisms are typically limited to finite divisions, as in Islamic legal reasoning (e.g., classifying acts as wajib, haram, mandub, makruh, mubah).

Refuting a Shubha Against Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author presents and refutes a sophistical objection to disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a sophistical objection (shubha) that claims affirming a disjunct invalidates the disjunctive syllogism, and construct a rebuttal using the author's method of affirming the persistence of the relation of opposition (in'ad).

Core Idea
A true disjunction's logical relation of mutual exclusion persists even after affirming one disjunct.

In this passage, the author presents common sophistical objections raised against the disjunctive syllogism. These objections mistakenly think that once you affirm one disjunct, the disjunction itself disappears, thereby invalidating the inference. The author refutes this by clarifying that the disjunction expresses a relationship of opposition that remains. Understanding this defense is crucial for defending the validity of disjunctive arguments in Islamic legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive syllogism under attack
The objection uses the example: 'This number is either even or odd; it is even; therefore it is not odd.' Recognize this as a valid exclusive disjunctive syllogism where the disjuncts are mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive.
The author refers to this as the 'true disjunction' (al-munfaṣila al-ḥaqīqiyya).
2
Extract the shubha's core claim
The objection argues that after saying 'it is even', the statement 'either even or odd' no longer holds, so the conclusion does not follow. The disjunction is thought to depend on uncertainty about which disjunct is true.
This objection relies on a misunderstanding: that the disjunction is only meaningful when the truth of the disjuncts is unknown.
3
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author says the disjunction expresses a relation of opposition (in'ad) such that from the realization of one disjunct, the negation of the other follows necessarily. This relation remains on all assumptions, including after one disjunct is affirmed. The implication is conditional: 'if it is even, then it is not odd' still holds.
The author explicitly states that the disjunction remains because the relation of opposition is independent of the actual truth of the disjuncts.
4
Apply the principle to an Islamic legal example
Consider a fiqh example: 'Either this sale is valid or it is riba (usury). It is not riba, therefore it is valid.' A shubha might claim that once we know it is not riba, the disjunction disappears. The rebuttal is the same: the relation of opposition (valid sale vs. riba) persists, so the implication holds.
This shows the practical importance: a faqih can rely on disjunctive reasoning even after determining one disjunct is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبه أيضًا؛ وذلك من وجوه [1] منها أن يقال استثناء عين المقدم لا ينتج نقيض التالي في قولنا هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد مثلا لما أن إنتاجه مبني على قولنا إما زوج وإما فرد؛ وذلك لا يكون باقيا بعد قولنا إنه زوج ولا بعد قولنا إنه فرد كذلك. فإنه إذا كان زوجًا أو فرض كونه زوجا فلا يصح أن يقال إنه إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا. [ب] ومنها أن يقال: إن التردد في زوجية هذا العدد وفرديته متحقق في نفس الأمر وإلا لا يصح قولنا إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردًا . ثمَّ ذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون باقيا عند الاستثناء؛ وذلك محال لاستحالة التردد في كونه زوجًا على تقدير أن يكون زوجا، أو لا يكون باقيا؛ وذلك محال أيضًا ضرورة تحققه في نفس الأمر فيكون المحال لازما على تقديري كونه باقيا وعدم كونه باقيا. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون هذا المجموع وهو الزوجية مع عدم الفردية منتفيًا في نفس الأمر قطعا أو محتملاً. وانتفاء المجموع مطلقا مما ينافي اللزوم بالضرورة فلا يلزم إذن من عين هذا نقیض ذلك. [ت] ومنها أن يقال: إن العناد بين كونه زوجًا وبين كونه فردًا مما ينا في أحد المجموعين وهو إما الزوجية مع عدم الفردية أو الفردية مع عدم الزوجية؛ وذلك لأن الزوجية في نفس الأمر لا يخلو من أن تكون ثابتة أو لا تكون وأيهما كان يلزم انتفاء أحد المجموعين أو نقول إن الزوجية لا يخلو من أن تكون ثابتة أو لا تكون وأيهما كان يلزم انتفاء أحد المجموعين. وانتفاء أحد المجموعين مما ينافي أحد اللزومين وهو إما لزوم عدم الفردية للزوجية أو لزوم عدم الزوجية للفردية؛ وهذا ظاهر. [ث] ومنها أن يقال: هذا المجموع بعينه وهو الزوجية مع عدم الزوجية أو ما يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء اللزوم يكون منتفيًا في نفس الأمر لما مرَّ أو لا في المتصلات وهو أن العدم لا يخلو من أن يكون شاملا لهما، أو لا يكون ويلزم من لزوم انتفاء هذا أو ذلك انتفاء اللزوم في نفس الأمر ؛ وهذا ظاهر أيضًا.

/ وأما الجواب [1] فنقول في الأول سلمنا أن إنتاجه مبني على ذلك لكن [٢٣٦ظ] لم قلتم بأن ذلك لا يكون باقيًا بعد قولنا إنه زوج أو إنه فرد بل يكون باقيا؛ إذ المفهوم منه هو العناد بينهما على وجه يلزم من تحقق هذا انتفاء ذلك وبالعكس؛ وذلك يكون باقيا على تقدير أن يكون زوجا حتى إذا لم يكن التقدير وهو كونه زوجا يلزم أن يكون فردًا . ولا يقال إنه إذا كان باقيًا على هذا التقدير يلزم على هذا التقدير أنه إذا لم يكن زوجا يكون فردا؛ وذلك في حيز المنع فإن كونه زوجًا مما يستلزم نقيض كونه فردا على جميع التقادير. وكذلك كونه فردًا لنقيض كونه زوجًا، وبالعكس في كل واحد منهما أيضًا وإلا لا تكون المنفصلة حقيقية. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن يكون فردًا على هذا التقدير إذا لم يكن زوجا وإلا لا يكون كونه فردًا من لوازم عدم كونه زوجًا على جميع التقادير. [ب] وفي الثاني نقول: سلمنا بأن المحال لازم عند الاستثناء سواء كان التردد باقيًا أو لا يكون باقيًا لكن لا يلزم منه انتفاء كونه رفعا في نفس الأمر ؛ وذلك لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم. وأما انتفاء ذلك المجموع وهو الزوجية مع عدم الفردية فكما أنه يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء ذلك المجموع؛ فكذلك يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء هذا المجموع أيضًا وهو الزوجية مع الفردية بل يلزم من انتفائه انتفاء المجموع المركب من هذه الثلاثة. وانتفاء المجموع مما يحقق اللزوم بين الجزء الأول وعدم ما عداه؛ وإنه مما يحقق اللزوم بين كونه زوجا وعدم كونه فردًا لاستحالة انتفاء ما عداه بانتفاء اللا فردية. وإن قيل كيف هو وانتفاء المجموع الأول مما يحقق هذا فنقول كما أن انتفاء ذلك المجموع مما يحققه؛ فانتفاء المجموع الثاني مما ينافيه. ولأن اللزوم فيما نحن فيه باعتبار
The fallacies also; that is from aspects: [1] Among them it is said that excluding (affirming) the very premise does not produce the contradictory of the consequent in our saying 'This number is either even or odd', for example, because its production is based on our saying 'Either even or odd'; and that does not remain after we say 'It is even' nor after we say 'It is odd'. Because if it is even or is assumed to be even, it is not correct to say 'It is either even or odd'. [2] Among them it is said: The hesitation (indeterminacy) regarding the evenness and oddness of this number is actual in itself, otherwise our saying 'It is either even or odd' is not correct. Then that (hesitation) is not free from either being remaining at the time of exception—that is impossible because of the impossibility of hesitation regarding its being even on the assumption that it is even—or not being remaining; that is also impossible due to its actual realization in itself. So the impossible is necessary on both assumptions: its remaining and its not remaining. Then it follows that this aggregate—namely evenness with non-oddness—is negated in actual existence either definitively or possibly. And the negation of the aggregate absolutely contradicts the implication necessarily; so from this [premise] the contradictory of that [consequent] does not follow. [3] Among them it is said: The opposition between its being even and its being odd relates to one of two aggregates, namely either evenness-with-non-oddness or oddness-with-non-evenness; because evenness in actual existence is not free from either being established or not, and whichever it is, the negation of one of the two aggregates follows. Or we say: Evenness is not free from either being established or not, and whichever it is, the negation of one of the two aggregates follows. And the negation of one of the two aggregates contradicts one of the two implications, namely either the implication of non-oddness from evenness or the implication of non-evenness from oddness; and this is apparent. [4] Among them it is said: This very aggregate—namely evenness with non-evenness—or what follows from its negation, namely the negation of implication, is negated in actual existence due to what passed, or in conditionals it is that non-existence is not free from either encompassing both or not, and from the necessary negation of this or that follows the negation of implication in actual existence; and this is also apparent.

/ As for the answer: [1] We say regarding the first: We grant that its production is based on that, but why do you say that that does not remain after we say 'It is even' or 'It is odd'? Rather it remains; because what is understood from it is the opposition between them in a way that from the realization of this follows the negation of that and vice versa; and that remains on the assumption that it is even, such that if the assumption (i.e., its being even) is not [real], it follows that it is odd. And it is not said that if it remains on this assumption, then on this assumption it follows that if it is not even then it is odd; that is in the realm of prevention, for its being even implies the contradictory of its being odd on all assumptions. And likewise its being odd implies the contradictory of its being even, and vice versa for each of them as well; otherwise the disjunctive would not be real. And if it were so, then it would be among the necessary consequences that it be odd on this assumption if it is not even, otherwise its being odd would not be among the necessary consequences of its not being even on all assumptions. [2] Regarding the second: We say: We grant that the impossible is necessary at the exception, whether the hesitation remains or does not remain, but the negation of its being a proposition in actual existence does not follow from that; and that does not prevent the implication. As for the negation of that aggregate—namely evenness with non-oddness—just as from its negation follows the negation of that aggregate, so also from its negation follows the negation of this aggregate, namely evenness with oddness; rather from its negation follows the negation of the composite aggregate of these three. And the negation of the aggregate realizes the implication between the first part and the non-existence of what is other than it; and that realizes the implication between its being even and its not being odd because of the impossibility of the negation of what is other than it by the negation of non-oddness. And if it is said: How is that? And the negation of the first aggregate realizes this? We say: Just as the negation of that aggregate realizes it, so the negation of the second aggregate contradicts it. And because the implication in our case is considered.

Commentary
This passage presents three sophistical objections (شبهات) against the validity of the disjunctive syllogism, specifically the rule that affirming one disjunct yields the contradictory of the other disjunct. The author then refutes each objection in turn. The objections are subtle and hinge on the nature of the disjunction and the persistence of the disjunctive proposition after one disjunct is affirmed.

The first objection claims that when we say 'This number is either even or odd', the disjunction no longer holds after we affirm 'It is even', so we cannot conclude 'It is not odd'. The author answers by explaining that the disjunction expresses a relationship of opposition such that the implication of the negation of the other remains even when one disjunct is affirmed; the conditional relationship persists.

The second objection argues that the hesitation (indeterminacy) between evenness and oddness is real in actual fact, but if we consider it at the time of exception, it either remains (impossible because evenness is known) or disappears (impossible because it is real). The author concedes that an impossibility arises but denies that it invalidates the implication; the negation of the aggregate (evenness with non-oddness) does not harm the logical connection.

The third objection centers on the opposition between the two aggregates (evenness with non-oddness vs. oddness with non-evenness) and argues that the negation of one aggregate contradicts the necessary implication. The author's full response is cut off but likely continues the defense.

The author's style is dialectical: he states each objection (naqd) and then provides the answer (jawab). This is a characteristic of Islamic logical commentaries that engage with and refute skeptical challenges. The discussion uses the example of a number being either even or odd, which is a familiar logical example in the tradition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first objection in the source, why is the disjunctive syllogism 'This number is either even or odd; it is even; therefore it is not odd' claimed to be invalid?
A) Because even numbers can also be odd.
B) Because the disjunction 'either even or odd' no longer holds after affirming 'it is even'.
C) Because the conclusion is not contradictory to the premise.
D) Because the number could be both even and odd.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a disjunctive syllogism relies on the persistent logical relation of mutual exclusion between the disjuncts, not on the ongoing possibility of both disjuncts being considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a shubha against the following disjunctive syllogism from fiqh: 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram. It is not wajib, therefore it is haram.' Then write the rebuttal using the author's method of emphasizing the persistent opposition relation.
?
In the passage, the first objection claims the disjunction does not remain after affirmation. Explain in your own words why the author says it does remain, using the concept of 'in'ad' (opposition).
